THE RED PAGE.
Hush Girls.
[For The Bulletin.]
I.—Rabbiting.
PI, if you don’t come at once, you nasty
pig ! I won’t go rabbiting at all.”
“ Grub ! wait till I’ve finished this
page.”
“Not an instant! Come on. I’ve got the axe
and lunch ; you bring the shovel and bag. I sup-
pose the dogs have gone now ! Oh, no, here they
are. Oh, there’s the hateful little puppy coming.
Moth-er! Keep the puppy ! Put him in a
match-box, or tie him up with a bit of cotton.”
Away we go, across the springy, tufted grass,
over the paddocks, and through fences, until we
come to a network of shallow burrows, which are
as yet untried ground to us.
“ Let’s have something to eat; never mind the
rabbits ; ” —says' Pi, sinking under an emu-bush.
“You ’re a cat! there ! Look ! Pluto’s got a
burrow ; come on ! ”
Pluto is plodding away at a burrow with his
great heavy paws. Novice comes up, pushes
Pluto gently aside, takes a long breath, and then
runs sniffing along the top of the ground to a
certain point, gives a delicate scratch, and gives
us a look which means, “ Here’s the rabbits ;
just here. Don’t take any notice of a dog like
Pluto !”
“ Give me the shovel, Pi! Oh, I feel a rabbit!
—look out! —catch it ! —its' head’s this way—get
it by the ears ! Oh, there’s another !—look out !
it’s gone !—it ’ll get away ! Put your hand in the
burrow to stop any more getting out. Oh, Novice
has got it! Oh, Pi, have you got another? Look
out! here’s another—and there’s another ! What
an angel Novice is ; we’ve got five already with-
out any trouble.”
“Pluto’s got another burrow ! Oh, there, he’s
let the rabbit out'! Ah, you clumsy animal,
Pluto! There! it’s running out on the plain—
after it, Novice ! —sool it ! sool it! Pi, fly like
the wind to the burrow, and stop it from getting
back ; I ’ll try and knock it down here with a
stick! Look out!—it’ll be in the burrow—oh,
there you are, stupid ! Never mind ; we ’ll dig it
out. Oh, I say, look !—only a short burrow.
Oh—eh—ah ! —it’s out ! Sool it, Novice ! —sool
it, boys ! There, it’s in a log !—what a big one,
too ! Run and get the axe, Pi—under that tree ;
what a good thing we brought it ! ”
The log is a heavy solid box, not long felled,
and consequently half green ; still we attack it
bravely, chopping hole after hole, until we breathe
in gasps, and our hands burn and bleed with the
friction of the axe.
At last we come to the rabbit. Pi drags it out
by the tail, and hits it on the head with force
enough to kill a kangaroo, saying “I ’ll teach you
not to let us catch you ! You ’ll know better next
time! ”
For three hours we catch rabbits ; —digging
them out of burrows, chasing them on to the
plain, until they give in and squeal for mercy ;
logging them and chopping them out, and smok-
ing them out of hollow trees. Heaps of rabbits
lie everywhere.
And now the exciting part is over, and we have
to skin them. We collect them under a shady
box ; I pull out father’s pocket-knife with a
guilty feeling ; Pi produces a rusty butcher’s-
knife, and operations commence. There could be
no more sickening business in the world, but we
draw long breaths and struggle on.
“I’ll race you.”
“Right!”
Pi struggles with her fifth; I with my twentieth.
I have got thirty when Pi holds up a little baby-
rabbit, skinned to the tip of the nose, saying,
“ Oh, you beauty ! run away now; go on ! ”
And somehow after that Pi falls to playing
“stick-knife” by herself, and I struggle through
alone.
We count the skins—fifty-two. “And there
would have been fifty-three if that one had n’t
disappeared;—l wonder where it went,” says Pi,
regretfully.
The skins are put in a bag ; the oarcases thrown
in a heap; and then we make a bee-line for the
nearest tank, where we wash and scrub our hands
until the skin nearly comes off.
Then, to Pi’s great delight, I produce the lunch-
bag. We lie flat on the ground, under the shade
of murmuring belars, with the dogs panting
peacefully around us, and eat with great appetite
slices of mother’s cake, which is only bread
sweetened, with currants * shot ” into it.
“ ‘The curse of gold is on the land,’ ” says Pi.
No answer.
“You know, Berrie, it’s very cruel! What
would other girls think of us ? It made my heart
ache when that poor little baby-rabbit squealed so.
I wanted to let it go.”
I swallow painfully before I answer: “That
is all very pretty ; but, you know, in the first place
we are helping to rid the country of a pest. In
the second, you want to play ; I want to write.
You can’t play without music, and I can’t write
on bark; so we must have money, and what
matter eveu though we have to swim through rab-
bits’ blood to get it? And,” I add dryly, “I
notice your scruples don’t trouble you much when
you are singing ‘ Life’s Dream is o’er ’ with Alec.”
We collect our materials, and start homeward.
“ Berrie ! Berrie ! there’s Alec at the cross-
track ; if he sees us like this ! ”
“My goodness ! We ’ll have to run.”
We force our tired limbs into a quick trot; the
axe becomes a dead-vfeight upon my shoulder ; I
can scarcely move.
Alec is coming at a good canter ; suddenly he
pulls into a walk. We breathe more freely ; dash
through the garden into our bedroom ; pull off our
“ French ” boots ; and plunge our faces into fresh
water. Long before we are half-di'essed Alec is iff
the sitting-room, and he shouts out, “How are
you ? ”
“ Oh, all right! We ’re changing our French
boots.”
“So help me cat! do you think I don’t know
that? I saw you runnin’, and I thought how
splendid I could cut you off ; and then I thought,
‘ Oh, dash me rags ! I ’ll let them get home and
change their togs. ’ I brought your mail; I see by
the paper rabbit-skins have gone down a penny.
Blake’s ‘ Grand March ’ for you, Pi. All your
MS. returned, Berrie ! ”
I laugh to hide my sickening sense of disap-
pointment.
“ You ought, to give up spend in’ your money on
paper to write stories ; what’s the good ? ”
“Music’s better, isn’t it, Alec?” says Pi,
eagerly.
“ Rats ! pending over to plaguey Sydney for
music! Some of these days a microbe 'will come
sailing over on a piece of music as happy as Larry,
and then where’ll you be? How many rabbit-
skins have you got now? I’m going to weigh
them Crikey! Seventeen pounds!—that’s
good.”
Iso, you must be making a mistake,” cry we,
m a breath.
“ Come and see ! ”
We rush out, fastening our belts. There, sure
enough, is the pile of rabbit-skins on the scale, and
the weight at 17. We give cries of delight, and
piles of fresh white paper and stamps rush before
m y eyes. Pi and Alec wander down the garden,
and forget all things in the delight of each other’s
society; I sit on the floor, and already feel half-a-
sovereign slipping through my fingers. I lift the
rabbit-skins. A horrible suspicion comes over
me. I tear them apart. The wretch ! he has
concealed two bricks inside them !
4 .
ll.— Robbing Bees.
I’VE found them at last! ” cries Pi, the
lazy, the philosophic ; fairly bursting into
the room.
“ The horses ? Well, I think I’ve found
horses before ; but I never thought it worth
slating over.”
I got the horses down in the lignum; you’d
have never found them. But I ’ve found a bee-
hive ! ”
“Oh ! I say, Pi! ”
“Probably eighty pounds of honey in it, and
the bees as well. Where’s the paper ? Best
honey, 7d. Oh, glorious ! No more skinning
rabbits for me ! Berrie ! put that scribbling
away. Poll’s in the yard ; harness her ! ”
“ Poll’s got a sore shoulder.”
“ Oh, fix a bag under the collar; we’ll have to
take her, ’cause she won’t mind the bees stinging.”
“ Oh, all right. You get the buckets, a box, and
sheet; —oh, and hunt up some gloves and veils.
We mustn't forget the ‘smoker’ and lots of
paper.”
I harness Poll, and drive with a flourish to the
front gate. Pi comes out, throws in the axe and
whetstone; snatches the whip and reins, and off
we go full gallop—the usual pace when Pi drives.
Across the plain ; through the timber ; dashing
under boughs, narrowly escaping rabbit-burrows,
—until we come to a great solid box-tree, standing
alone.
“Four hours’ work, Pi! Never mind; we
must go steady. Give me the axe ! ”
Chip — Chop — Chip—Chop. Great sappy chips
go flying round. We take it in turns for four
hours. The only intermission is when a bee
fiercer than the rest buzzes after us. Then we
throw down the axe and dart away, fighting with
it. Cho-op — chop — chip — chip.
“ How white you look, Pi! It ’ll be down in a
minute; cheer up ! . , . Oh, bother this bee !
Look out ! Stand clear ; there, it’s falling ! Just
look at the honey. Oh, the lovely comb ! Pi, Pi,
a hundred pounds ! Don’t rush ! Get the smoker:
light up! ”
“Oh, there’s millions of pounds!” we scream
together. We no longer feel fatigue, but chop
fresh holes, wildly pulling at the honey, uncon-
scious of occasional stings. Looking up, I see
Alec sitting immovable on his horse, chewing his
switch, and regarding us calmly.
“Yes,” he says, slowly, “I’ve been goin’ to show
you my bee-hive for a long time. But I never
thought of you bean’ so kind as to chop it down for
me. Crikey ! you’re clever girls ! To get through
such a wopper. I ’ll see if I can’t spare you a
couple of bottles of the honey. I hope you have n’t
messed up the queen; I particularly want the
hive.”
“ Alec, if I had a gun I’d shoot you ! ” says Pi,
solemnly.
“Pig ! ” I mutter.
“ Oh, dash me rags, girls ! what’s the good of
talkin’ like that ? It’s my beehive ; I found it
three months ago, when I was out lookin’ for emu-
eggs. It must have been hard work cuttin’ it
down; but it’s no good whippin’ the cat over it
now.”
He leans forward to let his horse crop the
grass ; his back is half towards us.
Pi looks at me with lightning eyes, points at
Alec, and then to the ground, where, half-dead
and covered with honey, is the queen. Quickly
and deftly she gets it on a stick, and puts it on
Alec’s back. We breathe hard, while In •■ continues,
unconscious of swiftly-coming danger. “One of
our calves threw a seven yesterday ; and the black
kitten had a rollin’, squirmin’ fit. You can have
it now, Pi ; I don’t want it any more. Shoo !
bee ! Well Bother the bees ! What are
they cornin’ round me like this for ? Anyway
Oh, crickey ! I ’ll have to get. Just look at the
bees ! Shoo ! shoo ! Cantankerous animals ! ”
He sets off full gallop, wildly waving his hat,
with the bees swarming round him.
Pi and I give a screech of delight, scramble into
the buggy, and drive for our lives.
L
lll. —Capitalists.
“ so-long, girls ! ” cries Alec, put-
Wm/ ting his foot in the stirrup, and per-
▼ T forming a kind of jig round his
horse, who is restless. He succeeds
in throwing his leg over, and is just off, when he
pulls up short, saying,
“Oh, so help me cat '! I nearly forgot to tell
you ! There’s a man coming round buyin’ up
bones, horns, horse-hair, hoofs, ‘ dead ’ wool,
rabbit-skins, and goodness knows what. So there’s
a chance for you ! Stack the coins on their ends
So-long ! ”
“Berrie ! Berrie ! methinks I see palmy days
stretching before us. Thank Heaven for this
year of drought ! Just think, Berrie ! there must
be 40 sheep down in the five-comers.”
“Oh, and a lot in the creek !”
“ And Spotty, and Tiny, and Redcow, and
Darby in the Ring-bark. Just think of their
hoofs and horns ! Oh, Spotty’s splendid horns!”
“Yes, and Ben and Darkey and Lofty out on
the plain ! ”
“Oh, yes ; Ben’s tail hadn’t been trimmed for
ages before he died. What a pity horses have n’t
got horns, too ! Come on ! Let’s get our hats,
and some bran-bagS, and start at once ! ”
Filled with wild delight, we race across the
paddocks. All the afternoon we work. With a
hammer and chisel we force off horns and hoofs;
we shear off the horsehair with a pair of shears ;
and pick wool until our backs ache. And then,
just at sundown, we return home laden with
spoil, which we cast in a heap in one corner of the
garden.
Day by day the heap grows higher; we scour
everyone’s paddocks. Anything with hoofs and
horns becomes of vital interest. We wait in
silent expectation.
“Pi, I say! I see a dust!” I shout, one
evening after sundown.
Pi rushes on to the verandah.
“ He comes ! He comes ! Oh, my poor heart !
He’s through the gate; will he turn in here?
Yes ! The awful suspense is over.”
“Isn’t he nice-looking, Pi ?”
“Oh, Grub! Lanky! Skinny! Berrie! I
know: he’s the man who hid behind the crow-
bar ! ”
He stands before us and grins a long, inane
grin ; tries to speak, and ends in a long gulping
sound ; tries again, and says, in a voice which
squeaks like a file when applied to wire: “How
are yer ? ”
“All right, thank you !”
“ Hot to-day.”
“Yes.”
“ I’m gom’ round all the stations buyin’ bones,
skins, and all kinds er things. Has yer father
any ? ”
“No, but we have ! ” bursts out Pi.
“ Yas ? Wait till I git me scales.”
“ Thirty-six pounds er wool. Well, I’ll give
yer a penny-ha’penny a pound.”
“That is below current price.”
“ Oh, yas; but ain’t I got to cart it to Hay?”
“ You ought to be able to give tuppence.”
“All right, then; but I’ll lose on it. How
about the rest ? I’ll give yer half-a-crown for the
whole lot as they are.”
“ Half-a-crown ! Why, just look at these
horns ! —there was not another cow in the country
had h®rns like these. Look at all this horse-hair !
Half-a-crown ! ”
“ Three shillin’, then.”
“No ; give us four, will you ? ”
“ Here you are, then.”
He puts his hand deep into his trousers-pocket;
there is a delicious tinkle of money ; and he pulls
out four half-crowns. Pi and I restrain ourselves
from snatching them ; take them indifferently ;
but shut our hands over them with feverish eager-
ness.
“ You can depend on me once a fortnight,” he
says; shakes hands ; and goes on.
; “ Once a fortnight ! These riches once a fort-
night ! Don’t move, Berrie ! don’t breathe,! don’t
■speak ! for fear it should prove a dream ! ”
4
IV. —Ruination.
PI and I come through the garden gate with
dragging footsteps, after a stiff morning’s
work up the paddock. We have just
finished a discussion on our financial posi-
tion. Everything is at the lowest ebb.
Pi’s eye rests gloomily on a heap of horns, hoofs,
bones, etc., in a corner of the garden. She turns
her head wearily aside.
_ “ He said we could depend on him once a fort-
night ; and here it is two months! Oh, Berrie !
it must be true that he’s ‘ gone bung ’ 1 ”
She looks round quickly as we step on to the
verandah, and see Alec’s hat on the floor.
“Think you’re very smart; don’t you!” she
says, savagely, throwing a stone at a bulge in the
hammock. A frowsy black head appears over the
side, and a voice says—
“ Hullo ! Been graftin’ ? ”
“Bring me some !” I shout, as Pi disappears
towards the kitchen,—knowing by long experi-
ence that she is going for cake.
“Have the hammock?” says Alec, ironically,
as I squat down on the ground and lean back
against a pepper-tree.
Pi comes stamping out; throws herself down,
and begins chopping at the ground with a toma-
hawk. Her eyes are starting from her head with
rage.
“Berrie ! what do you think mother’sdone?”
“What?”
“ First, there was a minister here this morning.”
“A minister!”
“Yes, a smooth-faced, sneaking, crawling
minister! He, stayed two hours ; had some tea ;
and, when he was going, he asked mother for
some money for their church debt, and mother
had none, and then he asked if there was any-
thing she could give towards a—a—swindling
bazaar; and mother gave him our jar of honey
that we had to sell! ”
“ We ’ll never have anything more to do with
mother! ”
“A sneaking, crawling minister! If it had
been anyone else! And if she wanted to be
generous, she might have sold it to him cheap—
but to give—it—away ! ”
Pi’s voice breaks, and she turns her head aside.
Various sounds are coming from the hammock.
It rolls and sways back and forth ; at last it comes
down with a flop, and Alec lies kicking, squirm-
ing, slapping his legs, and gasping with laughter.
“ Holy Frost! Ha ! Hah ! Oh, it’s rich ! To
think you stole that honey from me, and now
after all, you’ve lost it. Oh, he! he! Ah, ha!
ha ! Great Caesar ! ” «
“Pig!”
“Good Heavens!” says Pi, in a despairing
whisper, and holds up to my view Alec’s cherished
whip cut in half. She has done it with the
“tommy.”
“ What on earth are we to do now ? ” Pi
rises heavily, and goes inside. My heart turns
coward when I see her returning with the money-
box.
“ Alec, I’ve cut your whip in half; here’s half-
a-crown for another. ”
Alec picks up his whip, regretfully. He turns
red as he sees Pi’s outstretched hand, and says :
“You’re only doing that for show : anyway, half-
a-crown ’s no good ; this whip cost five shillings.”
“ This is all I’ve got; will you take the - bones
and horns as security for the rest ? ”
“ Pi,” I say, indignantly, “ are n’t we partners ?
Give Alec my half-crown as well. And for the
future, Alec, don’t leave your piggish whips
about! Come on, Pi, and let’s think how we can
make some more money.”
N.S.W.
M.H.
The South African War,
A MARVELLOUS thing has happened.
Men have again been permitted to see a
pillar of cloud by day and of fire by night.
God has made a wind to pass over the
face of the earth and the waters of overwhelming
might have been assuaged.
Two years ago the herdsmen of the little
republic of South Africa drove their cattle across
the veldts of the Transvaal and of the Orange
Free State in peaceful and contented obscurity.
But buried beneath their rugged hills was the
wealth of gold and diamonds, and they stood in the
way and obstructed the progress of that kind of
civilisation which is represented by such adven-
turers as Barney Barnato and Cecil Rhodes, and
is sung by such poets as Rudyard Kipling. Two
years ago the name of England would have
stood against the world. Her armed vessels
steamed along every coast and in every sea.
Canada, Jamaica, ancient Egypt, farther Ind,
and the islands of the South Sea paid her tribute.
Like Persia in the time of Xerxes, Rome in the'
days of Caesar, and Spain under the rule of Charles
V., was England among the modern nations, and
more. For in these days the merchants and
bankers of London, Berlin, Amsterdam, and New
York are all akin, and the.iear of a fall in the
price of corn destroys manhood, stifles the call of
conscience, and makes allies everywhere. The
lawyer in his office in Washington construes the
requirements of international law, and ascertains
what are neutral goods in such a way. as not to
depress the price Of stocks.
In an evil day England, inspired by the lust of
conquest and of gain, girded up her loins, and,
with the banners of St. George floating in the air
and fifes shrieking the notes of “God Save the
Queen,” with the wealth of the world supporting
her, calling upon the Krupps of Germany to supply
her with guns, and gathering horses—oh, the shame
of it!—in the prairies of Illinois, started to drive
the herdsmen of South Africa from their frugal
homes, to burn their houses and their barns,
and to annex the Transvaal to her dominions.
To this unholy and dastardly work she devoted
245,000 men, the largest and best appointed
army raised in the whole course of her history,
and at their heads rode her military chieftains,
my Lord Roberts of Candahar and my Lord
Kitchener of Khartoum. The selfish Courts of
the Continent watched to see with what kind of
spirit the hunted Boers would perish on their
pyres, with as little concern as the people of
Constance viewed the death of Huss, as the
people of Geneva waited for the death of Servetus.
The great republic over the seas founded by
George Washington and maintained by Abraham
Lincoln, toward which alone the harried burghers
might turn with hope, gave them no whisper of
encouragement, and was only eager that no con-
sul too zealous about his opened correspondence,
no questions of the seizure of flour, or of the
limitation of neutral rights, should arise to dis-
turb the success of the English foray. There was
still money to be made by deals with Thread-
needle-street and these republican burghers were
few in number, poor- in substance, and far away.
Let them die.
Two years have rolled along in their course,
and what thing has come of it? The bones of
forty thousand Englishmen, one for every Boer in
the field, lie scattered along the paths where the
herdsmen drove their cattle. Twenty thousand
Englishmen have been captured. A billion of
dollars has been spent in vain. The output of
the coveted Rand has fallen from seventy millions
to seven millions a year, and the imposing forms
of Cecil Rhodes and Dr. Jameson have sunk into
darkness. The unconquered and unconquerable
Boers still ride over the desolate and wasted
veldts of the Transvaal, and “unfortunate occur-
rences” are still reported in the coffee-houses of
London from the very heart of Cape Colony.
Who is now so poor as to do England reverence?
Nothing can ever give a gloss to attempted wrong
but success. The power of the Empire has been
halted. The very walls of Windsor Castle have
been shaken. The German works his rude will in
China. Russia pushes sternly towards the Pacific.
France threatens in Barbary with impunity.
America will open her canal between the oceans,
and which of them stops to heed whether it serves
the purpose of England or not ?
Striving to know what this struggle means to
the Boers, it is not for me to do more than bid
them cheer, but with all my soul I pray they may
be able to maintain the liberty which is theirs.
If this is not vouchsafed to them, then may they
uphold their manhood to the end. It is well for
us all and for the future of the world that there
still exists a race with pluck enough to resist
sordid crime, whatever be its might, regardless
of consequences. The light which inspired
the Greeks, the Dutch at Leyden, and Anthony
Wayne at Stony Point, is now streaming in
full flood over the barren peaks of the Trans-
vaal. The uncouth names of Majuba Hill
and Spion Kop begin to resound in our ears
like those of Marathon and Thermopylae
of old, like those of Trenton and Brandy-
wine in later days. Kruger and Botha and De
Wet have taken their places among the heroes of
all time and the whole world, beside Leonidas,
Winkelried, William of Orange, and Washington,
to incite the brave to effort for all the ages yet to
come. When the English nation, old and tooth-
less, like the giant in “ The Pilgrim’s Progress,”
shall sit by the wayside snarling over the memory
of its victories won from the weak in Ireland and
India, at Wyoming and at St. Helena, with every
traveller ready to knock it on the head for its
past wickedness, mothers will tell their children,
poets will sing the story, and historians will write
in their pages how the burghers fought and died
upon the kopjes of South Africa to save their
homes from the despoiler.—S. W. Pennypacker,
in N. Y. Sun.

[For Thu Bulletin.]
By the Month of the Shaft.
The red light from the lanterns
Lit the faces round the shaft,
Faces swarthy, stem, and tanned,
As they cut the fallen timber,
Cleared the rotten windlass aft,
Rigged another roughly planned.
One man fancied, leaning over,
Fastening on the jagged rope,
A faint movement in the brown
Foul ferment of the darkness;
Then Black Bill, the leader, spoke :
“ He’s alive, boys ! Who’ll go down ? ”
Pushing through the crowd that started,
Sam the Ski ter forward sprung,—
Caught the rope with steady hand ;
“ Lower down ! ” he cried ; “ I’m ready ! ”
Round the creaking windlass swung,
Strained the rope in every strand.
Half-way down the foul air met him,
And the candle flickered out
And the vapour seized his brain,
Clutched his throat with demon fingers,
But he raised a reeling shout
And they hauled him up again.
“It’s the gas, mates ! Where’s the tommy ?
Cut those smoke bushes here—
Beat the air in ! Haul ’em round !
Now let down a lighted candle :
Steady, boy ! the air is clear ”
And they wound him under ground,
Through the foul breath of the darkness ;
And the grinding windlass turned
Till the vapour hung like smoke,
And the slinking shadows cowered
Where the candle dimly burned,
Flickered on the straining rope ;
Lit the damp and slimy shingles,
And the rotten beams abaft ;
The lad felt a sudden chill, —
He could hear the water dripping
From the mullock up the shaft
To the bottom black and still.
Down below him, doubled over
’Neath a fallen cedar stump,
Something red and battered lay,
Where the coil of stagnant water,
Like a crawling viper slunk
With its dazed and broken prey.
* * * •
As they stood in silence round him,
One dark-bearded digger spoke :
“ Give him air ! he’s coming to ! ”
But the Skiter, leaning over,
Shook his head : “Boys, there’s no hope ;
See, the lips are going blue ! ”
And he raised him like a woman :
“ Ain’t he altered, as he lies
There so quiet-like and strange—
A damned lump of mullock struck me,
Boys, the sand’s got in my eyes—
God ! old fellow, what a change !
“ And the girl at Kelly’s shanty—
We must break the blow to her;
They had fixed it up last week ;
And he meant to chuck prospectin’—
’Twas his last day at the Spur—
And then leave for Golden Creek.
“Hurt her; bless you ! it won’t hurt her.
She’d have chucked him for a kiss
From that measle of a toff,
Who some say—but nothing matters :
Poor old chap, he’s come to this ! . . .
Boys, the rain is keeping off,
“ But the wind’s from a bad quarter . . .
Yes, a brother, so I’ve heard,
But he hasn’t wrote for years;
Send the yellow dust to Liza —
It was all she ever cared,
And she’ll soon pan off her tears ! ”
And they laid him on a stretcher,
With the night-wet on his hair
And the blood-wet on his breast:
Harder weight than head of woman
That had leaned across him there
Where the beam had pinned his chest.
O’er the blue line of the ranges
Rose the dawning faint and chill,
And the moon, a shadow dim,
Like a sorrow-stricken woman
Leaned her pale brow on the hill,
As the New Day broke for him.
M.L.

of the Supreme Court. “It is impossible,” said
Mr. Bruce Smith, when seen in reference to this
matter, “for the representatives of the different
States to give up the whole year to Parliamen-
tary work, to the complete loss of their means
of livelihood; and the country has the Govern-,
mtnt of the Commonwealth to thank for the
boneless muddle into which the business of
Parliament has been thrown.” Mr. Bruce Smith
will arrange a pair during his absence.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 23, 1901.
Butler, Buller, and Bull.
The drumming of General Buller out of his
army 'cum maud in response to popular
clamor is another indication of the mental
and moral deterioration of John Bull.
There have been many such indications since
the outbreak of the Boer war. The old ideal
of the English character was solid, almost
stolid, strength ; rugged, almost stupid,
determination ; calm impassiveness scorning
hysteria ; courageous pugnacity, anxious
always for a fight, preferably with a superior
force ; limitless capacity for receiving pun-
ishment without squealing. That, in the
year 1889, was the traditional English char-
acter ; it might have been a slightly-damaged
tradition since the days of Drake or of
Henry V., but it was still in good enough
repair to serve for common use and to find
eager acceptance in most English minds;
and a race tradition is always intended for
home consumption —it is an article which
can rarely be exported.
* * * *
The Tradition was founded on a long,
strenuous fighting career. From the time
when the stolid Anglo-Saxon had absorbed
the Norman, and set up in business as a
romantic pirate and a picturesque highway-
man, he stole with a success and a daring the
thought of which makes the blood of every
Englishman thrill with pride. France,
Spain, Germany, Scotland, Ireland were all
made the victims of predatory exploits on
land which claim the name of glorious,
whilst the successful piracy on sea of Drake
and Frobisher, and a score of others, was even
more notable in its results and exhilarating
in its incidents. Emerging from those
days, passing through the less glittering
periods of the later Stuarts and the Dutch
William, England plunged into the
Napoleonic wars, to emerge with a
reputation still the first in Europe.
She had proved not only her ability to steal
with audacity, but also her disposition—
and her power—to prevent anyone else from
stealing on anything like the same scale.
Indubitably she had the right to play lead in
the European combination. Her neighbors
had during centuries been playing with her
the game of nations, that vast tragedy of
mutual slaughter, and had been beaten on
almost every point. The Holy Roman
Empire, France, Spain, had in turn fallen
from the place of command ; and the stolid,
impassive, coldly courageous Englishman,
relying on the blessing of Providence and
the strength of a good right arm, was
emphatically on top. At this time, the
English Tradition was at its zenith. “ These
bull-dog English : they do not know when
they are beaten ” was said to have been
Napoleon’s verdict on his destroyers.
Probably he never used the words, but they
give a correct estimate of what the English-
man thought of himself then—a valuation
which was grudgingly but still generally ac-
cepted in Europe—and the witnesses to
which were his liens on the best parts of the
civilised globe.
* • • •
Through later years, the Tradition began
to lose a little of its strength. England took
to Exeter Hall and Freetrade, and lost
honesty as a robber, and vigor as a pirate.
But. there was still plenty of vigor in the
Tradition; and the events of the Crimean war,
the Charge of the Six Hundred, the battles
of Alma and Inkerman, gave it a fresh
stimulus. But now at the end of the Boer
war—and it seems that the war must end
shortly on terms favorable to the Boers —
what remains of the Tradition ?
* * * •
The tales of Agincourt and Crecy, and of
Richard Grenville’s gallant fight, when
Englishmen met appalling odds with cheery
hearts, have been swept from the memory of
Europe by the details of the unavailing fight
of the 200,000 against the 50,000. England
has figured like the Persians at Marathon
and Thermopylae, as a vast, enfeebled host
kept at bay by a few strong men. Her
hardy, aeath-or-glory veterans have been
seen with their pianos, and ice-chests, and
hair-mattresses, and wine-cellars, battling in
vain against a little mob of peasants. The
part the Spaniards played in Holland, and the
Austrians in the Tyrol, has fallen to her lot
this time. The Tradition had been created
and kept up by little bands of Englishmen
holding out against unnumbered odds in all
corners of the earth. Now, what man, even
on a music-hall stage, would venture to tell
that an Englishman is as good at fighting as
five of any other race ?
* • • «
But, after all, that part of the Tradition
was not absolutely vital. A nation’s generals
may blunder and a nation’s armies lose
battles : so long as the nation’s character
remains firm, fixed, courageous, all disasters
can be retrieved, all losses repaired. But
the events of the Boer war have made more
seriously disquieting revelations with regard
to English character than with regard to
English fighting power. The spirit of the
robust robber, wh’ose firm conviction that
the Almighty gave him power to rob, and
would in Person see that his robberies
were successful, has faded away, and its
place taken by weak vain-gloriousness, by
screeching hysteria, by contemptible lia-
bility to panic, by mean searching-out of
scapegoats to excuse national failings. The
nation that once looked unmoved on the
Spanish Armada and stood out to sea, stern,
resolute, cheerful, for its destruction, has
had all the moods of a vaporish woman, in a
campaign against a tiny body of South
African farmers—a surety of victory in-
tolerant of any good advice, a shuddersome
panic, a desperate flurry of effort, a weak
spitefulness, a savage searching for some-
body who can be crushed with impunity. It
is not England, not the England of old,
going out now to fight—this draggle-tailed
harridan, shrieking out threats, enforcing
her courage with drams, cuffing her brats
because she cannot reach to the enemy.
* * * *
The first marked indication of the national
decadence was revealed when John Bull
dismissed from the S’African command with
contumely and scorn General Butler. That
faithful servant of the British Crown knew
Naboth, and was averse to stealing his
vineyard. But if the theft was resolved
upon—well, he wished to warn his master
that Naboth kept a dog, and the fence was
spiked, and there was a loaded gun in
Naboth’s best room. John Bull raged and
fumed, and dismissed Butler as an ignorant
and impudent person. He knew better : it
was only necessary to vault over the fence,
have a pleasant grape breakfast, and enter
into possession. Now, the Nation that
cannot bear to learn the truth, and can listen
only to the smooth tongue of the flatterer, is
in a bad way. From that on, developments
in illustration of English national decadence
crowded on one another’s heels—the sense-
less, extravagant panic when Naboth pro-
duced his gun, the wild alarums for rein-
forcements, the savage criticisms of men
who were being beaten only because
Butler’s advice had not been taken, the
hysterical talk of the great Boer war, the
inglorious exultation over a few barren
victories, won with the aid of overwhelming
numbers, the drunken Mafeking carousals
(which gave the stamp of supreme approval to
a disgraceful and absurd incident of amateur
theatricalism) all told the one tale of a
nation no longer stolid, firm, resolute,
courageous, of a people degenerating into a
mob.
• • • •
The dismissal of General Buller seems to
roll the last stone on the grave of British
character. Buller fought like an old Eng-
lishman, doggedly, courageously, perhaps a
little stupidly. At Tugela he failed—mainly
because Butler’s advice had been scorn-
fully rejected and provision made for a pic-
nic campaign, not a real war. Failing, he
recognised that, in all human probability,
his failure involved the fall of Ladysmith.
If he had been an hysterical old woman
(sharing the character thus of the British
public), he might have heliographed to
White to “Fight on! fight on!” Being
a practical soldier, and not a posturer,
he knew that it was not a question
of fight, but of feed, and, being a
chivalrous gentleman, he took on himself the
responsibility of the fall of the town, as he
had lost the battle, and sent a message
accordingly to the beleaguered captain,
not saying “surrender,” but giving the
exact position of affairs and instructions how
to make a surrender least disastrous if it
became inevitable. For this, being now
informed of the facts for the first time, the
British Public (which, by the way, knows
nothing of the feeling of taking on itself
responsibility, but is to the last degree
anxious to shift to other shoulders the bur-
den created by its greed and folly and blind
following of false leaders) sentences Buller
to degradation. The shrieking harridan
cuffs her kids.
* * • •
It may be that the symptoms of national
rottenness which have been disclosed by this
war are evidence of a passing virulent
disease rather than of a hopeless infirmity.
The Bulletin, yielding to none in admira-
tion of the achievements of the British
race, hopes that so it will prove. English-
men have done so much for human Liberty
and human Progress that it would be a
sorrow indeed to watch at the bedside of
their once virile race, expiring in womanish
hysteria. Purged of her present politicians
and her mischievous, sensational press,
enabled by a sounder economic system to
feed her own people and till her own lands,
England may enjoy future days of greatness,
though at present she seems to be reeling
quickly down the path to ruin ; and already
the epitaph is being cut —
England, that was wont to conquer others,
Hath made a shameful conquest of herself.
Tie Inky Way.
On the P. E. Quinn proposal, in N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, that every newspaper article be signed with
the name of the writer thereof—who could con
scientiously sign the average daily-paper leade
on an important subject ? This is how it is made
Editor Robinson meets, by appointment, leaderr
writers Jones, Brown, Smith and Tompkins. The.
position is discussed from every point of view.-
Perhaps the business-manager is consulted, or the
proprietor, or the chief director. Finally, from
the mingling of all opinions, the lines which the
article will take are set down, and Jones writes
it. Then Editor Robinson revises it, and leaves
something out and puts something in. Perhaps
Reader Montmorency, up in the compositors’
room, finds out a mistake somewhere (printers’
readers are well-nigh omniscient) and corrects it.
Thus the article goes to the public—the joint work
of Robinson, Jones, Brown, Smith, Tompkins,
Montmorency and Business-manager M'Dougall,
with also a good deal of the influence of dead-
-and-gone Editor Atkinson, whose precepts are
still remembered by his old friend, leader-writer
Smith. Now, how’s it going to be signed ?
» » * *
Another P. Quinn point : The most important
man on a paper is the editor, who Leaves Things
Out, and often suggests the writing of the things
which are to go in. The things a paper leaves out
furnish, perhaps, the most vital part of its policy.
Should they not be published in a daily supple-
ment and signed as having been left out by the
editor? If the desire is to get back to the real
source of newspaper power, and indicate it clearly
to the public, that must certainly be done. And
if the P. Quinn Bill is just the desire of some
semi-politicians semi-journalists to pose before
the public (which The Bulletin shrewdly sus-
pects), that object can be just as well served by
the “ pressmen ” who want that sort of puffing in-
sisting on their signatures being printed with
their articles.
* * * *
Remarkable statement from leading columns of
Sydney D.T. (discussing proposal that the P.
Quinns should be allowed to spread their tails at
the end of newspaper articles): “The court can
at any time order the name of the writer to be dis-
closed, so that he can also be made answerable.”
That statement cannot ha ve been a deliberate one
on the Daily Telegraph’s part. The journalistic
tradition is that any decent editor would sooner
go to gaol than give up the name of a writer.
In fact, the self-respecting . editor—like Mr.
Gullett—would submit to be fried first.
• * * *
A reporter’s axiom ; Mankind is divided into
two parts —those who want their names promin-
ently displayed in the paper, and those who want
theirs kept out.
* * * •
The directors of “ United Australia” magazine
are Messrs. Barton, Bruce Smith, Edward Brad-
don, and R. E. O’Connor. Originally purely a
Federalist magazine, it is now a Freetrade and
pro-Kanaka organ, and in its last issue charges
Barton with “a breach of political faith” in
bringing down his tariff. Mr. Bruce Smith is
understood to be editor of the paper, which ex-
plains its politics. But what is there to explain
Mr. Barton and Mr. O’Connor continuing on its
directorate ?
■ • * *
One of the almost countless worries of the sub-
editor of a paper is the placing of the columns.
Leave the comps, to themselves, and they will
secure bewildering results. A Sydney daily
which runs a once published side by
side with a disquisition by Cardinal Moran on the
celibacy of the clergy a highly technical discus-
sion on some matters in relation to stud dogs and
their mates. The dog matter was awful enough
in itself. Its position made it simply calamitous.
A little Welsh country paper must have dealt a
sore blow to Chamberlain’s pride when it solemnly
published this paragraph :
The Right Hon. Joseph Chamberlain, the man who is
responsible for the war, passed through Bala Junction on
Monday, on his way home from Dolgelly, where he nas
been visiting. Our representative had a conversation
with him on the subject of the war. If our space permits
perhaps the conversation will be published in our next
number.
Australian newspapers could pick up a valuable
point as to the hostile treatment of an opponent
from this.
m * * *
“West Coaster” complains from Hokitika
(M.L.): “Have the Foreign-trade gang the con-
trol of the news supplied to this island ? Almost
every day we have: ‘Great Freetrade.meeting.
Tariff unanimously condemned ’ ; or this : ‘ The
D.T. says so and so’; ‘the S.M. Herald ditto
ditto’; ‘Mr. Reid says such and such.’ Hardly
ever do we get what Turner, Barton, or Kingston
say.” M.L. Press Association has its Australian
head-quarters in Sydney, where it has to rely on
the Freetrade dailies for news, because there are
no Protectionist daily newspapers. Probably,
however, it gives M.L. papers what they want on
the tariff question: they can’t be expected to
enthuse over duties which seriously affect M.L.
products.
The Care of the Aborigine.
Dr. Roth, Northern Protector of Aborigines, to
The Bulletin
I was much struck with thecommon senseof the humani-
tarian stand taken by Ernest Favenc (8., Oct. 12.) Since
my appointment as Administrator of the Aboriginals
Protection Act for N.Q., responsible for my actions only
to the Home Secretary, Mr. Foxton, I have had the
opportunity of bringing the matter of federating all
aboriginal interests under the notice, not only of my
chief, but also of our last Governor and the present
Governor-General. The difficulty of making the question
a Federal one is enhanced, however, by the fact that
three of the Federated States—Tasmania (with no abo-
riginals), Victoria and N.S.W. (with comparatively few) —
cannot be expected to make the pecuniary sacrifices
necessary for carrying the project into execution. So far
as N.Q. is concerned, Mr. Favenc’s strictures are appa-
rently based on incorrect information, in that there has
been no “ hysterical outburst ” —I have acted rather in too
grim earnest —in carrying out the laws regulating the
intercourse between whites and blacks. Parliament has
at present under consideration an Amending Aborigines’
Protection Bill, the provisions of which are, in a large
measure, based upon the abuses found to be existing.
Thanks to the Home Secretary, the regulations have, in
no sense, become “ practically a dead letter.”
• * * •
As to the principle of establishing reserves for the
blacks where they could live in their primitive state, the
details of a scheme have now arrived at such maturity
that its practical execution, in the hands of Mr. Foxton,
is almost completed. The natives are not to be forcibly'
removed from their homes, but large reserves are to be
proclaimed in those unoccupied areas of country (in the
Peninsula, and Gulf-coasts) where the blacks already are.
Where I emphatically differ from Mr. Favenc is with
regard to his views as to the necessity of the missionary
being as absolutely prohibited from these Reserves as the
rest of the' general public. Ido not speak from senti-
ment, but from experience. From the fact of the
Government subsidising our mission stations, and provid-
ing them with provisional school-teachers, it is one of my
duties to inspect these institutions and report upon their
administration and working. With one exception, in
regard to which the Home Secretary took drastic
measures, all these missions have done, and are doing,
good and useful work.
* * • •
Cocoa-nuts would grow well on certain of these re-
serves, where, indeed, they have been tried. On Cape
Bedford Reserve some 600 have been already planted,
with a view to copra-making in the future. With regard
to the native police, let Mr. Favenc and others be assured,
once and for all, that that regime is now a thing of the
past. One of the first acts which followed the appoint-
ment of the present Commissioner of Police was the
issue of distinct orders—which have been implicitly
obeyed—that the aborigines were to be treated
on humanitarian lines, with the result that not
a single “dispersal" has since then taken place.
Where the advance of white settlement has destroyed
the hunting-grounds of the aboriginals, the Government
has provided feeding-stations at various centres in the
Northern districts—the food-distribution being in the
hands of responsible and reliable people. Something
between £6O and £7O monthly is thus spent. In other
cases relief in the form of tobacco, etc., is granted for
conciliatory and other purposes. Medical assistance is
also being regularly given to the sick and diseased. The
recruiting and employment of natives in the pearl-shelling
and bSche-de-mer industries is now under proper
supervision. Heavy penalties await the purveyor
or supplier of opium and grog to our natives.
Indeed, the whole object of the Aborigines Protection
Act of ’97, with its Amending Bill now under considera-
tion, is to promote the welfare and ameliorate the condi-
tion of the natives, to end hitherto-existing abuses, and
to prevent their recurrence in the future. The aims of
this legislation are gradually, yet assuredly, being
crowned with success. Indeed, the other States might
well follow the example now set by Queensland in her
dealings with her autochthonous population.
Yours,
WALTER E. ROTH.
Northern Protector of Aboriginals (Q.).

The Peacock’s Draggled Tail.
As Vic. Premier-designate, Peacock, at the last
election, taking Turner’s programme, fought the
then office-holders — M‘Lean and co. —and was
sent back with a working majority, which he still
has. The only essential point of difference
between the two programmes was as to the
necessity for a convention to plan a new State
Constitution, which M‘Lean condemned and Pea-
cock advocated. A Bill representing the Peacock
platform was brought in and passed its second read-
ing, and then, apparently, the man with the laugh
found himself too weak to force it through the
Council. Anyhow, last week, ’mid the scoffing
jeers of the Opposition, and in the contemptuous
silence of his followers, he withdrew the Bill—
which a majority of the Assembly was still
pledged to support—and announced an alter-
native scheme of reform. This beats even
Rum-ism or LYNE-ism. The Convention scheme
was the one point on which the electors were
asked to give a decided answer. They gave it,
trusting to Peacock to carry out their wishes,
and, at the first light breath of opposition, he
betrays his trust. The electors demanded a con-
vention to give them, not a tardy and grudging
measure of reform, according to the sweet will of
the 48 Fat Landlords, but a new Constitution,
framed by the best men of the State, elected by
popular suffrage. Peacock offers them a so-called
Reform Bill, framed by himself, and liable to be
hacked and bashed by the mediocrities of the
Assembly, and the worse than mediocrities of the
Council. The M‘Lean Government invited the
electors to give the Assembly power to reform the
Constitution, and the people declined to trust the
Assembly. Now Peacock deliberately flouts the
electors. His proposals are, in some respects,
good enough—the reduction of Assembly to 80 and
Council to 40, single electorates for both Houses,
adult suffrage for the Assembly, ratepayers (male
and female) to be electors for Council (males only
eligible to sit), Council to have same money-bill
powers as Federal Senate, Ministers to be as
numerous as at present, plural voting to be en-
tirely abolished, and deadlocks to be provided for
by a double dissolution preparatory to a joint


sitting ; but the trouble is that, whilst enthusias-
tically accepting the bad, the House of Landlords
will almost certainly reject the good points. And,
even if Peacock were able to force such
a measure through both Houses, the reforms
are far less than wha t were expected _by
the majority who asked for the convention.
Had the electors been able to elect a convention,
that body would probably have provided for a
single House, with the Referendum as a check,
and the right of initiative. Now, after Mr.
Peacock’s shameful back-down, they will have to
put up with such trifling reforms as the House of
Fat is likely to give. It is a great, betrayal.
♦
J ourn alegoism.
Presumably the N.S.W. Newspaper Articles Bill
has been hurled into happy oblivion. Essentially
it was wind and nonsense. Journalists did not
want it; newspaper proprietors did not want it;
the public did not want it. True : the grand
doctrine of personal responsibility should be
brought home to every journalist. None should
pen, or be permitted to print, a line which
he would not back with his name and
character. But journalists do not wish, often
cannot afford, to sign their names to every-
thing they write : they wish to pick and
choose—to sign only the creditable matter, or
the matter which will not embroil them socially.
Newspaper proprietors do not wish matter signed
as a rule, because signature detracts from that
valuable asset—“ the credit of the paper.” And
the public would soon weary of the reiteration of
undistinguished names beneath undistinguished
matter. The present arrangement is thus a com-
promise between conflicting interests. The mask
of the editorial We would be disadvantageously
exchanged for the hard face of the advertising
politician, to whose inferior writing the
fool-public would transfer the credit or notoriety
gained in politics. And, finally, compulsory legis-
lation is impracticable ; because it could be evaded
by the easy method of employing an office cipher
to sign everything. Since, then, it is not possible
in every case to preserve the personal responsi-
bility of the writer, that responsibility should be
transferred to the editor, who should not be per-
mitted to plead good faith merely “as far as he
knows.”
♦
The Drunkard and the Theologian.
The Rev. Courtenay Smith, a gentleman whose
well-meaning, and, to an extent, successful, efforts
to deal with individual cases of drink-mania
deserve warm appreciation, advances a most
extraordinary argument against State effort to
deal with inebriates. In the columns of the
Australian Churchman Mr, Smith writes:
Of late years an idea, fostered by a section of the
medical profession, has become widespread that drunken-
ness is purely a disease. As a matter of fact, this is
setting the human mind against the mind of God. For
in Hoiy Writ it is most distinctly set forth that drunken-
ness is a sin—not a disease—and is to be cured only in the
same way as all other sin. lam old-fashioned enough to
take the Bible as I find it, and an experience of 23 years
with about 5000 inebriates has confirmed me in the
knowledge that the Christian way is the only way to
cope with this dreadful evil. . . . Have my temperance
friends and acquaintances considered that, in calmly
handing the whole affair over to the State, they are
positively sacrificing the vast majority of the unhappy
inebriates of New South Wales ? Yet, that is so.
These are the essential points in a long plea for
leaving the treatment of the inebriate to the
Christian theologian, rather than to the scientific
physician, and treating him as a sinner rather
than as a patient. It would not be possible to
imagine a more fantastically ludicrous idea.
Before declaring an opinion contrary to all the
conclusions come to from a scientific study of
ascertained facts, Mr. Courtenay Smith should
have asked himself these questions : If Christian
theology is a cure for, or a prophylactic against
the drink-habit, how is it that many profoundly
religious people are afflicted by drunken-
ness—clergymen of all denominations amongst
them ? If the Christian way is the only
way to cope with alcoholic excess, how is it
that Mohammedans, Buddhists, and many brands
of Pagans cope with it absolutely, successfully,
and with no help from any Christian methods ?
In short, how is it that, to all intents and pur-
poses, Christian peoples are the only drunken
peoples on earth, Mahommedans, Buddhists, Con-
fucians, and most kinds of Pagans (before the
advent of the Christian missionary) being ab-
stainers ? To advance theology as an alternative
to segregation and medical care in the treatment
of inebriety requires a bold contempt for all the
ascertained facts of the subject.

The Rofe Case.
The persistency of Thomas Ernest Rofe, the ex-
solicitor who was sentenced to five years’ im-
prisonment on a conspiracy charge in connection
with the notorious Butler divorce case, would be
remarkable in anyone but a N.S.W. ex-attorney.
Though sentenced, Rofe never served a day of
richly-deserved imprisonment. He had influen-
tial friends. The Judge was merciful enough to
release him under the First Offenders’ Act. This
seemed extraordinary, as Judge Backhouse was
far from satisfied with Rofe’s conduct in the wit-
ness-box, and plainly said so. It is the more extra-
ordinary, in view of the recent case of Richard
Croft, another N.S.W. solicitor, found guilty of
swearing a false affidavit, and sent to gaol for it—
though his conduct had none of the shameful con-
sequences of Rofe’s. But, then, Croft lacked “in-
fluential friends.” Again, while Rofe’s associates
in a diabolical crime—that of concocting and
fastening an immoral charge against a virtuous
woman and mother—were sentenced to long terms
of hard labor— Rofe himself was given absolute
freedom in the county of Cumberland. At the
time (’95) this strange divagation of justice excited
indignation. But Rofe was not an easily
satisfied man. On the grounds of possessing
fresh evidence of a startling nature, his case
was _ re-opened. Note again the influence
of friends! (Suffield’s case could not be re-
opened.) Judge Coffey (since deceased) con-
ducted the enquiry, and his finding was distinctly
unfavorable to the persistent solicitor. Rofe’s
sentence expired on 25th October, 1900, and now,
a year later, he requests re-instatement as a
solicitor—incidentally showing his opinion of
N.S.W. Supreme Court. And, just at this
point, the versatile Rofe unfolds a new
tale. He now avers that a certain draft affida-
vit of an incriminating nature which was found in
his possession when arrested was a stupid and
silly device on his part to trap Butler (his own
client !) in the hope of winning kudos in
the profession. Dr. Cogiilan is said to have
been aware of this baneful scheme of Rofe’s,
and to have advised him against it; but Dr.
Coghlan was not put forward as a witness either
at the Criminal Court, «r at the special enquiry
under the Criminal Law Amendment Act, or at
the last application. In stating Rofe’s case
before the Full Court, Attorney-General Wise
admitteu the allegation against Rofe to be that
he knew of the shocking conspiracy against Mrs.
Butler in January, 1895, and that heheld his tongue
in the matter till April following, when the draft
affidavit got into other hands. Apart from the
verdict of the jury as to Rofe’s guiltiness, the
fact that he kept to himself for three months
the knowledge that an unknown co-respondent
and petitioner’s private detective were one and the
same person should be sufficient for every honest
man. N.S.W. Full Court last week unanimously
refused to reinstate Rofe —at present. Pending
a fresh application, one would be glad to hear that
Mr. Rope had done something to compensate the
victim of the foul Butler conspiracy for the
injury wrought her.

The Concentration Camps—two
Explanations
The man who, charged with assault, pleaded first
that lie hit the man under great provocation, and,
secondly, that he didn’t hit him at all, failed for
obvious reasons. Nor did his ingenuity pay much
compliment to his common sense. Official Eng-
land, at last exasperated by the charges of whole-
sale child-murder in S’Africa into an attempt
to excuse itself, has within a week put for-
ward two “explanations” which, as cabled to
Australia, stand :
London, Tuesday afternoon.—Dr. W. H. Symons,
medical officer of health for the country borough of Bath
(Somersetshire) shows that the infant mortality in Birken-
head, Salford, and Manchester during the summer quarter
was greater than that in the concentration camps in
South Africa.
London, Sunday afternoon. —The Blue-Book which has
been issued in connection with the Concentration Camps
in South Africa states that the Boer mothers refuse to
obey the camp doctors or nurses, and administer, as a
remedy for measles, a tea made from the dung of goats
and horses.
The women also refuse to wash their children, although
they are as black as Kaffirs, owing to dirt.
They also paint the children’s bodies, except the faces,
with green paint, thereby causing arsenical poisoning, in-
sist upon administering Dutch quack medicinescontaining
ether and opium, feed the little ones on sardines instead
of milk, and use horse-dung poultices—refusing proper
ones.
Moreover, the women abstain from entering the
maternity hospitals, believing that they would be starved
therein. They prefer untrained attendance, and are
extremely callous of one another’s sufferings.
The effect of the two explanations is : that no ex-
ceptional infant mortality occurs : and that the
exceptional mortality which does not occur is due
to the barbarous and ignorant conduct of the Boer
women. Without the corroborative evidence of
the conflicting character of the two explanations,
showing that one or both must be false, it is not
hard to conclude that this green-paint and
goat-dung yarn (based, perhaps, on a few
isolated instances of superstition), is a clumsy
and venomous lie. Whenever in the past Mr.
Chamberlain has been in a particularly tight
place in regard to S’Africa some Boer atrocity or
instance of Boer filthiness has been alleged in
excuse. But at this stage of the war, when the
testimony of thousands and scores of thousands of
British and Australian soldiers exists to prove
that the Boers enjoy at least as high a degree of
civilisation as rural people in England or Aus-
tralia ; when the official records of farm-burning
generally relate the destruction of musical in-
struments and books ; the tough lies of this Blue
Book might well have been spared. Is it not
enough that a people, once chivalrous and gallant,
have been betrayed by their Chamberlains into
hanging the Boer men and killing the Boer
children ?

Subsidised &ugar-Mills.
Statistics of the Q. Govt.-subsidised sugar-mills
are interesting reading just now. Under Q.
“Sugar Works Guarantee Acts,” 1893 to 1895,
Govt, has advanced £498,800 to 13 mill and tram-
way co’s. Two mills worked under Govt, control
have received £13,800 more, making a total ad-
vance of £512,600; against which Govt, holds
securities valued at twice the amount (land mort-
gages and bills of sale on plant). The companies
are supposed to pay interest on advances
and a sum in redemption every year; but
at 30th June, 1901, only two companies were
clear—Mossman and Mulgrave. (Did not the
late Q. Minister Chataway say that the
advantage of having the Govt, for a creditor is
that you need never pay up, if you have political
“ pull ” enough ?) Proserpine owes £13,118; Plane
Creek owes £12,019 ; total owing for interest and
redemption by all co’s, £61,372. Yet the mill
shareholders were usually well repaid. A total
of 833 growers crushed 255,418 tons cane, and
divided £l6s,BBo—an average of nearly 13s. per
ton. The average mill profit was 2s. lgd. per ton
of cane; nearly 19s. per ton of sugar. On an
average B’BB tons cane made one ton 88 per cent,
sugar. As showing the profits of cane-growing,
66 Mossman growers paid all that was due to Govt,
out of mill profits, and received an average of £551
per man for their season’s cane. Forty-three Mul-
grave growers received an average of £673 per
man. The average return per grower for the
whole season’s product was nearly £2OO. A
little scrutiny of the statistics shows why some
mills make so much more profit than others. Mul-
grave and Mossman growers work better land,
and keep their mills supplied. At Mackay the
land is generally poorer ; and the district is over-
milled. The same with the Brisbane district.
Mills are erected to crush 2000 tons of sugar per
season, and the growers supply cane for only
1000. How can the mill pay expenses and
interest —running half-time ? Or they are erected
to crush 4000 tons of sugar, and the
growers supply cane for 2000 tons. How can the
Govt, get interest and redemption out of idle
machinery ? The two mills which have paid what
they owe have been kept supplied with cane ; the
others have not —and that is the reason why the
companies fall behind. “Political influence” has
put up mills where there is no hope of making
them pay; just as “political influence” repur-
chased, for £22,622, the private Seaforth estate, to
be cut up for agricultural settlement—upon which
not one man has been induced to settle ! It is
time that the working of Q. Sugar Works Guaran-
tee Acts was given to an independent Board, if an
independent Board can be found in Kanakaland.
Such a Board, by ruthlessly shutting down un-
profitable mills, and by erecting refineries, could
utilise the magnificent bonus given to Q. sugar-
growers by the Federal Protective duty in order
to make the industry the most profitable of all
Australian industries —and All White at that.
The Blessing' of the Plague.
The mild case of bubonic plague reported from a
Sydney suburb may prove a boon and a blessing,
for already it has set various authorities to work
washing up, and perhaps may force on that
thorough and general cleaning which the big dirty
seaport requires. When the plague last visited
Sydney the city got such a wash as it had never
had since the day of its birth. But nothing
really effective was done to compel landlords to
put insanitary hovels in order, or to properly
connect otherwise-cleanly properties with the
city sewerage. Moved thereto by a report that
only about 10 per cent, of the city houses, where
sewerage facilities were available, had proper
sanitary arrangements, Attorney-General B. R.
Wise threatened loudly to put certain
property-holders into the criminal dock,
and legislation was promised to empower
the Water and Sewerage Board to compel all
house-owners to comply with the requirements of
sanitary decency. But Mr. Wise’s words were
braver than his deeds ; and the scare began to die
down as plague cases lessened, so that in the end
nothing was done. Sydney had been washed
pretty thoroughly, but left with all the old facili-
ties for getting dirty again, and it very cheerfully
turned back to wallow in the mire. Perhaps,
with a mild case of plague or two now, with
a possibility that that disease will become
endemic in the city, the municipal and
State Governments will be startled into resolute
and effective action. A plague epidemic costing
500 lives would be a cheap price to pay for
thorough sanitary reform, which would prevent
at least that number of deaths per year from
pneumonia, typhoid, scarlet-fever, phthisis and
other diseases. Providence sent to Sydney one
blessing in the shape of a plague scare, but the
kindly warning was little heeded. Will a second
mild hint be more successful, or will common-
sense be learned only through calamity ?

The Black Bliglit-
A RESIDENT of Handah (Q.): —
The latest contention of the Kanaka-favoring sugar-
growers is that only small areas of cane can be worked
without the aid of Kanakas. If so, let us, by all means,
have the small area. Better that 10 men should earn
£2OO a year without the alien than one man should make
£2OOO with him. The mills, it is said, won’t collect the cane
on small areas, as Che expense would be too great. Bun-
kum ! Does anyone for an instant think that mill-owners
will let thousands of pounds’ worth of machinery lie idle
because a large estate has been cut up into smaller ones ?
One paper goes on to say that the only way for the small
cane-farmer to get his produce to the mill will be by the
aid of his wife and children, as white labor will be too
scarce. Scarcity of labor! Well, at the present time there
are hundreds of men here who would do any kind of
work and can’t get it at a decent wage, and, what’s more,
there always will be. Let any disinterested man reside
at Mackay, or any of the northern towns, for a time, and
then hear the views on the Kanaka question. No young
girl dare go out after dark in many places—not even in
daylight in some—without an escort. What with the
black scourge and the Yellow curse, this State is fast
becoming a hell upon earth.
A Maclean (N.S.W.) correspondent on the same
subject (showing how the black blight is
spreading):—
During the hearing of a charge of perjury against a
Hindoo at Grafton recently, three of the Hindoo witnesses
admitted that they had atone time or another either been
indicted for murder or fled from their country to escape
the just consequences of that act, while one had
stood his trial and been acquitted. Still another had
been charged with a serious offence on a female.
At almost every Quarter Sessions and Circuit Court at
Grafton and Lismore some of our lovable fellow subjects
from India are charged with abominable offences. The
aliens, though not numerous as yet, take up a very large
proportion of the time of any North Coast court.

The Cohentingenter—in War and. Peace.
Major Rupert Carrington sends to N.S.W.
Govt, an urgent appeal for 150 more Bushmen for
his regiment in S’Africa. The chance of getting
them is not a good one, unless deadbeats ;ire
picked, whose eyes are on the pay and plunder
offering and nothing else. Of the first con-
tingents to S’Africa a large number were men
and soldiers; they had the war-enthusiasm,
and, no matter what one’s opinion of the
justice of their cause, there could be no
doubt as to their sterling military quality. In
later contingents the ranks comprised many abso-
lute ruffians, and the chances are that recruits for
any body to be despatched now, when all the war
fever has subsided, would be made up very largely
of undesirables. And there are too many of them
in S’Africa just now. An Australian writing in
October from S’Africa to a friend in Melbourne
says:—
The Australians are admitted second to none on the
field. In the towns some of them are an easy first—for
disgraceful behaviour. They are conducting themselves
just like a herd of Kaffirs. One body recently paid off,
on their way down in the trains, robbed every station or
refreshment-room that the train stopped at.
In Queenstown they attempted to rob a Coolie of his
fruit, and upon his refusal to hand it over without a
struggle they belted his brains out. A Kaffir who went
to his assistance was similarly dealt with. They called at
a house one night, and a Kaffir opened the door. Some-
body kicked him in the stomach, and when he rose
another shot him dead. An inquest was held, the pub-
lished verdict being an open one.
Nearing this place (East London) some of them amused
themselves by running along the roof of the train and
flinging their saddles out of the windows at people on the
crossings. During their few days’ stay here numbers were
to be seen at all hours leaning up against each other for
support, helplessly drunk, and filthy dirty. I had the
pleasure (?) of hearing some of the officers call them “ the
riff-raff of Australia,” and certainly their recent behaviour
justifies the remark.
At Rensberg, recently, a crowd of Australians stormed
some kopjes, and, after a gallant fight, secured two
Maxims. Later on, the Boers returned and re-took the
g-uns. Rumor says that amongst the captured stores our
fellows found some whisky, and most of them got drunk.
Anyway, the Maxims were never seen again.
It is in one sense a good thing to have this sort of
fellow out of the country : it saves the police a
lot of trouble. But Australia, being a young
person just starting as a nation, can hardly stand
them being exported as representative types, and
the English commanders have enough work on
hand now, Heaven knows. So, we pray, no more
contingents.
“ Cane-cutter ” writes from Queensland on the
latest development in the plot to blacken Aus-
tralia :
The newest faked example of white men’s “ unre-
liability ”is at Ripple Creek, Herbert River, Q. The facts
are : 24 white men were engaged to cut and load cane, at
3s. per ton. Evidently they did not understand the work,
or they would not have taken the job at such a rate.
These men, after working two days, found that they were
only making wages at the rate of half-a-crown per day,
and they naturally jibbed. Now, it is well known that,
in N.Q., a nigger cuts and loads, on an average, 30cwt. of
cane per day, and (as the pro-aliens assert a white man
can’t do more work than a nigger), at 3s. per ton the
latter would only earn 4s. 6d. per day, less tucker (say,
25.) leaving the princely sum of 2s. 6d. for a day’s work.
When the pro-aliens can quote instances where white
men at 30s. per week, with decent food and accommoda-
tion, refuse to work at cultivating or harvesting sugar-
cane, it will then be time for Australians to believe in
these “ unreliable ” stories about white labor.
Cane-cutting is worth 10s. per day ; and, properly
managed, with the present duty, the Q. sugar
industry can pay that easily.

There is call for some statesmanship in dealing
with the New Guinea question. John Bui.l has
unloaded some White Man’s burden on to Aus-
tralia’s shoulders. But if Australia is to pay for
the up-keep of the Administration of British New
Guinea and its adjacent islands, she should
demand entire control thereof even to the
appointment of the Lieut.-Governor. This
country ought not to pay the piper while J. Bull
calls the tune and nominates his own piper. In
the future there must of necessity arise questions
—of water and road rights, of trading concessions,
etc. —with the contiguous States of German and
Dutch New Guinea. Britain hadn’t sense enough
to seize the whole property when she had the
chance, and some day it will probably pay
this country to buy the other owners out.
If so, they must be bought out for Australia, not
for Bull. The native question also demands some
attention. The Papuans, of course, will not be
given citizenship in the Commonwealth, and to
preserve a “ White Australia,” they must be
treated as colored aliens, though they are not
technically aliens, and kept from immigration to
the continent. From some points of view it may
be satisfactory to see Australia beginning to
assume her natural position as Suzerain of the
Pacific ; but there are disquieting possibilities of
trouble in taking rule over colored peoples, and
The B. does not look on the prospect with un-
alloyed joy.

It will not be the fault of the Victorian Education
Dept, if the children of the State schools are not
turned out red-hot patriots of the most approved
Imperial pattern. The Dept, recently issued a
circular to all head-teachers, giving directions for
the ceremony of saluting the flag at stated
intervals. The children are to be drawn up in a
hollow square facing the pole, and all, placing the
right hand on the left breast, are to say the
following words simultaneously: “I love God and
my country ; I honor the flag; I will serve the
King, and cheerfully obey my parents, teachers,
and the laws.” No explanation is given as to how
a child is to serve the King. Directions are given
that the flag is to be hoisted every Monday
morning, the children to repeat simultaneously
the words of the foregoing declaration ; and on
public holidays, such as the King’s birthday, the
school-rooms are to be decorated with suitable
mottoes, such as “the crimson thread of kinship. ”
On school-days special lessons must be given bear-
ing on the subject. There is thus no danger that
“ Gor Save ” will be a forgotten anthem, or that
there will be any lack of machine-made “loyalists ”
and offensive little prigs.

Two days last week the audience at Melbourne
Divorce Court got some mild entertainment from
the case of Kitz v. Kitz. Mrs. Kitz, a daughter
of the late millionaire, J. B. Watson, whose
sorrows are somewhat soothed by a private income
of £2OOO a year from the Watson estate, petitioned
for a divorce on the ground of desertion ; but, inas-
much as the deserter appeared to be her own fair
self rather than respondent Kitz, her petition was
dismissed. The case, indeed, was a palpable
waste of legal expenses. The law, as it stands,
holds out no hope of freedom for the lady as
long as her husband lives; neither does the
law care twopence whether they live together
or otherwise. Both Kitzes agree (and the Judge
agreed with them) on one painful point. The
husband is subject to attacks of nervous trouble,
at which times they are best apart. Mrs. K.
reckons they are best apart at all other times, and,
presumably, if the intermittent delusionist were
to apply for restitution of conjugal rights, the law
would side with the mother of his children. And
yet it refuses to dissolve the contract whereOy
Mrs. Kitz married Kitz for better or for worse.
Also, if Kitz were now to desert his wife
in reality, or introduce a co-respondent
upon the scene, the law would be in
honor bound to accuse his wife of “ contributory
negligence,” or something to that effect, and
refuse a decree nisi accordingly. Likewise, it is
certain that if Kitz were to kidnap the lady and
insist upon resuming honeymoon relations, despite
her existing right to keep him at a distance, then
the law—civil or criminal—would give her no
more redress than it offers to Kitz, the unwilling
grass-widower. All of which suggests that
marriages are not made in Heaven, where neither
wrath nor lust doth corrupt, and Kitz cannot
break through and steal.

Pooh old Simon Fraser, for want of a ready wit,
had to let himself be most absurdly bluffed by
Labor Senator M ‘Gregor, the other night. They
were discussing the eternal question of newspaper
“sedition,” and Fraser, in a flaming fury,
said if he were the autocrat of Britain’s
“ free breakfast table ” he would hang mast-high
any who spoke disrespectfully of the British Flag.
Then M ‘Gregor wanted to know whether Senator
Fraser would hang any man who advocated a
forcible change of Government, to which the Old
Man dutiful replied, “ Aye, that 1 would—l would
string them up ! ” M ‘Gregor then said, “Itis as
well that Senator Fraser didn’t live in the days
of King William, or he would have hanged that
monarch.” A roar of laughter followed. The
Senate evidently thought that M ‘Gregor had
said something clever, and the unhappy
Fraser couldn’t raise the means of undeceiving
their hilarity. He squirmed in silence. By this
time, perhaps, it has occurred to Fraser that
William 111 was only the leading man in a
successful revolution which happened to have
come to stay. Had there been a successful re-
action in favor of the King who fled, it would
have been quite open to the reactionists to wipe
out William on the scaffold the same as Charles
First would have beheaded Cromwell if
the luck hadn’t gone t’other way. Up
to now, the practical advocates of a forcible
change of Government, or some of them, have
come to a gory end whenever the “ treason ”
failed. Old-fashioned Fraser was not at all out-
of-date in his screech, only blind to his oppor-
tunity. He might have crushed his tormentor by
quoting the historical fact that his (Fraser’s)
ancestor, Lord Lovat, was executed by the afore-
said William for the treason of attempting—it
was a stupidly weak attempt —to replace on the
throne the very James whom William had
ousted. So, there now ! Does it astonish Labor-
Senator M ‘Gregor to hear that Might is in-
variably Right ? He preaches the doctrine him-
self every time he asserts the majesty of a majority
vote —only he doesn’t know the true inwardness
of his own gospel. Might is Right. Anything is
Right as long as it continues to flourish, and the
men who vainly rail against it can consider them-
selves hanged.

Barrister C. G. Wade, as Royal Commissioner
to inquire into the accident which, on May 24,
killed six men in Broken Hill South mine, ex-
presses some opinions which ought to make miners
sit up. For instance :
All mining is more or less dangerous ; and if a man
undertakes to perform and continues to do certain work
which obviously entails a risk, and he has the means of
ascertaining the nature of the employment and its
dangers, he is deemed thereby to have contracted to sub-
mit to the risks incidental to such operation. . . . These
men knew that they must rely upon themselves, and
must use every care. Yet no complaint was made to the
mine officials that would lead them to believe that the
men refused to take the risk. The fact, therefore, of
their continuance at work under these circumstances
forces your Commissioner to the conclusion that the un-
fortunate men who are now dead undertook the risks
that were incidental to this operation.
Apparently therefore neither owner nor manager
takes any responsibility. A manager tells a man
to go into a working: apparently the onus is
thrown on the miner to decide whether it is safe

The Tasmanian Supreme Court has now
on hand a long job—the action V.D.L. Co. v.
Table Cape Marine Board, which has run into
weeks. The co. is suing the Board for trespassing
on the foreshore by building a jetty. The co.
claims a trifle of 105 miles of Tasmania’s coastline
under its original grant, and bars the Tas. Govt,
from crossing said coast-line without paying toll.
Barristers Bryant and Waterhouse appear for the
co., while Senator Symon and Tas. Solicitor-
General Dobbie are doing their best to dodge the
effects of the hideous blunder the old-time Tas.
Govt, made in failing to hand the whole island
over to the V.D.L. Co. while it was about it.
