“The Sign of the Cross,” as interpreted
by its original perpetrators, at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, on Saturday night, was marked by more
.vigor and earnestness than the Sydney public had
been accustomed to note in the efforts of the pre-
vious co. The Christian prayers were more
strenuous, the Pagans were a little more drunken
and dishevelled, the points at which the young
.lady in the stalls doesn’t like to meet the eyes of
the young gentleman accompanying her, were more
.strongly marked. Delicacy of treatment or
restraint are emphatically not among Mr. Bar-
rett’s methods. As Marcus Superbus, he shows
you clearly that he is superb, and mighty by
every strut, and gesture, and intonation ; by the
gorgeous series of frocks (six in all) in which he
clothes a Roman of the epoch when manhood was
giving place to millinery; by the devices with
which he ekes out the somewhat scanty inches
that Nature has given him, so that he may show
up as the noblest and tallest Roman of them all.
There is just as marked an absence of restraint in
his portrayal of Superbus’ passionate pursuit
of Mercia, culminating in a scene of attempted
violence, which, as acted by him and Miss Lillah
McCarthy, fairly made the audience gasp. It is
often said that Australian audiences demand of
the actor bolder, broader effects than are con-
sidered correct in London. Comparison of “The
Sign of the Cross” as played by a partly Aus-
tralian, and by a “complete London c 0.,” does
not bear this theory out. The play is one in
which a certain glossing over of offensive and
absolutely painful scenes would be of advantage.
If Stephanus’ wild shrieking during the torture
scene were omitted, if the closing incident (not
easy to describe in detail in a public newspaper)
of Act 111. were toned down, the development of
the play would be just as strong and clear, and
the clergy would surely attend just as enthusi-
astically. But Mr. Barrett apparently does not
think so. In his interpretation, Stephanus’ shriek
is more poignant, the attempt on Mercia’s virtue
more ardent than before. In the scene referred to,
theactingof Mr. Barrett and of Miss M‘Carthy is
vivid a.nd real: the passion of the one, the respond-
ing passion in the other kept in control only by
supreme effort, is represented with realistic
strength and power. ,
• * *
*
Three Pagan ladies of beauty are very
successfully played by Miss Edyth Latimer, Miss
Daisy Belmore, and Miss Nellie Bonser. The
last, as Poppea, was the only quiet, restful woman
in the play : one can understand why a nerve-
wracked monarch like Nero found comfort with
this placid person when the balance of the female
population of Rome seemed to be either bibulous
or hysterical. Mr. George Barrett was very good
in a small part; Mr. Atholwood painted his Nero
in far too lurid colors. In various matters of
detail the production showed the improvement
whieh can be effected by painstaking stage-
management —Mr. Barrett’s strong point. The
theatre, in spite of the tropical temperature, was
crowded, and the audience markedly enthusiastic.
* # ifc #
The penalty of a popular play is that in its
old age it falls into the hands of poor companies,
and probably the majority of the rising generation
have never seen “East Lynne,” except as in-
terpreted by a stout Isabel and a phlegmatic and
round-faced Archibald Carlyle. At the Theatre
Royal, last Saturday, Holloway raised the per-
formance of this melancholy drama nearer to
its old-time level. The version is a good one on
the whole, and the scenery is lively. At the end
of one pathetic speech the scene divides in two,
carrying a lady with a table to one side, and two
other persons with the rest of the furniture to the
other. Lady Isabel sees more visions than usual.
While Levison is tempting her to fly, the drawing-
room wall disappears upwards, showing Archibald
and Barbara merely saying good-bye to each other
under a porch, which proceeding on Archibald’s
part finally determines Lady Isabel to fly. In
the lodging-house scene the wall is once more
obliging in order to show Archibald with his
children at his knee, the impression being that the
piece of masonry divides itself to very little
purpose. Laurence Dunbar, as Archibald Carlyle,
make a very fair presentment of the cold, good
hero in whom Mrs. Wood delighted. On the
desertion of Lady Isabel he strikes a pathetic note
by subsiding into a dressing-gown. Ida Gresham
rather fails to touch the right chord in the little
Willy scene, but in other respects gives a good
picture of the lady who ran away with someone—
mainly because she saw her husband shake hands
with another lady. The singing of “ When Other
Lips ” was not a success, however, and that
melancholy tune might be banished, from the
drama. Miss Roxy Barton and Miss .Madge
Hope make creditable first appearances with this
cqmpany, Miss Madge Hope giving a sympathetic
Joyce Hallijohn. Miss Roland Watts-Phillips
gives a clever, unexaggerated presentment of
Cornelia Carlyle.
“ Wang ” still runs a merry course at the
Sydney Criterion, and is proving a liberal educa-
tion in American humor to many citizens. The
embarrassed Siamese ruler, who, owing to the
state of his exchequer, hasn’t been able to reign so
far “but only to sprinkle,” is a distinctly bright
character, and, effectively played, helps the
comedy materially to attain success, whilst Miss
Stanton, as a sacred Siamese white prince in a
suit of flannels, is also a valuable source of enter-
tainment.
** * «
At Sydney Empire much the same bill as
usual is being presented, and the negro comedian
falls about with the old captivating disregard of
his bones. Audiences generally are fairly large
and appreciative.
** * *
“ The Ladder of Life, or Gordons to the
Front,” revived at the Lyceum last Saturday, is a
play crowded with bare-kneed Highlanders (re-
cruited in Ireland), who claim to be doing great
deeds “for England.” The first act of “The
Ladder ” is mainly domestic, representing an old
gentleman making and remaking wills in his front
garden, with intervals for fits. When he has
died, the heroine is accused of being the mother
of a small boy who has strolled on the pre-
mises. Naturally everybody adjourns to the
Heights of Dargai, where the natives come
in from the wings and fall down before any-
one does anything to them. The way
natives fall down dead before the awful British
eye is a striking feature iii this great patriotic
drama. Later, the sub-heroine is rescued from
the Great Nasmyth Hammer by three gentlemen
who make a.living by standing on one another’s
shoulders to get people out of awkward places,
after which all is peace. Good work in the
humorous line is done by Temple Harrison and
Miss Buckley, as a playful policeman and a
sprightly servant-girl. Stirling White, as usual,
dies in the first act, and has the rest of the evening
off. He is a good actor, and should occasionally
be permitted by the management to survive into
act 2. H. O. Willard is an extra-smart villain,
who allots parents to the awkward small child just
as it suits him. Miss Helen Fergus is, as usual, a
capital old woman, and the Silvinis make ladders
of themselves in the day of trouble to everybody’s
satisfaction.
** * «
There is still no change in the performers
at the Tivoli, though several new songs vary Part
One. Miss Neva Carr Glynn sings “ Home,
Deary, Home,” and Will Whitburn introduces a
new humorosity, while Howard Chambers still
continues to “Stand upon his Vessel’s Deck,” to
general satisfaction. The last nights are an-
nounced of Prof. De Wynne, juggler and shadow-
graphist, and of the marvellous Craggs.
** * *
Judging from the general rank, standing,
and applause of the crowd at Her Majesty’s, Mel-
bourne, last Saturday night, this return season of
the Italian co. will be properly appreciated.
Melbourne rolled up to the feast of opera, and
licked its lips between the courses. Five months
ago, when the trouble at Her Majesty’s was to
keep one’s teeth from chattering, the most ardent
love-music had pretty much the effect of a hot
chop served on ice ; but at this time of year the
Philistine floats delightedly with a high C, so to
speak, and everything in Marguerite’s garden
seems great, you understand. Nothing but a fall
of frozen brickbats, warranted to wreck the build-
ing, can stop the success of the Italian summer
season.
• * * *
After Hazon had accepted the plaudits of
Saturday’s audience he smote the music-stand
with his little rod, and “Faust” gushed forth.
There was some “ Faust” at the Princess’s about
this time 1900 A.D., but the rendering of Gounod’s
opera in English couldn’t be said to gush. It
trickled, pleasingly. The present performance,
considered as a whole, is no doubt the strongest
that has been given in Australia, and because all
parts of the whole carry their recommendation
with them it is permissible to remark that this
“ Faust ”is not a smasher of records all round. J.
C. Williamson’s show wins on its average, although
no feature of superlative excellence shines out.
Cattadori, as Valentine, the one principal who
threatened to be weak, was a satisfactory surprise
in his singing and appearance. Apparently he
took liberties with the music to suit himself.
Signorina Bassich made a bright little Siebel, and
the Martha was a valuable item. Travaglini has
improved vastly since he played Mephistopheles
with the Cutticas years ago. He puts more vigor
and humor into his acting, and his great voice
sounds smoother than before ; but, seeing that the
Devil never attempts to conceal his identity and
is even addicted to red-lighting effects, present
scribe would like to know why Travaglini has
modified the luridness of his costume. Why not
go the whole Devil in a brilliant all-crimson
costume, as of yore ? Remembering the silken
redness of Travaglini’s original garments one is
sorry to find him in half-mourning.
* * * *
Carlo Dani is not yet too plump for the
name-part. He looks well and sings sweetly ; and
Signora Sapio, who is more like a pretty actress
than a conventional Marguerite, sings sweetly,
likewise; All the same, the duets of these capital
artists were rather passionless on Saturday, and
Dani’s tall solo, “ Salve Dimora, ” suggests that lie
was not in his best voice, which is at least in-
tended as a compliment to his second best voice,
for Dani, like the other principals, had to refuse
encores. The bill at Her Majesty’s for this week
is “II Trovatore” to-night (Thursday), “Faust”
to-morrow, and “ Otello ” on Saturday, with
Larizza as the vehement Moorish gentleman.
** * *
The sugary-sentiment and gold-lace trim-
mings of “ The Second in Command ” have given
much satisfaction to moderate-sized audiences of
mostly women at Melb. Princess’s, but the average
man who wants a lot of laugh in his comedy, and is,
moreover, weary of kharki details, doesn’t enthuse
over Brough’s present entertainment. Yet he
seems to believe in Brough’s alleged deed of valor>
for when the portentous royal Jook brings the
Major a nice little Victoria Cross “from the King,”
as though His Maj. had bought it out of his own
money and was dying to know how it would
go with the pattern of the invalid’s coat, there
is a hearty banging of hands together, and it
doesn’t seem to be a loyal demonstration pro-
vided by the management either. Fact is, the
Jook’s visit in the play is more of a splash of
realism than the genuine “York” amounted to.
Royalty was made for the stage. “The Second
in Command ” is to put out its invisible camp
fires after Friday night, and on Saturday the
bill at the Princess’s will be “The Magistrate”
—Godblesshim !
** * *
A first change of programme at Fitzgeralds’
Circus brought the High Diving Horses to their
watery plunge on Saturday, when the Fitz.’s
spacious iron tabernacle was crowded once again.
The Diving Gees do their duty to the advt.,
although they don’t happen quite as was expected.
They are a delusive fact and an exciting fraud at
present, but they promise to acquire genuineness
with further practice. On Saturday the champion
diver, who is a mare, was rather more nervous
than her two pupils. The three distinguished
animals deserved greater honor for running up an
incline to the high-diving platform than for the
uneasy manner in which they got off the platform
when it tilted them towards the deep water-hole in
the arena. Having consented to take the narrow
way that leadeth to the mechanical precipice there
comes a moment in their lives when they are bound
to fall into 18ft. of water unless they can hang on to
something by their huge yellow teeth, and at this
supreme crisis each animal in turn appears to
shut its eyes and leave the rest to Providence. A
Diving Horse would seem rather a matter for
sympathy if it didn’t come along and drop to its
fate for a second and third time, which indicates
fortitude and ambition in the noble animal.
Apart from this damp equine novelty the circus
second programme is mostly different to the first.
The “ Damen Kapelle” has been turned on, like-
wise the tiger and the leather-plated steed, and an
Arab Wonder from the Sahara Desert or some-
where. Also, the two boys, Cardello and Shand,
who have grown up with the circus and are tall
boys now, have arrived at the dignity of a double-
jockey act on a horse instead of a pony.

The Baccarat Scandal, the avalanching of
Leopold Bland Holt into eternity, the Study from
the Nood, and the biographic description of the
beastly brewer are occurring six times a week at
Melb. Royal. This is B.H.’s fourth week as
Leopold Kalditz, and he continues to lend £lO,OOO
to Miss Ross on the insecurity of Miss Ireland’s
innocent young life with a recklessness that is
somewhat shocking in one whose name ends with
“itz.” Bland has been several different sorts of
Jew during his long and honorable melodramatic
career, but he never before was such an easy
Hebrew to get money from as is the purblind dis-
counter in “Hearts are Trumps.”

It appears that “ her own ” London con-
cert which Miss Amy Castles was said to be going
to “give” on the 19th, was really a Kubelik con-
cert, so the friends of the re-boomed young woman
have brought considerable influence (or coin) to
bear upon the matter. Kubelik, who heads the
show, is the star violinist of the season, but a
cable to the Melbourne dailies alleges that the
“patronage” of Jook of York was due to the
presence of Amy rather than the genius of the
young fiddler. Anyway, the Vie. Agent-General
seems to have somehow got a letter from the
King announcing that he took a royal interest in
Amy’s debut. And now it remains to be seen
what the least uncandid critics will say about
Miss Castles as a vocalist, at a concert where at
least one of the performers would be in the highest
class.
Vic. Railway Department is tempting Pro-
vidence with a vengeance. Yet another returning
engine ran on the wrong line the other day, and
had gone some distance before the driver recol-
lected he was going straight to smash. The usual
secret departmental inquiry has recommended
‘ ‘ dealing with ” the culpable officials indulgently.
Meanwhile Vic. public only travels when it has to,
and then mostly rides with its head out of the
window.
A well-known of Swanston-street,
Melb., is an octogenarian Irishwoman,. who
shuffles along gaily in sunshine or rain, trying to
sell bootlaces. To a sympathetic purchaser, who
remarked that it was a wet day for her to be out,
she replied, surprised, “ An’ what else would I be
doin’, my son?” The old lady is a good R.C.,
which is doubtless why she harbors no resentment
against her deceased brother, who bequeathed
£l6OO to the Church, and left his sister to struggle
on in poverty to the end.
John See, N.S.W. Minister for War, no
less, at the Hector Macdonald pow-wow :
What N.S.W. had done in the war, was nothing to
what they would do if necessity arose. They had sent
6000 men, but, if the men were needed, they would send
another 5000, or even 10,000. He was very proud that
this had been done while he was Minister for War.
They would, would they ! Let Mr. John See
bring down to Parliament a proposal to send
another 5000 men, and see who will be Minister
for War a month after !
Perce Abbott, a valued Bulletin contri-
butor, died suddenly last week in a Melb. hos-
pital. Abbott was American-born, and articled
to the law, but before completing his time was
appointed to the U.S. Consulate at Dunedin.
Thence he went to Fiji, and came back to Riverina
(N.S.W.) journalism. He was a charming racon-
teur, also a clever musician, and composer of
several popular songs ; and in the last half-dozen
years was all over the world as pianist with
Heller’s Mahatma company. A good fellow, of
varied talents, many will be sorry to
bear of his death.
The tailoresses’ strike broke 'out afresh in
Sydney last week, and Anthony Hordern’s em-
ployees are again “out.” It is said that the wages
Eoints in dispute between the firm and its girls
. ave been settled, and the only question remain-
ing is that of the exclusive employment of union
labor. The strikers generally have the public
sympathy, and collections in their aid have
brought in a good sum of money. The
men engaged in the loading of frozen
meat are also on strike for an increase of
pay from 9d. to Is. an hour, with allowances
for overtime. Seeing that they have to work
partly in an Arctic temperature, their demands
are strictly reasonable ; and the employers are
likely to concede them. Boot-trade workers are
moving for increases, and, generally, the tariff
seems to have been the signal for all hands to ask
for a rise—and to get it.
Waverley Bowling Club last week held a
progressive-euchre party to celebrate the return ol
one of the N.S.W. bowling contingenters who
lately raided Britain. After the game the Presi-
dent arose and handed out the prizes, and when
he came to the. “ booby ” prize, he solemnly as-
sured the unwilling recipient that any prize was
better than no prize at all. He didn’t know what
the packet contained, but no doubt it was some-
thing he might treasure all his days, and pass
on to his descendants. Hereupon he handed over
a much-wrapped parcel, but the suspicious winner
gave it back to be sold by auction for the benefit
of the Waverley Benevolent Society, and it was
sold and resold till it had brought £l2 for the
society’s funds ; fhen it was opened and found to
be a box of Fry and Sons’ (of cocoa fame) safety
matches. The Australian representative of Fry’s
bought in the lot for £24, so that with the Govt,
subsidy of £ per £, the little box of 70 matches
brought in £72 (over £1 per match), for the benefit
of the charity named.
Mr. C. O. Michel (erstwhile N.S.W. Govt,
interpreter).is exhibiting, in Sydney, anew fire-
escape, which he has patented as “ The Cham-
pion. ” The apparatus is very simple, and seems
perfect. It is a ladder built on the Venetian-blind
system, which hangs unobtrusively inside your
bedroom window when not required. When the
alarm comes, it is heaved out and hangs like a
staircase down the wall; the weight of the
person descending keeping it perfectly rigid. It
can be used by elderly men and old ladies,
to whom the knotted rope and the rope-
ladder would be simply a mocking delusion.
It can be carried to any part of the house, and if
there are no hooks in the window-sill to fasten it
to, the spreader of a bedstead can be utilised, or
the. whole bedstead itself, or anything that is
sufficient to bear the weight of a human being.
Just now Mr. Michel, who is over middle-age, is
travelling round Sydney throwing the escape from
windows and descending hastily to demonstrate
how a man may carry out the whole of his family
one after another, as it is just as easy to ascend by
the escape as to descend. The price is only £3
10s. for an escape suitable for an ordinary dwell-
ing-house.

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly, classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The'table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

A very successful trial of the Gardner Electric
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily fixed, bored 8J inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill.*

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing)
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-,
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

Mr. Orlando H. Baker, U.S. Consul (who seems
to devote more time to thinking and less to flapping his
wings than his predecessor in office) tells some home
truths to the Australian public on the question of
finance. Sydney D.T., having bewailed the fact
that, under the M’Kinley tariff, America always had a
surplus, which it squandered, and thus robbed the people
by unnecessary taxation, Mr. Baker made these remarks :
“There is no weeping in America over having a surplus
in the Treasury. We wish it were larger ; we wish it
were large enough to pay the whole Government debt.
The truth is, it is not a surplus at all. A nation, or a
man, cannot be said to have a surplus as long as just
debts are unpaid. The U.S. debt is not far from
£400,000,000. It is the fixed policy of the U.S. Govt, to
pay off the debt as soon as possible, and stop the slavery
of interest. The man or the nation that is in debt, in the
opinion of the thinking American citizen, is not free.
The Republican party accumulated a surplus, and besides
paying out millions to the soldiers who saved the union
and perpetuated free government, steadily reduced the
public debt until 1892. There was a cry raised against
this policy . . . and the people elected Grover Cleveland.
What followed ? ... In time of peace, with all the re-
sources of the country unimpaired, Grover Cleveland in-
creased the public debt over £25,000,000 to meet the
ordinary expenses of the Government. That cured the
people of their dissatisfaction with a “surplus,” and at
the end of Cleveland’s term they elected M'Kinley . . .
and although millions were spent in the Spanish war, the
U.S. Government, under Republican administration, has
not borrowed a dollar, and has something in the Treasury
to pay on the public debt. I wish Australia a like happy
state of affairs.” To this The B. says devoutly “ Amen! ”
Mr. Baker’s remarks come at a most opportune moment,
justwhen theForeign-tradepapers are painfully shocked at
the discovery that in the 22 days of last month the Federal
tariff brought in as much as £684,000, and are calling
loudly in consequence for a big reduction of taxation to
avoid the horrors of a surplus. “ A nation cannot be said
to have a surplus so long as just debts are unpaid.” The
sentence should be inscribed in letters of gold over the
portals of every Australian Parliament House. The
Australian States at present not only have so much just
debt that their resources are almost mortgaged up to the
hilt, but have either sold or are selling the public estate,
which was part of the security for the loan, and using the
proceeds as revenue; and are borrowing further sums,
year by year, to pay ordinary house-keeping, fence-
repairing, road-patching expenditure.
If the Federal Government were to raise
£10,000,000 or £11,000,000 a year, instead of something
under £9,000,000, it could find honest and economical use
for every penny of its share, and each State could also
employ profitably and honestly the amount returned to
it from that revenue. But the Australian political idea
seems to be to shunt every responsibility, dodge every
liability, indulge in every possible national dishonesty,
and so drift on to the inevitable crisis.
* * * *
Why should the wasteful system of selling loam
stock at a fixed minimum be continued by the Australian
Governments when they fly their kites in London ? All
recent new flotations have been offered on that plan.
John Bull, when he borrows, fixes a minimum, but no
maximum, and offers the loan to the public on the
tender system. You can offer to pay £9B, the mini-
mum or £lOO or £lOl for each unit of stock, and the
highest bidders have their money taken. But the
Australian Government fixes a figure, say £97, pays a
commission to someone to underwrite the issue, and then
offers the stock at that absolute price. Some investors,
in order to make sure of getting the stock, might be will-
ing to pay £99, but they are not allowed to. The Aus-
tralian financial business in London is just as badly mud-
dled in details as on main principles. ,
* • * •
An item in the Bank of Australasia balance-
sheet this year is £lO,OOO for “ reduction of bank premises
account.” The chairman, referring to this, said that the
ideal system in regard to premises was to follow the
example of the Bank of England, which in its balance-
sheets counted its building as of no value at all. How
would some Australian insurance and building societies,
whose only big asset is their building, pan out under that
condition ? An asset is only worth what it will realise on
Bale, and most of the huge buildings put up by financial
companies would not bring a quarter of their cost in the
case of a crisis, when some scores of big buildings would
be suddenly rushed on the market.
'* * « •
A rumor slithered round lately that the B.H.
Prop, was on the verge of shutting down for some weeks.
There was nothing in it, but it made a lot of people feel
peculiar. What has actually happened is that the chloro-
dising works have been shut up—but that is a little
thing. The most hopeful feature in the Broken Hill
situation is the likelihood of the early re-starting of the
zinc works, partly through improvements in the magnetic
processes, partly through lowering of freights, partly
through European coal and coke being much cheaper
than they have been lately. These zinc-extraction pro-
cesses are mostly in German and Belgian hands; it is
stated that these people look for an improved lead-market
(lead is their chief by-product) early in the year, though
so far as English advices are concerned they are a blank.
■» * * *
Booth’s Reward, N.9.W.: This thing has come
to life again with all its paper richness. The Sabbath
Tipster has come to its rescue, and this is how he does it:
“ The ore is rich in mineral, carrying as much as 75 per
cent, thereof, but no gold can be observed by the naked
eye. Assays, however, show it to be auriferous to the
extent of soz. to the ton. The difficulty, of course, is
the extraction ; and to accomplish this the English experts
propose to adopt a method of concentration which" has
lately given excellent results at home. It appears that
for the purpose of this method a by-product of kerosene
shale, in the form of oil, is largely used, but the details
have not been disclosed.” Hitherto it has been generally
understood that everything else connected with shale but
oil is a by-product. The tipster has got things upside-
down as usual.
• • * *
Chillagoe Railway and Mines : This “ oat ” has
got out of the bag at last, and newspapers which for the
last three years have published columns of comment
without facts are now engaged in publishing columns of
facts without comment, allowing the public to draw, as
it can easily do, its own conclusion. Danvers Power’s
figures of tonnage and values, the result of an examina-
tion extending over two months, are even worse than
Weinberg’s, and do not confirm those of Stewart, which
most newspapers said was likely. Stewart’s estimate was
600,000 tons of ore proved to date (30th June, 1900) of
good workable value, portions of which would be dressed
but would produce 300,000 tons to smelt, containing
17,000 tons of copper, 1500 tons of lead, and a million
ounces of silver, besides appreciable gold. When asked
if this estimate was in addition to that contained in his
original report Mr. Stewart replied that the two estimates
were perfectly distinct. One—the first—“ referred to the
probable amount of ore judged from surface exposure, and
the other was the ore proven by work done since.” These
figures were accepted as correct until Weinberg, last July,
reported that the ore-reserves of the Chillagoe mines, ex-
clusive of the Mungana, consisted of 173,631 tons, averag-
ing 2.35 per cent, copper, a little overloz. silver, and con-
taining a total of 4086 tons copper, 476 tons lead,
192,0830z5. silver, and 15960 z. gold. There is a tremendous
falling-off here. Danvers Power was appointed to check
both estimates after Stewart had declared that his esti-
mate was correct, would admit of no question or qualifica-
tion whatever, and would be borne out under capable
management.
Power says : “There is very little actual ore in
sight to be seen in any of your mines. Most of your
actual ore in sight is confined to the heaps of mined ore
on the surface, and some of these will have to be picked
over before they are sufficiently rich to pay for treat-
ment.” Of course, he did not wish to convey the idea
that there was no other ore left in the leases,
but the deposits were not opened up to warrant
one in estimating a fixed quantity of ore as proved,
and this is equivalent to saying that the mines are
not sufficiently developed to keep the furnaces in blast.
His estimate of tonnage of the Chillagoe and Mungana
mines combined is 139,837 tons, or about 75,000 tons in
the Chillagoe mines alone, and the total contents are put
down at 5696 tons copper, 2780 tons lead, and 331,9370 z.
silver, and a little over 1500 oz. gold. Here is another
tremendous falling-off. Power’s tonnage is considerably
lower than Weinberg’s, but his copper contents are
higher, and that will probably account for the discre-
pancy. One man has measured any ore, the other only
pay ore. Power says that as the venture was hardly out
of the prospecting stage, a great mistake was made in
proceeding with what" was intended to be permanent
work before the nature of the property was properly
understood ; that the mines are not in a position to be
operated economically ; and as preparations for stoping
must be made, mining expenses would be greater than
they should be ; that for want of ore to keep the nest of
furnaoes going one furnace had to bear the expense
of services which would otherwise be divided over
the whole plant, hence the richer ores from the
various groups had to be selected, and some of it had
to be carted long distances. Weinberg says while some
of the garnet formations were not likely to improve in
depth, there were Others like the Zillmanton, Ruddygore,
and Redcap group which offer every inducement for
energetic development. He agrees with Power, that in
many cases the mining expenditure had been on unpro-
ductive ground, and that most of the important lodes
were allowed to remain practically unexplored, conse-
quently considerable expense would have to be incurred
before they could expect to reap any material benefit
from these mines. At the same time he thinks the pro-
spect is such as to justify the directors in asking share-
holders to provide the additional capital required for the
purpose. And the public, is told that the directors are
seriously considering proposals to raise additional capital,
and will place them before shareholders at the earliest
moment.
The Age says that this information comes as a
great shock to the public, especially after the pinnacle of
mining excellence reached by Stewart. The financial
condition is much worse than the prospects of the mines.
£590,000 has been spent to date—railway and rolling-
stock, £382,000 ; mines, including plant, £140,000 ; reduc-
tion works, £53,000. The co. is burdened with a huge
debenture-debt, which it is not possible to liquidate with
shares. It borrowed £50,000 some months ago, and it is
said that it is another £25,000 behind, and shareholders
are to be called to face the music. Some weeks ago, a
statement was published showing the share-transactions
of some of the big men since the formation of the co.,
making it quite clear that, while the public was going in
largely with the assistance of false newspaper reports, the
others were quietly getting out. They sold between them
some tens of thousands of shares, and the share-regis-
ter shows that most of them were sold at the
best market-prices. The original founder of the
mine, John Moffat, is the only director who retained
his full interest, 25,000 shares, which he holds
to-day, and by comparison, anyhow, that is something to
be proud of. The price Moffat got for these mines in cash
and shares was insignificant compared to the valuation
they subsequently reached. Perhaps the greatest shock
of his life was when he read Stewart’s report on Chillagoe,
and as Stewart was then the greatest mining gun boom-
ing under the Southern Cross, no doubt Moffat thought
he had sold the mine too cheap, and that the best thing
was to stick to his shares ; either that, or he determined
to see the thing through and not allow the public to
suffer by reason of his exercising knowledge in respect to
which it was kept in the dark. The dates in the share-
register show that some people did not believe in
Stewart, that they exercised their own judgment, or they
got the truth from other experts. But the public was
induced to pin its faith to Stewart. The matter
demands the strictest investigation.
Before any meeting of shareholders to put up
more money takes place, a committee should be appointed
to see how far the directors themselves are responsible for
the present state of things, and the extent to which the
public has been played with. And if it is proved that the
directors are still satisfied with the greatness of these
mines, and that those of them who parted with shares parted
for the most excellent of reasons, then let them put up the
money required to put the mines into a working and pay-
ing condition. There is, however, very little hope of that.
But for the big Broken Hill and Mt. Lyell mining guns
the Chillagoe mines would have been regarded as
only prospects, money would have been put up
to explore them, and if they turned out failures
that would have been the end of them. £60,000
would have been ample for that purpose. As it
is, this co. built a railway costing nearly £400,000
for which at present there is little use. It spent
£140,000 in mining, and, according to its own
officers, most of this has been thrown away— i.e., small,
pockety garnet formations have been worked as if they
were permanent bodies, while big lodes, having ironstone
and gossan cappings, containing carbonates of copper on
the surface, with every appearance of sulphides below,
are practically untouched. It has spent over £50,000 in
building six furnaces, which could be put into blast
immediately—and there is only one running. Even the
estimated value of the ore was nearly double the actual
value proved to-day by smelting, and many of the dumps
are so low in value that they will not stand carting to the
smelters; while the leases from which the ore was
obtained are not good enough to warrant their being con-
nected by tramway.
It would be a sorry day for the debenture-
holders if they were asked to take over the whole pro-
perty for the debt, so what a lively time there is before
the unfortunate shareholders ! Altogether, it looks like
Australia’s biggest mining fiasco, and should make Aus-
tralia’s so-called biggest miningmen feel very mean indeed.
The public has been dazzled by the glamour surrounding
the name of Stewart, the finest mining administrator that
Broken Hill has ever had ; but it now begins to remember*
or hears for the. first time, that Stewart got his original
prestige from Rio Tinto, a mine which was working cen-
turies before he was born, and that when he arrived at
Broken Hill its fate and fortune were already known;
the Chillagoe mines were the first big undeveloped pro-
perties he had handled. These mines, which he said con-
tained over a million pounds worth of copper, a million
ounces of silver, many thousands of ounces of gold, besides
many thousands of tons of lead (all this in sight) had been
sold for a song by John Moffat, the pioneer of tin-mining
in N.S.W. and Queensland, and no fool in copper-mining
(for he had a smelter at Chillagoe 10 years ago).
But this great man Stewart had other great
mining men behind him, some of whom parted
with their shares at big prices and now ask-
the public to find another £lOO,OOO by tlie issue of that
number of shares, which are to carry a 10 per cent,
preferential dividend. How much is likely to be left to
pay divs. on ordinary shares when the interest on about
half-a-million in debentures is met and this 10 per cent, is
paid ? They are down to 45., and under these circum-
stances would be very dear at 4d. These groups of mines
were condemned some 10 years ago when they were
offered to the Wallaroo and Moonta Co. They were con-
demned again three years ago by the same man, whom
the public refused to believe, fascinated as it was by the
magnificence of Stewart (alleged salary, £lO,OOO a year)
and the capitalistic influence of the directors. The ending
is truly ignominious.
Tartana, Limited, is the old North Chillagoe
under a new name, and the Chillagoe’s adversity has been
taken as this show’s opportunity. Nominal capital,
£126,000, divided into 504,000 shares of ss. each, of which
344,000 are to be issued as fully-paid, and 100,000 are con-
tributing. Subscribers for the 120,000 contributing
shares now offered will receive as a bonus one paid-up
share on each contributing share when fully paid to 55.,
or a bonus of 120,000 shares in all. As the Chillagoe
mines have not enough ore developed to keep the nest of
six furnaces going, the Tartana hopes to be able to supply
ore to keep two Chillagoe furnaces in full work, and to
be able to make better terms with that co. than if
they smelted themselves. As 100 tons of the Tartana
silicious ore will flux 200 tons of Mt. Garnet basic ore, ’tis
said the Mt. Garnet people will smelt 100 tons a day for
the actual cost of treatment. That is the representation,
anyhow. The promoters sanguinely say they are confi-
dent of 100 per cent, annual divs.

Briseis Tin, Tas. : These shares have gone from
Bs. to 28s. (they have fallen somewhat now) in a few
weeks. Of course, it is on account of a “ bear ” squeeze.
No mining stock can rise in Victoria without something
of that kind, and investors have been warned and asked
to remember the effect of the corner in the Duke of York
shares some time back. But in this case, as in that of
Mt. Lyell some years ago, little Tasmania has every
reason to retain and be proud of its morning and evening
prayer —“God bless Bowes Kelly.” For it is to 8.K.,
and B.K. only, that this substantial rise in the
shares is due. He evidently promised the London
management, or some heavy shareholders there,
that he would give the property his personal attention.
This he has done, and has also had the report of two well-
known hydraulic mining men, on the strength of which
he started buying weeks ago at Bs., and knocked off a few
days since at 245. 6d., securing altogether over 25,000
shares ; others followed his example, and it is said that
over 100,000 shares are on their way to Australia from
London. Another man reported favorably on behalf of a
London syndicate, who bought 15,000 shares on the
strength of his report. From 28s. they receded in a few
days to £l, all on account of the collapse in Ohillagoes.
When the effect of Danvers Power’s report was published
in London, shareholders there were prepared to take ss.
less for Briseis stock, which fell to 18s. 9d., and would
have receded further but for the continuance
of Australian buying. Of the mine, it is said that
holes and bores put down in the bottom drift have proved
that it is much higher in tin than that above it, and much
richer than was expected. The tin drift has an average
depth of about 100 ft., runs from 5 to 10 chains in width,
and there is a length of nearly f of a mile in the co.’s
ground. The over-burden averages about 50ft., and,
except in a few isolated places, can easily be removed by
water. For some time past the tin production has been
from 20 to 25 tons per month, and this at a
profit of about £l2OO. In the course of three
or four months the big race to be brought in
to sluice the bottom drift will be completed, when
it is said the tin production will be largely increased each
month until it is brought up to a monthly production of
over 200 tons. Undoubtedly it is the biggest tin-mine
remaining in Tasmania, the glory of Mt. Bischoff having
faded. If there is a fly in the ointment it will probably
be found when an attempt is made to remove in a day
more drift and overburden than is now taken away in a
week. If it is established that the water is ample to
carry away daily some tens of thousands of cubic yards
of drift without any silting—there would be no reason
to doubt the permanent greatness of the Briseis.
Writer is told by a man who ought to know
his business that the average value of the tin drift
is certainly £1 per cubic yard, that it can be treated, the
overburden removed, and all machinery and manage-
ment expenses defrayed by 2s. 6d. ; that metallic tin can be
produced at under £2O per ton, and that the existence of
some millions of tons of tin-drift has been proved. Capital,
£600,000 in that number of shares. Working capital
exhausted some months ago, and indebtedness is now
being wiped off by the substantial profit made on a small
production.

The Tasmanian anti-coupon law debars “any
trader issuing any stamp, wrapper, or other device which
entitles the recipient to obtain goods or money from any
trader.” This not only blocks the coupon man ; it makes
tea coupons and distillers’ “£1 orders ” illegal. But, so
far, neither the late James Service’s tea co., nor the pro-
prietors of “ Dingo Gin ” have been prosecuted, though
both continue their “ evil ” practices in the Land of
Sleep-a-Lot.

A story of Sussex-street, Sydney. Copy of letter
neatly framed and hung in the best parlor of a N.S.W.
fowl-raiser:
“ Sydney, 1901.
“ Dear Mr. B——. —l have to acknowledge the safe
arrival of the crate of ducks as advised. The birds were
all in splendid order. Herewith cheque £3 ss. in payment.
“ Yours truly,
* “ Blank’Blank.”
The writer of the letter, a Sydney business man, who had
co-operated with some suburban friends to purchase a
crate of table poultry, some time afterwards paid a visit
to the consignor, and, seeing his letter in a frame of
honor, wanted to know why and wherefore. The fowl-
farmer explained that he had been sending poultry to
Sussex-street dealers for 25 years, and had never before,
in all that time, received an account-sale wherein deduc-
tions had not been made for casualties. Items like “1£
pairs missing,” “ 6 ducks dead,” “ 8 chickens smothered,”
figured among the deductions for commission, etc., as the
regular thing. The Sydney man was astonished and not
a little chagrined at the revelation.; “ You don’t look too‘
pleased,” observed the dock-farmer. “ Well, no,” was the
response ; “ you see, had I known the custom I might so
easily have conformed with it.”

“Canip” to “W.J.M.” re Callide railway :—■
“ Not one inch of the permanent or working survey has
been done towards Callide Coal Property, nor is the trial
survey to that property yet fully completed. Any esti-
mate of cost based on trial survey must only be approxi-
mate ; and as for “ W.J.M.’s” estimate of £2200 per mile'
for first section, well, unless grades are to be excessively
steep, and the line to be a mere tram, such estimate is
ridiculous. The 3ft. 6in. gauge lines ever con-
structed by Q. Govt, were those over western rolling
downs, and they ran into over £2OOO per mile. Good
working lines, 3ft-. Oin. gauge, constructed in Q. over
similar country to that intervening ’twixt Callide pro-
perty and seaboard, have invariably cost Q. Govt., on an
average, about £5OOO per mile. Facilities of Gladstone
harbor and Geologist Band’s report are beside the ques-
tion—the C. Co. has .got to get its coal to port, and, if a
tunnel is not gone in for, an additional five or six miles of
carriage, construction, and maintenance have to be con-
sidered. Seventy miles of railway over indifferent country
hasneveryet been constructed in Australia under about two
years; and, finally, W.J.M. has quite ignored the fact of re-
cently-discovered Port Clinton coalfield within hearing of
• the Pacific’s roar; three bore-holes, approximately two miles
apart, and wonderfully good seams struck in each at a
moderate depth ; moreover, a steam trial at sea has proved
the quality of coal to be undeniable. These are irre-
futable facts worthy of note by intending coal specula-
tors. Port Clinton, or Broadsound, also lies on the great
eastern highway. Gladstone district is. undoubtedly a
rich mineral one, but that, especially in the coalline,
does not remove disadvantages and powerful competitors.

The petition of 976 Broken Hill workers in
favor of the amendment of the Truck Act to allow the
co’s to deduct weekly contributions to the local hospital
from their wages, and pay the sum direct to the institu-
tion, should not be allowed to influence Parliament to
give any and every employer power to make a like deduc-
tion for any other hospital or benefit-fund. What is
needed is a short clause by which an employer, at the
written request of a majority of his workers, could get a
permit to deduct not more than, say, Is. per week to be
paid to a recognised public hospital, and not to any
benefit or accident fund run by the firm. Once the big
employer can get a benefit or accident fund, on the lines
of the benefit fund started by the Maoriland U.S.S. Co.,
which Hori Grey denounced and smashed up in M.L.
Parliament as an attempt to interfere with the liberty of
the subject, that employer begins to acquire a tyrannous
power.
The Presbyterian Assembly of Vic. is
seriously considering the question of excluding
reporters from its happy gatherings, where violent'
scenes are of such frequent occurrence that the
reports give the public the idea of a “harmony”
gathering at a low-class pub. It is one of the
humorous side-lights on the Presbyterian clerical
character that the suggestion is to suppress, nob
the scenes, but the reports thereof.
Certain Ballarat (Vic.) sky T pilots lately
assembled in solemn confabulation to discuss ways
and means of keeping the übiquitous small boy
and girl off the streets at night, and all the com-
bined wisdom of the dozen or so church-leaders
was able to suggest was the establishment of the
“curfew.” It may be casually suggested that
Herod the Great found out a workable method of
keeping the small boy off the streets at a late hour.
Nothing more successful has been devised since
his day.
' From a daily paper :
During the running of the Manly steamer Kuringai
between Manly and Sydney, last evening, her steering
gear carried away, practically disabling the vessel. After
some delay her passengers were safely landed at the
wharf, where temporary repairs were effected to the
steamer. Later, she was taken to the company’s works,
Neutral Bay, for more thorough treatment.
The Bulletin is quite tired of this sort of thing,
which, in the lorg run, will lead to an inexpress-
ibly-hideous calamity. The Kuringai is licensed
to carry 1000 persons or so, and sometimes she
does carry quite that number.

The modesty of S’John Madden exceeds
even his volubility. Speaking from the fullness
of his stummick at the Melbourne Mayoral
banquet, The Bulletin’s valuable subject admitted
some personal misgivings as to whether he didn’t
attend too many Victorian “functions,” and spout
to excess every time. He said “he recognised
how near he was to the illustrious animal in Scrip-
ture which goeth up and down the world.” Ye
gods ! As he gazes into the mirror and parts the
remnants of his back-hair by the aid of a hand-
glass, Madden revels in his fancied resemblance
to a Roaring Lion. Happy Waxellency 1
THE ONLY POSSIBLE CONCLUSION.
MABEL (to Ethel, in the background): “ How
awfully rich she must be !”
An intensely loyal inebriate made things
very unpleasant for the consort of a State
Governor at a certain recent race-meeting. He
was a tall, superior sort of inebriate, one of those
astonishing individuals who can become horribly
drugged without destroying the smug wonder of
his lofty Paris hat or perverting a single fold of a
frock-coat than which there is no finer worn by
any shopwalker of them all. Seeing Her Excel-
lency moving through a crowd, the loyal drunk
was suddenly seized with the fervor of a latter-
day Raleigh, and, backing through the amazed
people before her, he cleared them to the right
an> j left, crying with official sternness: “Stand
aside, please! Stand aside, there! Make way
for Her Excellency ! ” Then the chivalrous shop-
man (he is condemned on his garb) completed the
tableau by stooping and hastily sweeping the scat-
tered betting tickets from the grass of the vice-
regal right-of-way. But at this point Her Ex.
tied in the other direction, blushing quite like an
ordinary person.
* * * •
Some of the Sydney churches, in connec-
tion with a “united mission,” have been allowed
to erect tents in Hyde and Belmore Parks,
Sydney. In permitting this, the Government
has seriously departed from the rule—no State
subsidies to sects. Some little time ago it was
rightly decided that no more grants of land for
churches or other religious buildings should be
made, on the principle that, there being no State
church, no particular denomination should be
specially recognised. A church has no more right
t? i-i c-f ”hi Hyde Park than a circus lias.
But the See Government seems to have definitely
embarked on a policy of conciliating and sub-
sidising various sects—Roman Catholic and Pro-
testant. It will find it in tho end a dangerous
system.
* * # *
Members of N.S.W. Parliament should,
before adjourning for Christmas holidays, place
on record their objection to Parliamentary jun-
ketings. Already invitations, oral and otherwise,
are going about for a gorgeous razzle-dazzle under
Government at Christmas time. If Ministers like
to “stand Sam” out of their own pockets, no one
can complain; but steamers, launches, wines,
cigars, and the all-round- spree will, no doubt, be
dumped down on the back of the common tax-
payer, as usual. The House, before breaking up,
should at least fix a sum for the Ministerial Xmas
drunk.
* * * •
Apropos the request of Hon. Rupert
Carington (commanding something or other in
S’Africa) that N.S.W. Govt, will pay passages for
another 150 bushmen, ss. a-day is guaranteed
by the Imperial Govt., which won’t pay its own
Yeomanry ! As for the passage-money, our own
bill is not receipted yet. Someone ought to tell
Rupert that the S’African spasm is past; but we
can let him have a lot of surplus Kanakas cheap.
* * * B
Consider the marvellous ways of Queens-
land’s Continuous Government. It won’t allow a
horse-gamble advt. to appear in the papers, yet,
in Charters Towers, the pubs are open all day
Sunday, special Sunday trains are run for foot-
ball and cricket matches, and much-bedecked Jap
damsels are allowed openly to “solicit.”

A very young man asks us to get him a
billet as rhyming Police Courter on one of the
very dull dailies. Herewith a sample of his art,
tried on a Hekald par.:—•
At the Water Police Court, before Mr. F. S. Isaacs,
S.M., Minnie Bell, alias Abdul, alias Rose, 57, a married
woman, was charged with stealing two white sheets, the
property of Charles Mahamed. ■ Accused was fined £3, or
in default one month’s imprisonment. At the same Court
Hector Gillies, 73, a laborer, was charged with stealing 10
chaff bags of the value Of 25., the property of Messrs.
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co., Limited. He was sentenced
to one month’s imprisonment with hard labor.
Roland and Oliver at Newcastle (N.S.W.).
At last meeting of the Hospital Committee a
letter was read from the local Ministers’ Associa-
tion protesting against the committee accepting, in
aid of the hospital funds, any moneys raised by
horse-racing, “as such a practice is offensive to
our principles,” etc. The communication was re-
ceived. Immediately afterwards the Hospital
House Committee’s report was read. The report
stated that grave complaints exist respecting the
irregularity of religious services in the institution,
“the ministers sometimes neglecting the place for
several weeks.” Then ordinary business proceeded.
By the way, the last race-meeting in aid of New-
castle Hospital realised £450, while the combined
annual donations of the Newcastle church congre-
gations do not exceed £lO !

THE SPECTACLED MAN: “George Reid mag be an orator, but I can't stand his reiteration."
THE DISCIPLE (doubtfully): “ Yes; perhaps so. What did you say you couldn’t stand?"
THE S.M. : “I say I can’t stand his tendency to reiterate.”
THE DISCIPLE (timidly): What s that?" The S.M.: “ To repeat, sir, to repeat”
THE DISCIPLE (hastily): “Never mind repeating it; I understand. That's all right. Go on.”
THE S.M. (with patient emphasis): “I say a thing, and then I say it again.”
THE DISCIPLE (in a bewildered way); “I know you do. But what do you want to say it
twice for?” (Then the train went into the tunnel.)

* * * *
Now alleged that Colonel Tom Price is an
applicant for the billet of Chief Commissioner of
Vic. Police, but The Bulletin can’t believe that
rumor. The very name of Price is associated with
oppressive acts of authority in the old days. A
group of figures at Melb. Waxworks is a reminder
that Tom Price’s iron-nerved father fell a victim
to some convicts, who thought it better to be
hanged from a beam than quartered under his
tyranny. Every policeman knows the story of
how Price pere was singled out for vengeance
and done to death whilst other convicts
caught hold of his warder companions, say-
ing, “We don’t want to hurt you.” The sins of
the fathers, in some cases, disqualify the children
for particular billets, and this is one of those cases.
Besides, Col. Tom Price, a dozen years ago, was
popularly known in Melbourne as “ Fire-low-and-
jay-’em-out! ” on account of his instructions to
the boy-swashbucklers who were called out to awe
the working man. Thomas is personally an
honorable man and a generous one, but he is an
impossible Chief-Com. of Vic. Police. He’s the
boss man of the Mounted Rifles, and he has a
notoriously fine command of langwidge—let him
rest on his honors.
* ♦ * *
According to N.S. W. prison authorities, no
less than 78 more or less mad prisoners were sent
to them last year, besides another 46 who showed
symptoms of insanity within a month. There are
such records as these:—“No. 21, breaking and
entering, 21 years’ hard labor, delusive (on admis-
sion), previously insane”; “No. 32, shooting
with intent, seven years’ servitude, delusive (on ad-
mission), suffering from melancholia, previously
insane ” ; “ No. 31, assault and robbery, five years’
servitude, epileptic on admission, insane (epileptic)
in two weeks five days ; not recovered.” And there
is the sad ca.se of a foreigner, sentenced to five
months for being on premises with intent to commit
a felony, found on admission to be suffering from
general paralysis of the insane, and remanded to an
asylum to die.

Telegraphed from Scone, N.S.W., to
Sydney Daily Telegraph :
A monument to the memory of the late Lieut. Ebsworth,
Belltrees Troop of the First Australian Horse, has been
erected in the Church of England grounds. The monu-
ment is inscribed : “In memory of Lieut. Ebsworth,”
&c. “ Erected by his friends and comrades. The follow-
ing members of the Belltrees Troop of Australian Horse
were also engaged in South Africa : ” (names given).
There must surely be some mistake here ! The
Bulletin refuses to believe that the last sentence
is. inscribed on the monument, and that “ the
friends and comrades” of the dead man would
thus paste their posters on his tomb, to advertise
their share in S’African exploits.
* * * *
It is significant that the lec. ntly- published
annual report of the Victorian Military Com-
mandant makes no reference whate/er to the
newly-formed and particularly naice Melbourne
cavalry corps, which was created for the purpose
of permitting bank-clerks to swagger about in
gold-and-blue uniforms. It is an open secret that
all the Australian cavalry regiments are to be
converted into mounted rifles, though the authori-
ties will “make it aisy for the poor baste” by
permitting them to retain the name of cavalry
and to turn-out with their obsolete weapons at
public shivoos. The Melbourne cavalry reaches
unplumbed depths of fatuity in having a strip of
chain-mail on each shoulder—presumably to thwart
the downward stroke of a weapon which all
nations are about to abandon.
* * *' •
Some scaremongers are hinting that pneu-
monia, strangely prevalent now throughout
Eastern Australia, is “the plague” in another
form. Though there is one well-defined form of
plague known as lung plague, which closely
resembles septic pneumonia in some of its symp-
toms, there is no reason at all, save the unreason
of fear, for this scare rumor. But, undoubtedlv,
the pneumonia outbreak demands serious con-
sideration. Old-fashioned ideas set down this
deadly disease as simply an aggravated cold : as a
matter of fact, it is just as markedly a “filth”
disease as typhoid, and calls for the same anti-
septic precautions. That it is to a great extent
infectious or contagious seems to be also a fact,
and, therefore, it calls for segregation measures
which are not always adopted. The sufferer
should be treated, so far as precautions against
contact go, with even more care than a typhoid
patient, and a case of pneumonia in a neighbor-
hood should be taken as a hint to look to drains,
sources of milk supply, and other possible means
of infection.
* * * *
Bernard Shaw says that the proper place
to study all descriptions of love is in the police-
court. As effective a bit of pathos as has been
recorded in Australia for years occurred the other
day at Coburg (Vic.) court, when O’Connor, high-
wayman and Pentridge escapee, was charged.
His little girl, born after he was sent to gaol seven
years ago, was waiting for him after the hearing
of the charge, and, as he was removed to gaol to
await his trial, he met his own child for the first
time.
* » * *
A Bill is shortly to be introduced in Vic.-.
State Parliament to provide for the establish-
ment of a compulsory superannuation fund for
State-school teachers, on the lines of the S.A.
system. Annual subscriptions—for males, £l6„
£l2, £B, and £4 ; and for females, £l2, £9, £<\
and £3. In 38 years a male teacher, at the
highest rate, will have contributed £6OB, and will
receive in return a yearly allowance of £113175. 6d.
As this is much more expensive than the terms of
an ordinary insurance policy, the idea is not likely
to be hailed with much delight by married school-
teachers, who, as a body, find it hard to live on
their meagre salaries.
THE LORD AND HIS MAKER.
My tailor is iny master,
I cheerfully confess;
Like dummies shaped in plaster,
I’m governed by my dress.
Yet if, perchance, my hatter
Attempt to boss the show—
That’s quite another matter :
I d n his eyes, and go.
The fashion of my “ topper ”
Black, monumental pile—
Is just what 1 think proper
In cumbrous “ topper ” style.
But touching body raiment—
My coat, and pants, and vest,
I merely do the payment—
The tailor does the rest.
I’m subject to his fancies—
He cuts me as he will ;
My value he enhances
According to his skill.
Ye men of lower station,
Look up ! Be not afraid I
And note with admiration
Lord Hopetoun, tailor-made.
Minnie Rose-citro-Abdul greets,
Sweet and twenty-seven,, she says,
Went to bed and stole the sheets—
“ Sixty bob or thirty days !”
Hector Gillies, man of woes
Seventy-three—(O youth of mine !)
Stole some sacks for his bed-clothes—
“ Thirty days without a fine !”
Poverty’s an awful sin ;
Sex equality is grand;
Impotent the cynic’s grin
In this just and happy land.
Shaking bed-clothes—heinous crime I
But for women—just decree 1 —
Gold is antidote to grime
In an age of chivalry 1
Age is young, and youth is old,
Rose and Hector, both must go—
Rose, above, by grace of gold
Poor old Hector goes below.

The recently-distributed Victorian State
School Teachers Bill does not propose much im-
provement in salaries of the under-paid juniors.
Pupil teachers, who have to go through an expen-
sive course of instruction, are to receive for the
first two years—males, from £3O to £4O per an. ;
females, from £24 to £32 a year. This is not a
living wage. The maximum salary obtainable by
a first-class head teacher is £432. How can good
men be expected to join the Service when the
prize (the all but unobtainable prize) is £432
a year ?
• • • •
Under the Bill to amend Vic. Educa-
tion Act, a teacher cannot rise with his
school, or retain his school if he so desires
at a fixed salary. In one case a country school
was raised by the enthusiasm of a teacher—a first
sub-division man—from an average attendance of
100 to 180. After putting his heart into his work,
gaining the confidence of parents and children
alike, settling his boys in businesses and trades,
&c., &c., this man will be removed (if the Bill
passes) to a lower-grade school, although he is
quite content to stay where his energies are at
present concentrated. The old Act allowed this
option, and surely the amended Act should do the
same.
* • * *
At Tamworth (N.S.W.), t’other day, a
jury complained to the Judge that their seats were
“hard.” This scribe was present years ago in
the “New” Supreme Court, Melbourne (which
has been old these 40 years), when the late C. J.
Stawell talked from the bench to young J. L.
Purves about how jurors ought to be treated in
the Newer Law Courts about to be built in YVest
Melbourne. “A juryman,” said His Honor,
“ ought to have room to stretch his legs and have
a soft seat, otherwise he can’t think well.” “ And
there’s another thing, your Honor,” says Purves,
“ he oughtn’t to have this cocoa-nut matting under
his feet. I myself never go home alone from this
court.”

“S. Wrinkler ” : My scheme for a con-
sumptives’ sanatorium is to have a moveable one
in the dry country : the Urana to Albury Plains
for choice. Live in tents, which can be made
quite snug, and shift every six months. The old
ground to be burned-off, as with anthrax pad-
docks. lam not barracking for the district, be-
cause it’s a land of large estates ; but many have
come there to die —and have lived.
A “ Mere Male ” :
One of the Sydney dailies, to brighten up reports of the
tariff controversy, is publishing a recriminatory corres-
pondence between “an Englishwoman,” who complains
of the hoggish rudeness of Australian men, and various
heated champions of the local reputation for politeness.
Whatever the merits of the controversy, woman must
make up her mind shortly to do without something. At
present she wants the vote, and the right to sit in Parlia-
ment ; and to be a lawyer and a doctor and a drains-
inspector; to oust the clerk out of his job by black-
legging at half wages (Heaven knows the gentle
employer is ready enough to help her in this latter) ; to
rush any free refreshment that is going and leave the
crumbs only to the men ; and withal to be given the best
seat in the tram. Man is a patient animal, and while the
whisky-and-soda compartment of life was left to him
made the best of things. But woman now wants the
front seat there also, and the day of the revolting man
rapidly approaches.
• • * •
“Equality ” : If there is to be any anti-
spitting legislation, in the interests of public
health, let us hope it will apply to both sexes.
The lady-expectorator is an almost ordinary angel
nowadays. Of all the crowd waiting on the
Melbourne railway platform for a race-train last
Thursday, the only person whom I spotted in the
spitting-act was a stylishly-dressed female. She
turned her slender back to her cavalier, hung her
head, pressed her trimmings close to her with her
left hand to keep them “out of the wet,” and
expectorated like a bloke.
* • • •
“T.D.” : Re the “ Beardies,” or Christian
Israelites, I have never seen one, male or female,
who might not be called good-looking. All are
regular-featured—a fact that accentuates their
placid, cow-like demeanour. Cause and effect,
probably, but which is which ? Also, every male
Beardy has hair of a delicate light brown. Dye—
or what?

At Balmain, Sydney, a Presbyterian
clergyman has broken with his congregation on
the question of “ amusements,” and has resigned,
to go ’Ome to a better and a gloomier land. The
old Presbyterian idea, which looked upon a “Kist
of whustles” as an invention of the devil, and
anything but four whitewashed walls as a place
of abomination, does not thrive in Australia,
where Presbyterianism falls away from grace into
harbor picnics, comic sermons, and Pleasant
Sunday Afternoons. Buckle tells that the Scotch
congregations of the early 19th century looked for
a pastor who could “weep and howl ” well at the
call of Grace. The Australian churches want
men who can crack a joke in the pulpit, and play
Jolly Miller down the harbor. And so the
Faithful Remnant has to go ’Ome to find a fitting
gloom to be saved in.

Talk about Govt, sweating ! An operator
in Adelaide Telegraph Office had to take down in
longhand the whole of the tariff as it was being
wired from Melbourne, besides part of Turner’s
speech. All this without a pause, and no pay for
overtime.
“ Foreign Attachment ”
is a curious relic of ancient
London city law in force, in
parts of Australia, now be-
fore the Vic. courts. It i 3
only available when a person
owing debts and having pro-
perty in, say Victoria, has
flitted, or is reasonably sus-
pected of contemplating a >
hitting to Guam or its hin-
terland. On proof of this,
the writ puts an embargo on
the goods of the debtor, and
the creditor leisurely pays
himself out of the proceeds,
without the trouble of haul-
ing the tourist from beyond
the seas. Foreign attachment
was originally a custom of
the Lord Mayor’s court of
London, and is only in force
in Australia where it has been
expressly enacted by the local
legislatures.
The question of licensing
clergymen to marry, which ap-
pears to be now treated as a new
one by N.S.W. politicians, was
really settled by Chief Jus-
tice Stephen nearly 40 years
ago. The Rev. Dr. Bailey’s
congregation in Sydney fell
off until only a few old ladies
remained. The Registrar-
General withdrew his license.
Dr. Bailey applied to the
Court, and Stephen upheld
the Registrar, on the ground .
that a few old ladies meeting on s
a Sunday did not constitute a
congregation.
Sydney tram authorities now give their
drivers a small seat, but forbid the men cushion-
ing them or even sitting on their coats for com-
fort. There does not seem to be any reason for
this except sheer cantankerousness.

Visitors to Australia always have an old
retainer or two unearthed for them, to their
amazing joy and great advertisement. There
was Hopey’s coachman, York’s quarter-master,
and now Hector Macdonald’s nurse.

Harry Morris-Cohen was recently married
in a high-class Sydney suburb. Mr. Leo Cohen
acted as best man. Miss Colleen Keith-Cohen
was present, likewise Kenneth Cohen, Cedric
Cohen, and Colin Cohen. Mention was made of
Norman Cohen, Neville Cohen, George Cohen,
Keith-Cohen, and Cohen. As nothing was said
of John Smith Cohen, Donald M‘Cohen, and
Patrick O’Cohen, the inference is that these rela-
tives, together with Raoul EitzCohenand Griffith-
ap-Cohen, were unavoidably detained. By the
way, as the Hebrews are snapping-up all the
Scotch names (which seems like painting the lily)
when are we to have Mr. Urquhart Cohen and
Mr. Colquhoun Cohen ?
The Salvarmy in Victoria intends to run
its annual October self-denial fad close on the
heels of Hospital Sunday this year. There’s some
grim, unconscious iron’y about the circular in
which ‘ ‘ General ” Booth spurs on his Australian
adherents to starve and stint themselves some
more, to the financial benefit of the House of
Booth:
“ You have, by the grace of God, done wonders. Your
services and sacrifices are known on earth and recorded
in heaven. No one knows them better or appreciates
them more truly than your General.”
No doubt! And the “General” incidentally
brackets himself with God, too !
* * * •
A Victorian 10-year-old boy, when arrested
for placing some ballast on the railway-line
between Yan Yean and Whittlesea, told the
police that his object had been to throw a train off
the line and rob the bodies of the slain passengers.
Deadwood Dick again, most likely.
* • * *
The long arm of coincidence ! While four
men were sticking up Mr. Hayes, a Sydney
butcher, and robbing him of £4O, a butcher at
Brisbane, going home with his Saturday night’s
takings (£25), was stuck up by three men. A
tram came along and frustrated the attempt. In
Hayes’ case the tram came just a minute too
late.

“ Veteran ” : Cabled that the three Vic-
torians, sentenced in S’Africa for “ inciting, to
mutiny,” were released “because they were tried
under the wrong section of the Army Act.”
Should think so ! Mutiny is a combination to
actively and forcibly resist authority, not to pas-
sively-disobey orders, which was all that Steele
and his fellows proposed to do. .They were con-
templating refusal to go out again with Beatson,
who seems to resemble his father—a well-known
army surgeon of 40 years ago—in his habit of
plain-speaking. By the way, in Capt. Spilsbury s
THE COIN OF THE GODLY .
( Monday , 10.30 a.m.) Bung, junior: “Say,
parson! Dad says can you let him have
tray-bits for a sov. School’s in with the
poker-machine .”
MS. “Diary of the Siege of Gibraltar” there is a
story showing how Eliott dealt with such cases as
Steele’s: “October 3, 1779. One 58th man was
overheard saying, ‘lf the Spaniards came, d——n
him that would not join them.’ The Governor
said he must be mad, ordered his head to be
shaved, to be blistered, bled, sent to the provost
on bread and water, wear a strait waistcoat, and
be prayed-for in church.” Of course, this man’s
offence was far worse than Steele’s —being a direct
incitement to join the enemy, and Eliott was a
merciful man to impute it to madness. Much
more merciful than the contingent officers who
tried Steele and the others, or the stupid Ket-
ehener, who “ commuted ” the sentence.

proper thing to.do with Original Sin.
CREDO.
Life is so brief !
Drain, then, its cup of pleasure to the lees ;
Take, then, its gifts or leave them, as you please:
Life is so brief.
Love grows so cold !
Take, then, its kisses when on hot lips prest;
Take all of Love’s most passionate—its best:
Love grows so cold !
Night is so drear !
Take, then, thy comfort in the bright noon sun ;
Leave care and sorrow till the day be done :
Night is so drear !
Death comes so soon !
Take, then, of Life all that Life has to give,
Live with thy heart and brain and senses—Aye .*
Death comes so soon !
N.S.W.
NELLIE M. FIELD.
NOVEMBER.
[Foe The Bulletin.]
Day by day among the hills
All our flowers are dying ;
Thinner Springtime’s wine distils
Day by day. Among the hills
I, forlorn and pale with ills,
Hear my Youth go crying,
Day by day among the hills,
“ All our flowers are dying.”

A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, November 21, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Young singers’ benefit concerts, amateur per-
formances; the Scotsman with the Greek name,
and a case of plague are troubling our pool, and
just keeping it from stagnating.
Mayoress Graham’s last “At Home” at the
Town Hall was small, not more than six or eight
visitors being there at one time. Owing to the
presence of several superior persons it was very
conversational, and discussed Freetrade and Pro-
tection, the Conduct of Life, the route (in Europe)
likely' to be padded by the Mayoress’s mother,
and a lady who speaks five languages. Mrs.
Prof. Anderson and a Melbourne intruder
differed on the subject of Foundling Homes, but
were warmly reconciled over the Adelaide School
of Design.
Talking of Foundling Homes, the Cardinal and
the Archbishop went up to the Home at Waitara
the other day to open a garden fete (the Cardinal
pronounces it like the plural of “foot”) in aid
of the funds of the institution. The Sisters have
acquired some more land, and when finances permit
will start a laundry, so that they may keep on the
premises and give work to the mothers of such of
the infants as require mother’s nursing. There are
98 little ones in the Home at present. Eric, the
“ house-father ” —he was the first foundling taken
in—domineered over the other ancients, and sang
“Pop goes the weasel,” and received tbe prelates
with his gaze fixed on the big gold crosses on the
big, sacerdotal chests. The day was a fierce one,
with a withering northerly wind. Women who
had railed up from town to the opening had
tongues like strips of stringy-bark, from thirst, by
the time they made the return journey. On
Waitara platform a heretic, who had bought a
pair of perfectly superfluous woollen gloves and a
pin-cushion stuffed with pea-nuts at the sale of
work, screwed her eye to a keyhole of the ladies’
waiting-room, and, beholding the water-bottle
and glass, she first quoted Private Mulvaney on
thirst, and then began to cry. For the door was
locked. I don’t want to be a catechism, but I
would like to ask the railway authorities why that
door is kept locked? Perhaps foundlings have
been left there!
Miss Lillian Spiers was entertained at an “ At
Home” by her pupils in the Queen’s Hall last
Friday, and presented with a gold cable bangle.
A crowd of very nice, white-froeked, fair-haired
maidens played and sang, and one damsel, with a
singular likeness to Reging, Nagel, gave promise of
a fine contralto.
Major-General Hector Macdonald can’t be
sneered at for exploiting his uniform—probably he
hasn’t brought one with him. At the Sydney
citizens’ dinner to him he wore the Y.O. and a
row of medals on his black coat. His voice is not
as strong as one would expect from his depth of
chest, but is hard and penetrating. As he walked
through the two lines of Highlanders to his seat
the gallery (of women) noted that his tartan tie
was unmoored at the back and rucked up over the
collar, and the gallery sighed at the ill that may
happen to a bachelor. All the speeches were
heavily charged with references to the “ British
Empire,” and, from a gallery point of view, the
best thing of the evening was the playing of
exquisite old Scotch airs by De Groen’s band.
“Australia’s great contralto” and the Scotch
Hector brought a splendid audience to Sydney
Town Hall on Saturday night—a night of storm
and rain and smothered moonlight. Miss Alice
Hollander (emphasise the second syllable, please)
made her professional bow to an audience sharp-
set by Other-State criticisms, all as high as the
meridian itself. Miss Alice has a beautiful voice,
but the “greatness” has been thrust upon her.
She may achieve “greatness” by the time her
course of European study is over. At present, it
would be a little previous to say more than that
she is a singer of much promise. Her opening
number was the difficult and beautiful “ Lascia
ch’io Pianga,” which she managed delightfully,
and without difficulty. Tosti’s “ Good-Bye,”
the only approach to a popular song on
the programme, was given as an encore; and
Mascheroni’s “ Ave Maria” was most warmly
received. Mr. Bethune, the young contralto’s
trainer, made his first and last platform appear-
ance as a singer with a high, worn, though good-
quality baritone. “Angels Guard Thee” and
“ Where’er You Walk” were his numbers. Mr.
Bethune has a most musicianly style and a soul-
ful, gazing-at-the-mountain-tops manner, which
Miss Alice has caught to some extent. He is
also interesting for the reason that he is a cousin
of Lord Hopetoun’s.
Miss Bryant, at the organ, made excellent har-
mony with a “Faust” fantasia. Miss Hilda
Lane brought a pretty, school-girl presence and a
promising soprano to bear upon the audience.
The other attraction, warrior Mac., sat low,
in a high - backed chair, with the Dar-
leys, Grahams, Sees, and Wises around
him. If he had been placed a few rows
back he would have had a view of some fearsome
dressing. One woman wore a grass-cloth cape and
a Jubjub bird in her hair—at least, I think it was
that kind of bird. And the lady on whom it
roosted looked like a Bandersnatch. By the way,
Adelaide’s Attorney-General is going to bring in
a Bill on the lines of the Texas Act, making it
penal to wear the plumage of birds. At a woman’s
club I once heard a debate on the immorality of
plumage-wearing. The opener spoke strongly,'
and mentioned that the day previous she had taken
all the feathers out of all her hats. Next speaker
also anathematised the fashion, hut when the dis-
cussion was over she sidled up to the opener,
nudged her gently, and whispered, “Give me
those feathers, will you ? ”

0n ,sides, but glaring instances such

common. . I have seen women standing in trams

every day I see women tilt men’s hats and all but

Women s discourtesy to women is often the result
