Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
‘ The matinee hat was never so terrible a
nuisance as at the crowded show at Her Majesty’s
on r lhursday. Two up-standing, well-upholstered
women nearly wept when, after repeated adjura-
tions and anathemas from their neighbors, the
usher ordered the removal of their towering head-
pieces. The work of removal melted every mem-
ber of the hair-pin sex. The veils, reefed in at
twenty places, had to be tenderly removed, for
rough treatment threatened disaster to the toupee.
Then the hat skewers had to be withdrawn, also
tenderly. Then came the most critical and deli-
cate work of all—the raising of the chiffon lid.
Very slowly, softly, adroitly, was this accom-
plished, and the two.tortured souls, terrified at
the thought of criticism of their ruffled straw-
thatched roofs, sat sick, sorry, and desperate
through the rest of the show.
“ In the big audience that filled circle and front
stalls, many notables were scattered. It agreed
with the general order of things theatrical that the
‘cheaper’ sort of persons were posted in the
front, whilst Toorak curled its lips at the bloated
back-views of Opulence and Push from the darker
recesses of the five-shilling parts. Mayoress
Gillott, all in grey, was, however, one of the
official Melbournites in a prominent seat. Lady
Baker, with some girls, provided another fashion-
able oasis in the desert of the front. Miss Muriel
Williams sat with the President’s wife, and her
light puff-paste head-gear was one of the few
allowed to remain on active service. Most of the
millinery was put by till the final curtain-drop.
Miss Beatrice Smith was a fascinating figure in
the circle, although her long green coat and
plateau hat were tame enough. Mr. Oldham, of
Toorak, who always makes a splash at afternoon
affairs, spread himself over half of the manager’s
box—a male friend occupied the rest. Mr.
Oldham is the lucky brother of the late Mrs. Silas
Harding, whose death by a buggy accident set
this relative on the path of diamonds and glory.
“ The brightest and most tinselled gown sported
through all the varying scenes of the Matinee
programme was that of Miss Lizzie Collins, the
dark dazzler of Rickards’ present show. This was
a hyacinth-blue turnout, veiled in a polonaise of
point lace, and having apricot touches wherever
relief was needed. Mrs. Brough’s neat claret-
colored garb showed her ever-green figure to per-
fection, but Miss Vaughan spread forth her ample
form in a much richer garb of Ketchener (i.e.,
blood-red) satin.
“Light-heeled Maud Chetwynd (Mrs. What’s-
her-name) fussed round in lilac and lace, escorted
tty tenor Ernest Mozar. Mrs. Norman Menzies
(Dorothy Vane) in pale-pink flowered-brocade,
trimmed with black lace and flat hat, with girl
friend in tow, looked a gentle little thing, perhaps
thinking of a lost convent career. All the naicest
helped to swell the audience. Mrs. Lawson,
Rupert Clarke’s cousin-in-law, stood in the door-
way airing the fashionable toilette exaggerations
of the moment —solid dark hair over her ears,
enormous earrings, flat hat trimmed with bright
blue —the sort of thing that only a chiselled
Madonna face can stand without looking like a
Kaffir’s. Mrs. Willie Robb, interested in things
theatrical, as of old, looked fragile and interesting
in pale-blue and black. Miss Fenner, Mrs. Percy
Ohirnside’s sister, is one of the miniature
blondes. She combs her fair hair off her smooth,
white forehead, and makes one think that ears and
temples have their spectacular uses after all.
“On the stage, Carrie Moore made a showy
plaintiff in ‘ Trial by Jury.’ She sported
decollete bridal wear, the diamond pendant, and
carried on as if her own rumored real-life breach-
of-promise suit caused her no worry.
“Little Senator Zeal, who has always been
sneered at by his sex for his stolid deafness to the
songs of the sirens, earned the horror of all official
female clerks by his remarks in the Senate the
other night. He declared that many of these
Government toilers would do better as domestic
servants. The remark evoked looks of withering
scorn from some of the clerks referred to, who
were seated in the gallery, and many of the mem-
bers were warmed into shaking their heads
ominously at the uncompromising speaker. Mr.
Zeal is turning acrimonious. ' Diplomatist Jen-
kins is no longer at his side to sugar-coat his pills,
or pluck the thorns from the flowers of his speech.
“Politician Conroy, of the House of Reps., sat
with a feminine flock in one of the boxes at
1 Faust,’ on Saturday night. All were in uncon-
ventional attire ; and the champion interjector of
the Opposition' wore his forelock as ruffled as
ever, and his tweeds right rakishly. In the oppo-
site bo-x was Mrs. F. McEvoy with a group of
plumy friends—a pretty party. Round the
circle were the prevailing majority of the
tribes of Israel, and a majority of two
social twinklers. The two Madden girls, with
Miss Muriel Williams, sat with three men.
Miss Williams in shell-pink, with her voluminous
coils of dark hair fastened on her neck with black
velvet ribbon bows, looked the most sirenesque.
The rose-bud of the Maddens had her French-
polished tresses parted rigidly in the centre, and
dressed in a plain ‘ Good Friday ’ bun on the nape
of her neck—a style that overdid her roundness of
face and feature. Prof. Marshall Hall sat in
dark-tweeds amongst a party of giggling pupils,
and was the most diverting person of the circle
audience. His comments were audible all round
his area, and not flattering to the performers. He
declared that he had seen devils of all countries
and languages, but he had to confess this Italian
devil (Travaglini) was the best-humored he had
ever come across! He laughed immoderately over
this bit of irony. A much less demonstrative and
more engrossed person was Prof. Peterson, who
stood in a draughty corner of the circle and fol-
lowed the performance with unwavering interest.
The Scotch Professor’s likeness to Dr. Macgilli-
cuddy, one of the keenest of local amateur musi-
cians, is so remarkable that it will not be surpris-
ing if Mrs. Peterson herself one day rushes up to
the doctor in the street and addresses him as her
dear hubby.
“When the Gov.-General sampled ‘Second in
Command’ (Sat., Nov. 16), he laughed loudly now
and then, notably when Leslie Victor as a winey,
misquoting cub, remarks that ‘ strong waters are
the shame of a woman but the pride of a man.’
All the same, His Ex. gaped thrice for every once
that he guffawed. With the Hopetoun, came
Govt. House’s permanent petticoat trio—the Gov.-
Generaless, Mrs. Aide Corbett and Surgeon
Philson’s wife, all decidedly decorative but quite
different, one staunch, one sarcastic, one sweet —
affix the labels yourself.
“Seems to me Grace Noble is missed on the
Brough stage. Mrs. Brough makes a nice-enough
Muriel (the ‘ Second in Command ’ heroine), han-
dicapped by ‘middle aged voice-cadences,’ and
other traces of wear-and-tear, and tendency to
grimace, rather than smile, at grief. Miss Chat-
teris has pretty little teeth, and, consequently,
I suppose, a laughing mouth ; but she doesn’t
look like a colleen named Nora. Ray (short
for Rachel, Abbie (alias Abigail), or Sadie (i.e.,
Sara) seem more in her line somehow. As for
Bingham, the unlucky ‘Second in Command,’
Brough makes him engaging—except for his
dead, dark hair ; but in real life he would be
a poor-moubhed muff, always disparaging him-
self, and then hurt because employers take him
at his own estimate.
“Ping-pong, or the hit-and-giggle game, was
played in Parliament House gardens when Lady
Braddon, Lady M‘Millan, and Mrs. Winter-
Cooke entertained the political and fashionable
world with a lawn-party. Of the three dames,
the Tasmanian carried off the palm for smiling
grace and prettiness. The Sydney matron, turned
out in all cream, did her duty pleasantly; and
Mrs. Winter-Cooke was a substantial business-like
representative of this State. Her husband
hovered near, one eye owning a magnifying-glass,
the other doing its work unaided. Braddon,
gaunt, wrinkled, worried, only unbent for the
smartest of the chiffon sex, then he grew gallant,
almost cheerful. Minister Lyne moved round
after his spouse, with his specs balancing
on the lower ridge of his nose—he seemed as if
ready to revise the Hansard proof of his hoarse
rumbles in the House. The Bartons and
M‘Eacharns swopped partners, but kept frater-
nally together. John Forrest had a real good
time among his wife’s youthful following.
“ Among all the flutterers on the lawn, one of
the most decorative was Miss Ruby Madden in a
last-season blue frock—a bright becoming voile—
with gay relief columns of shrimp-pink, and a
saucy toque brimming over with bright colors.
An economical but very-smart turn-out of duck-
egg linen was sported by Miss Margot Gavan
Duffy, one of Sassiety’s really erudite girls. The
Duffys bring their children up in straight, stiff
lines. The correctly-dressed Mrs. Donald
M'Kinnon reared a proud, independent head, and
looked handsome. When finished hand-pumping,
Mistress Braddon butterflied among the guests
airily—always bright and fluttering.
“The most cheerful incident of Oaks-day at
Flemington occurred in front of the gubernatorial
reserve as the race was finishing. A young, well-
dressed, and rather handsome couple were stand-
ing together, anxiously watching the race, the
lady clutching her closed sunshade in her trem-
bling right hand. As Bramba romped in a winner
the female end of the partnership suddenly lost
control of herself, and cried, fiercely: ‘ There !
there ! What did I tell you ? You fool! you
fool ! ’ And, lifting her gingham, the lady smote
her lord and master several times heavily on his
glazed belltopper. One Excellency who was over-
looking this pretty domestic scene gasped aloud,
‘Just look at that! Well, I’m d d!’ he
said.
“ Perched on the stand on Steeplechase-day, one
ruminated on the descent of a stage star from the
social firmament. Last year the Great buzzed
about this charmer, descended from their carpeted
pen to hum their greetings in her ears, and the
most distinguished flutterers battled for a smile
from her. Now, all is changed. Sassiety gives a
grudging nod, turns away, and talks in an impu-
dently loud tone of ‘ the dresses of these actresses.
“ The dress that made the sensation of pup-
week—a pink and lilac ribbon and lace affair of
exquisite design—has brought home heaps of
orders to the wearer who made it. She is a young
Collins-street dress-builder, managing for a swag-
ger artiste in that line. The dress was designed
and put together in odd hours—mostly at night.
The day after the Cup two Sydney dames sent
from Menzies’ for the girl and gave her expensive
orders for reception frocks, and others followed
from various quarters, so that the show-creation
that had cost so much thought, money and time
has brought in a quick profit.
The Carnival brings its usual harvest of engage-
ments, among them that of Mr. Stuart Brunton,
son of Mr. T. Brunton, the flour miller, and
Miss Florrie Strong, only daughter of an
ex-Mayor. The contract was made and
sealed in rather sudden fashion. The wooer
belonged to a well-known bachelor set, wont
to congregate at Menzies’ at regular intervals
to celebrate their fortunate bachelorhood. The
charmer who broke the bachelor barriers is a tall,
simple, unsophisticated maiden, sheltered since
her debut at pa’s Mayoral hop by a crowd of
big brothers.
“ Another Alfred Hospital nurse scores a big
catch at the altar of Hymen this week. This is the
third nurse from that institution this year to land
a goldfish—and the line stretches out to the very
limit of the paying wards.
“The widow of a great racing-man is such a close
friend of a local military widower that speculators
are waiting from day to day to see their names go
up on the board of starters for the Matrimonial
Stakes.
“ A stout, undersized masher, who is somewhat
of a tennis and golf champion, is one of the most
prominent ‘ buoys ’ bobbing on the social sea at
present. At Sunbury and Geelong he is Sir
Oracle, and there the graces do him homage. But
Melbourne is very badly off just now for a line of
attractive cavaliers, guaranteed, like good fast
colors, not to run. As it is, a few weedy remnant
samples have to be made the best of.
“ That handsome Baptist parson who parted
with his flock rather than give up the girl of his
heart, is not the man to go whaling with. The
night of the big hailstorm (Nov. 14), sympathisers
were giving a shivoo in a suburban hall in honor
of his farewelling reverence’s birthday. Thunder
roared, ice-lumps pelted, windows smashed, and
one of the hostesses was laid low with an alarming
seizure, happily not fatal. Melbourne supped full
of horrors that night. A popular long-service
Titles Office official was taken mortally ill on his
roof, which he had climbed to sweep off the ice,
and was only brought down to die in his bed, after
desperate exertions on the part of his neighbors.
“ I have just welcomed back from ’Ome twin
four-year-old acquaintances, garbed in accordance
with British methods of the moment. Boy,
coiffured & la cockatoo, sports a trim crest-curl
and a roundabout row of short ringlets. Thanks
to this revival of a former fashion, photographs of
long-ago urchins look quite modish again.
The infant aforesaid brings from London an as-
sortment of belted tunics of woollen, pique, and
holland respectively, reaching to the knee and
worn over shorter knickers of the same material.
Each tunic has a Tam o’ Shanter and coachman-
caped overcoat of its own stuff, the cloth cap
smartened by a quill, while the holland and pique
millinery is simple enough to unbutton, flatten
out, and go to the wash with companion costumes.
Girl’s side-curls are tied in a little bunch, in
front of each ear, with narrow, white ribbon, and
her go-cart boasts several sets of cushions, match-
ing her various pelisses in color.
Writes a Hobart correspondent:—
“ Gov. Havelock was sworn in at Hobart last
week, but the ridiculousness of making a man
take his oath on top of the steps in front of the
Supreme Court was seen by somebody, and he
was sworn inside, upon the Bench. Most likely
Chief Justice Dodds’s strong common sense
came in this time. The sight of a man aloft
on a platform being manipulated by another man,
before a big howling crowd, used to be too awfully
suggestive of a hanging in the olden time.
“ The nephew of the Indian fighter is very like
the late George Bowen in style, appearance, and
voice, and resembles Chamberlain, too—in his eye-
glass. Jolly-looking, and sprightly withal—for
Havelock looks quite equal to a comic song.
His father was wounded at Waterloo and killed
in India. His uncle, the better-known Henry
Havelock, got a title and a pension, but died before
he heard of them, and his widow was allowed to
reap the benefits.
“ The new Governor’s wife seemed an informal
lady. She was seated on the bench-platform, and
while the swearing was going on, crossed .her
limbs and thrust out a nice little shoe, with silver
buckle. When all was over, she said she wanted
badly to see the wives of her husband’s Ministers,
and therefore Mesdaines Lewis, Collins, Mulcahy,
and Bird were paraded, with Mrs. Speaker Brown
and Mrs. Justice MTntyre bringing up the rear.
“During the late festivities, coral was little
favored by the smartest, but silk Maltese lace,
belts of gold tissue, and ribbon-sashes were
prevalent.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

The Mean Man was sitting on a chair in
the middle of the room, his head sticking out of a
blanket, which was draped over chair and man.
The blanket was his only covering, and the chair-
bottom was a piece of tin ; underneath the tin
burned a kerosene-lamp. The Mean Man was
having a cheap Turkish bath. Presently he began
to look meditative; then a pained expression
crossed his face ; then, muttering incoherently,
he swiftly got up, and for over a week afterwards
“ would rather stand, thank you.” He need not
have been so mean when for 275. 6d. he could have
bought a Peerless Folding Vapour Bath, or a
Cabinet Bath to last a life-time for £5, from
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., Elizabeth-street,
Melbourne.*
The Band Association of N.S. Wales is
holding a Monster Band Contest and Art Union
in Sydney Town Hall on Jan. 25th and 27th next.
For the contest £350 is offered, and there are 185
art union prizes, valued at £775, making a total
of £1125. The art union drawing is to come off
on Feb. 26th. The first prize is a gold cornet
worth £loo—reducible to £lOO cash by the winner
is what is probably meant. The tickets are on
sale at all music shops, news agents, tobacconists,
and, of course, from all bandsmen throughout the
Commonwealth and New Zealand. Half the nett
profits of the undertaking are to be divided
amongst the public hospitals.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
On November 14 John See, J. F. Burns and
William M'Millan, three N.S.W. ex-Treasurers,
celebrated their natal day.
Earl Beauchamp is mentioned by a pro-
minent Irish newspaper as likely to be the next
Lord Lieutenant of Ireland.
Arthur W. Jose is just now touring India,
gathering material for a book on that country.
Jose will probably drop into Australia on his way
back to England.
Conroy, epigrammatised the
charge against Barton, that he is unduly influenced
by Kingston and Turner, when he dubbed him
“the Primed Minister.”
Daniel O’Connor, M.L.A. (N S.W.) the
butt of so much badinage, is included in a late
official list of persons to be, if his Majesty pleases,
raised to the—er —dignity of C.M.G.
At Bothwell (Tas.), where lived John
Mitchell and his brother-in-law, John Martin (the
’4B Irishmen deported to Y.D.L.), there’s an old
gaol with cejls 7x3 feet —coffins for “living
corpses! ”
Rumor is again busy tipping Chief Justice
Darley as N.S.W. State Governor, and B. R.
Wise as his judicial successor in the chair of
Forbes, Dowling, Stephen, Martin, Salomons, and
the present Chief.
Both Goulburn’s C.E. bishops, Thomas and
Chalmers, died in harness. As a rule, the colonial
bishop goes ’Ome and settles down in a rich
vicarage. There are now about a dozen retired
Australasian bishops in England.
General Hutton, it seems, is now almost a
certainty for the Federal Commandantship. The
salary is £2500 —and the outspoken General is
fortunate enough to command privately as boun-
teous a store of money as of abusive language.
That fat billet, N.S.W. Crown-Solicitor-
ship, is still vacant. Rumor gives many whys as
to the delay, one the anxiety of the Govt, to give
it to a particular man who is not yet qualified as a
solicitor, hut who has done good work for the
State.
A Meroourne paper says that Bishop
Crane, of Bendigo, left no will and no estate.
Explanation—the Bishop was a member of the
Order of St. Augustine, and had no property of
his own. Whatever he used belonged to his order.
No “ religious ” can make a will.
The impression in Adelaide is that
Attorney-General Gordon anxiously waits to
accept the next vacant Judgeship. Bundey is ill,
Boucaut is old, and Way is ambitious of Imperial
distinction; so that one never knows when a
chance of promotion will happen.
Gov.-General Hopetoun intimates that he
mil hang-in with the Scots of Victoria in their
forthcoming national festivities. Hopetoun has
more savee than had poor old dead Governor
Duff—who brutally told a deputation of Sydney
Scots that he preferred a game of whist to haggis
kilts, or bagpipes.
Several Federal lawyer-politicians, whose
bar-work is slipping away, are credited with an
early intention of getting out. Hervey (W.A.),
Bruce Smith (N.S.W.), Symon and Glynn (S.A.)
are not considered likely to hang on very long,
especially as they now see very little chance of
snatching the plums of office from Barton.
Arthur Patchett Martin, The Bulletin
regrets to hear, has fallen on evil times in London.
He is suffering from a terrible illness and has not
the means to take the sea-voyage prescribed.
Victorian Agent-General Clarke is appealing to
Australians in London to help Mr. Martin, whose
old Australian friends will no doubt lend a hand.
Latest about King Edward’s health :
Lloyd’s have advanced the rate of premium on his life
from 10 to 15 per cent. These dealings indicate that the
ordinary insurance companies are shutting down on
policies on the King’s life. Friends of the King continue
to insist that he is only suffering from an aggravated case
of smoker’s-throat, complicated by gout.
And others say “Look at the family history! ”
News comes from London that Phil May
is going on the stage. “ Romeo and Juliet ”is to
be the initial venture; and Phil, as the Apothecary,
will draw corks and things. Alas, why don’t
people lay to their books ! As a practitioner in
black-and-white, an art wherein he stood at the
very topmost pinnacle, Phil May could earn £4OOO
a year.
In Shoalhaven district of N.S.W. South
Coast there are 150 old-age pensioners. Their
nationalities pan out: Irish, 58; English, 50;
Scotch, 20; Australian, 12; various, 10. Note
the relative positions occupied by the off-hand,
improvident Irish, and the cautious, canny Scotch.
The two races are about equally represented in
the district.
Poor old Dr. Chalmers, the just-deceased
Anglican Bishop of Goulburn, N.S.W., was given
a three years’ term among the savage Dyaks of
Borneo ere he was reckoned to be fitted for a
spiritual shepherdship of the wild tribes of Goul-
burn. After living unscathed among the Borneo
head-hunters, the Bishop finally succumbed to a
slippery church-step ! Such is fate !
Miss Emily Hobhouse, who wrote sensa-
tional accounts of the horrors of Kitchener’s
refugee camps, was not allowed to land in S’Africa
t’other day. Ketchener is determined to have no
serpents in his little Edens. Happily, there is no
need to deport the writers who gush about
Ketchener’s benevolence as displayed in those
camps—they have never been in Africa.
An M.L.A. with an inquiring mind has
demanded a return of N.S.W. Public Service
pensioners still able to work who are drawing
their pensions. Auditor-General Rennie, over 80,
and still toiling, is a marked contrast to the burly
football enthusiast, still a young man, who was
pensioned off long ago as medically unfit for duty.
He made a fine recovery, and is “something in
the city ” on his own account, with his pension to
boot.

hand out any of the usual birthday C.M.G.-ships.

country seclusion among his beloved merinos and
Herefords, and was keenly disappointed.

Senator Henry Dobson, ex-Tas. Premier,
will be 60 on Sunday (Nov. 24), and Senator Tom
Playford, of S.A., will be 64 on the following day,
which likewise is the fete-day of S.A. ex-Ohief-
Secretary O’Loghlin, who, like Playford, was a
member of last Kingston Cabinet. “J.V.,” as

Minister Larry O’Loghlin, is a year short of the

Kruger’s satire becomes savage. He has
now offered, it was cabled the other day,
to relinquish to England the Rand, whose
gold-mines caused the war, so long as his
people are left with their liberty. J. Bull has
talked so much cant about his holy motives in the
war that he has really come to believe some of the
cant himself. This jibe of Uncle Paul’s brutally
sweeps away the curtain of make-believe.
A clever parody of “Banjo” Paterson’s
verse (“Conroy’s Gap”) was written by a Vic.
rep. during Conroy’s four-hour speech against
time in the all-night sitting of the Tariff debate.
One verse:—
The trams and the trains are far away,
Gone is our chance for the midnight cab ;
So we snuggle down till the break of day,
Under the shadow of Oonroy’s gab.
Dr. R. S. Bright, the perky, abrupt-
mannered little surgeon who died, the other day,
at Hobart, was the doctor who attended Governor
Duff, of N.S.W., when he was stricken by his
last illness at Govt. House, Hobart. Dr. Bright
had lived in Hobart since he was seven years of
age, and he was 67 when he died. His father was
a London doctor who went to Hobart in ’43, and
died there 20 years later. “ Young ” Bright was
hon. medical officer to Hobart Hospital for 40
years, and resigned a week or two before his
death.
John Haynes so often yelps out “wolf”
on sectarian questions that a cry from him doesn’t
get the attention it deserves. So his protest
against a military guard escorting R.C. Arch-
bishop Kelly through the streets of Sydney was
passed off with a laugh. But it was based on solid
grounds : Australia can afford to give no military
recognition to either priest or parson, and the
N.S.W. Govt, should have refused to Archbishop
Kelly what it would have certainly denied to any
other religious official—except, perhaps, General
Booth, who commands so many votes.
Premier See’s son has weathered the storm
of indignation against his appointment to a com-
mission in N.S.W. Lancers. When he was pitch-
forked in last year the troopers demanded that a
non-com. in the regiment should receive the vacant
place. Mr. See then resigned, and re-entered as a
ranker. This softened the regimental heart, and
now he is gazetted as a .full lieutenant, with no
questions asked. Two sergeants are also gazetted
as probationary lieutenants, so the troopers seem
to have established the principle that meritorious
service in the ranks should precede a commission.
BLANOLT.
F. B. Suttor’s eulogy of Sir Joseph Banks
in N.S.W. Council carried out a piece of old
family history. Mr. Suttor’s grandfather was
acquainted with Banks, and, through the acquaint-
anceship, was induced to accept a post at Botany
Bay. Friendships appear to have been staunch
in those days. Gov. Bligh, though appointed by
the Crown, was really a nominee of Banks’, and
thus Suttor and Bligh held together. When, later
on, Bligh was placed under arrest by Major John-
ston, Macarthur and others, Suttor stuck to the
forlorn cause. For ‘ ‘ contumeliously ” disparaging
the new Governor set up, Suttor was tried, sen-
tenced to six months’ gaol, and fined. He lived,
however, to see Bligh exonerated by Crown war-
rant in the hands of Gov. Macquarie.

E. W. Cole, the remarkable Melb. “book-
seller” whom you find in a little office busy
clipping children’s scrap-books, with his thoughts
seemingly far away from the prosperous business
beneath him, is moved by the spirit of Altruism,
or Ad vt., to print a little pamphlet about “ White
Australia” and colored immigrants.' Cole con-
tends, in brief, that the Afghan, and the Chow,
and the Jap, and the colored rest should be wel-
comed and embraced because Jesus and the
Apostle Paul were colored Syrians. This is just
the appalling non-sequitur which one would expect
from a—Cole.
Thirty-seven years ago, two young bar-
risters, E. F. A’Beckett and John Madden, com-
peted for the billet of Registrar of Melbourne
University. Madden’s father was an attorney in
small practice, and not wealthy. A’Beckett was
son or nephew of the late Vic. Chief-Justice, and
had a relative in the Leg. Council; he got the post.
Now old Madden is Chancellor of the University,
with old A’Beckett in attendance as Registrar.
But old Madden doesn’t owe the old Regis-
trar any old grudge. He loves him for having
compelled him to become a Judge and a Governor,
instead of a quill-driver up Carlton way for the
term of his natural life.
Gr. H. Reid is looking after his Bar
practice in Sydney and leaving the tariff debate
to look after itself. He has openly announced
that though, as ever, he intends doing his best for
his political party, he cannot afford to neglect any
professional engagements—which is doubtless
quite true, for G.H.R. is poor, and he is growing
old and has a young family to provide for, and he
has worked like a tiger for Calico Jemmy without
getting anything like a decent pecuniary reward.
Unsympathetic administrations of the Old Age
Pensions Act in N.S.W. and Vic. seem to have
suggested to G.H.R. that there is nothing in
throwing away one’s living for politics.
“Melb.”: Bamford, in the House of
Reps., the other day, referred to a speech by
Kingsbury, at Bundaberg in ’93, when Kingsbury
said, “ Every brick here represents the blood of a
slave.” Bamford says this remark was borrowed
from a similar remark by G. V. Brooke in
Birmingham.. I think not. There would be no
more point in applying it to Birmingham than to
London. The real thing is a statement by G. F.
Cooke, the vinous tragedian, in Liverpool, very
early in the 19th century. He was drunk on the
stage, and the audience hissed. Cooke came to
the footlights with “Men of Liverpool! Every
brick in your accursed city is cemented with the
blood of a slave.”
The story printed as to John Longstaff
and E. P. Fox having been commissioned by the
trustees of Vic. National Gallery to paint certain
pictures on the strength of funds “ promised by a
squatter ” is incorrect. The sum at the trustees’
disposal is some £l5OO or so, which has accumu-
lated out of money left by Dr. William Gilbee,
surgeon, of Collins-st., Melbourne, many years
ago, to procure for the gallery a picture on some
Australian historical subject. Queerly enough,
the old bachelor-doctor stipulated that it should
be from the brush of an artist “residing in
England,” and it is at least partly on account of
this condition that the painters named have gone
to England. Longstaff’s subject is understood to
be the death of Burke at Cooper’s Creek ; he
made studies for it years ago.
The Bulletin knows the units of
the Sydney Shoddy Crowd too well to
imagine that they will do anything really
handsome for G. H. Reid, who works like a
cart-horse for them. They might dole him out
a kind of sweating wage, like that which they
give their clerks, until old age comes to him, but,
as for finding for himself and his wife the price of
an annuity of £5OO, or even £lOOO, which they
could well afford—well, not much ! It is a pity
that brains like Reid’s are wasted on such a crowd.
What an instrument the man with the eyeglass
could be in the cause of straight-out Australian
Nationalism ! Once or twice we have seen him
standing as it were on the verge—for instance,
when he laid aside those cohentingent papers and
contemptuously said the thing “ could wait,” and
when he alone of the Australian delegates in Lon-
don spoke up to Chamberlain. But, alas, he
Wobbles !
TIME ENOUGH
“ Yes, darling, I would willingly meet death at
your command.”
“ Oh, that would be lovely. But do wait until
after we are married.”
Mayor Graham, at the Sydney education
meeting, repeated the stereotyped tribute to “the
ability and genius of the framer of the present
N.S.W. (Education) Act, Sir Henry Parkes.” It
is time that this legend of Parkes as the original
inventor, patentee and propounder of the Edu-
cation Act was knocked on the head. As a cold,
brutal fact, Parkes fought the education move-
- ment tooth-and-nail, ridiculed it, scoffed at it, and
then annexed it in a high, marauding style when
he could no longer resist it —precisely as he did
with Federation. James Greenwood, the foremost
N.S.W. advocate for free, secular and compulsory
education, found Parkes his greatest adversary.
Yet there will always be people who will believe
firmly, without inquiry, that Parkes was the in-
ventor of the N.S.W. education system, and of
Federation.
Millionaire Carnegie is alleged to have
said that it is disgrace for a man to die rich. And
yet he cheerfully runs the risk of disgrace every
day of his life.
After the remarks of the consecrated
papers that M‘Kinley’s murder was attributable
to a godless system of education, it is interesting
to read-in the police description of the assassin:
Degree of education—Common school and parochial.
Religious instruction—Catholic.
Absurd people on the other side are now at liberty
to ascribe the act to the godly parochial-school
system.
London Daily Chronicle on the prohibi-
tion of colored labor on Australian mail steamers :
In conversation with the head of one of the largest mail
lines to Australia, after the insertion of the clause in the
House of Reps., this remark was made to the writer : “ It
has not yet passed the Upper House, and they are likely
to have more sense.”
But, alas for Toryism they hadn’t: an Tipper
House elected on manhood suffrage is not the
same sort of thing as a House elected by land-
lords.
Henry Lucy, in S.M. Herald, referring
to the S’African war, perpetuates the Jingo
suygestio falsi when he says “ the Boers had made
long and patient preparation for the conflict.”
The Boers’ preparation dates from the Jameson
raid ; before that they had no effective armament
whatever. After that—well, when you know
thieves are abroad, you naturally protect your hen-
roost. One irrefragable proof that the Boers had
not made “long and patient preparation” is the
fact that they were armed with absolutely up-to-
date weapons.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriglnalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ Ravenswood ” : Re “X.X.’s” query as
to why some bushmen are fonder of dogs than of
their own children. Australian youth at this end
of the continent (Q.) is simply reincarnated
aborigine. The native white in this part invari-
ably breaks a stick, not across his knee, but over
his cobra, like any other nigger, unless he is wear-
ing his best straw hat.

An unkind newspaper publishes far,and
wide the discovery of “an unknown animal in an
opalised state” at White Cliffs, N.S.W. Whilst
the quality of Australian rum remains at present
standard, news of finds of this sort should be
rigidly suppressed. Fancy being chased in the
jim-jams by a rose-opal bullock with a changeful
play of purple and green on its thi'eatening horns !
* * * *
“Walga” : A bitch at Haddon Rigg
station, Nevertire, N.S.W., gave birth to four
pups, and five days afterwards her progeny was
missing. Next day a man on the station killed a
carpet-snake of unusual girth, which was found to
contain the missing pups. That night the mother
of the pups arrived at the house in Nevertire
where the snake was, and, as soon as she spied the
reptile’s corpse, set up a mournful howl. After
pining for four days she died. She had travelled
from Haddon Rigg to Nevertire on the scent of
the snake. Truth of this is vouched for by Mr.
W. J. Carter, of Walgett.

The temperance lecturer was staying per-
force at the only hotel in the township, and was
naturally delighted when the landlord said he
preferred teetotallers to moderate drinkers any
day. Would he come to the meeting and say so?
He would, and he did. “My dear friends, lam
delighted to inform you that we have Mr. Boni-
face here, who will tell you that, after his long
experience, he prefers a teetotaller to a moderate
drinker. I will now call upon him to address
you.” (Loud applause.) “Well, you know,”
said Boniface, “in my experience, it’s this way.
A moderate drinker, he comes into the bar, orders
a drink, collars The Bulletin, gets a chair and
puts his feet up ; and there he is for half-an-hour,
for threepence or sixpence, as the ease may be.
Now, a teetotaller, he comes to the side door,
buys a bottle of stuff, pays for it, and is off like a
shot, and gives no trouble to nobody.” (Loud and
prolonged cheering.)
“Scutum”: Re the feats of strength
chronicled, 8., 16/11/’Ol. “Lifters” who wish to
remain strong should keep well within their
utmost. A few years ago Ike Goodwin, now 23
years of age, and living on Nambucca River
(N.S.W.), could surpass any of the strong men
mentioned (save, perhaps, the 2-ton gentleman),
but, through attempting too much, and that too
often, he’s now in a bad way. Writer once saw
him carry a 12cwt. roll of lead 50 yards. He
lifted one end of the roll, and, stooping down,
balanced it on one shoulder. He is also credited
with carrying three 2cwt. bags of corn from the
wharf at Bowra (N.S.W.) on to a drogher along-
side—a bag under each arm and one balanced on
his back. Ike is known by repute from Coff’s
Harbor to Port Macquarie.

“ Kob To one familiar with kangaroos
and their ways, the “Wallaby Camp,” by Harry
Garlick, in the N.S.W. Society of Artists’ present
exhibition, is most satisfactory. It is rare to see a
kangaroo pictured to anything like a resemblance,
but Garlick has them perfect in grouping, atti-
tude, and “manner.”

The record “shout” for Australia probably
occurred vvheif Cameron, of Golden-Horseshoe
fame, marched into Beechworth with his 2500 re-
tainers. A halt was made at Luke Riley’s pub.,
and £240 was planked down on the bar for the
first round of drinks. What sort of a night was
that!

“Clubs”: Re “Yar’s” idea to keep the
house clear of white ants (B. 29/6/’01). I once
put two of them on a black ants’ trail, and the
blacks went for ’em right enough, but couldn’t do
’em any harm, whereas the whites, with their
formidable nippers, kept cutting the bodies of
their assailants clean in two. Also, I have seen
white and black ants living in the same rafter.

Me and Bob
got in at Noo-

sort o’ piece,

agen, she goes

thing. “ Shut
that door! ” says

hot ! ” says Bob.
Then she starts

you orter seen
Bob —he just
gives one yell
and jumps!
After he’s gone,
she looks savage
at me and shuts

I didn’t say no-
thing for a bit,
but when it gets
pretty hot, I
shoved the door
open again.
“ Shut that ! ”
saysshe. “Wot?”
says I. “ That
door ! ” says she.
“ I won’t! ” says
I. “ I’ll stick the
pin in yer, if yer
don’t!” says she.
“You try it!”
says I, and I’m
blessed if she
didn’t! “ Curse
yer ! ” says I;
“think I’m made
of iron ?” “ Dun-
no,” says she;
“ but yer wasn’t
made a gentle-
man! Takeyer arm away I” “I won’t 1" says I; “and
don’t you try no pin business on me agen I " “ I’ll stick
it in yer till yer take yer arm away 1 ” says she. “ Just you
try it 1” says I, and she jist gets up when the tram stops
where I gets out. I felt pretty sorry I couldn’t have it
out, so I jist turns round and says : “ Don’t think you
made me get out here, because you didn’t ?” But she jist
turns up her nose and bangs the door and says nothing.
Some of them wimen are fair terrors, I can tell yer I

Editor Bulletin. —You are down on
Kipling for not having a correct knowledge of the
matters concerning which he writes, and still you
publish a par. (“ Abdul,” B. 6/7/’01) about people
on the Darling never having seen a two-storey
house. This is an infamous libel on Tinned-Dog-
Land. Why, at Tibooburra, long. 142 deg. E., we
have a two-storey building, and cut-stone at that,
the fame of which is spread even from Yalpunga
unto Booligal. Of course, you know where Yal-
punga is?—Yarkan.

kick at them, and knock them
over. Then he would tear
the windpipe out with his
teeth.”

“ Y.”: Casey was a raw new - chum.
Riding between Jembaicum bene and Braid wood,
he encountered a large flock of tame geese that
had to be ridden through. Casey was in a playful
mood, and flicked at a tall gander with his riding-
whip. Flying for his face, the bird grabbed the
long whiskers, and the horse bolted with go' se
and man in “holts,” and fighting like fiends.
The sight of the strong wings flapping was some-
thing to remember. Last I saw of Casey he was
on foot, and very lame. His beautiful whiskers —
all but a few scattered and broken hairs —had
departed, and his curse words were artistic. He
asked if I had seen a dashed, dashed, dashed
vulture.

“ P.X.” : I have heard bo much about the
awful wilderness of the Great West Land
(N.S.W.), so much of per-
petual drought and starved
stock and ruined squatters
and selectors, and not a
hint of another side to
the story, that I begin
involuntarily to kick.
It is a fact that many
squatters have had to
step out and let the
banks and other mort-
gage go’s, step in. The
early men made big and
rapid fortunes out of
this country, and the
second-comers, in most
cases, paid ridiculously
high prices for the
stations. That’s their
fault, not the State’s;
yet all the talk is as
though the State had
been fleecing them.
Their trouble with the
banks, &c., lias been,
of course, intensified by
the bad slump in price
of wool— which isn’t the
faultof eithertheeountry
or the State. Further,
in many cases, the
owners of these squat-
tages have expected to
live luxuriously in the
cities. Lately I read the
wail of one ; he was being
ruined; he must have
more liberal conditions.
Just about the same time
for that poor fellow’s pala-
tial city residence a laundress x
was|wanted —“ no cooking,” said
the advertisement ; the establish-
ment ran a cook and laundress.
Another of these ruined men’s mansions
was also just then illustrated in a print,
among “Our Stately Homes,” or something
of the kind. I have known men fight hard against
paying a rent of under Id. per acre to the State,
while at the very same time they have had an
offer from a squatter for 3d. per acre for the
grazing-right!

“ Eller : Re trapping wallabies (J.S.D.,
B. 16/3/’01). Last winter I struck, at Burra-
gorang, near Wilton (N.S.W.), the camp of two
men who were trapping parrots and sending them
to Sydney at the rate of 400 to 600 a week. They
were averaging at the time6d. per head. Not bad
for men who could get no work in town.

“ MS.” : Lately caught a mouse, which,
though it did not seem to have been hurt in a
trap or otherwise, had neither tail nor legs ; the
latter being represented by little stumps of bone

“ Right Angle ” voices the woes of the
bush-toiler:
When the bushman, grafting at 110° in the shade for
l‘2s. a week, and drinking muddy water instead of beer,
thinks over George Beid’s panegyric upon the Sydney
worker, the tea and nicotine that do duty for blood in ms
veins stir sluggishly. “ The great struggling masses, whose
only capital is the fleeting strength of their bone and
sinew which is encroached upon to the pitch of exhaustion
day by day, only to be renewed again by sleep.
The man “ outside ” eats ants in his sugar and drinks flies
in his tea, while, during the alleged harvest, be dispenses
with washing. His capital, also, is “ encroached upon to
the pitch of exhaustion,” but at the lower rate of 12s. per
week I So, when he pauses in his fight against drought,
ants, flies, rabbits, squatters, dry watercourses, gaping
tanks, and general isolated hopelessness, to listen to the
praise passed upon his fellow of the city, he begins to
wonder why for himself there is no minimum-wage no
platform commendation. He considers that he, as well
as the other fellow, is the “backbone and sinew,” but
never remembers it being assisted at more than 12 bob
a-week I
* * * *
Poetical outburst by a Yic. country paper
inspired by the—hie —annual agricultural gala :
Silence reigns, broken, perhaps, by the tooting of a
French horn from some circus-lent. Then the birds start
twittering in sweet chorus. The sun goes down like a
great ball of fire, and leaves behind a beautiful legacy in
the western sky—a perfect marvel of color—of purple,
blue, gold and crimson. By-and-by the stars peep out
one by one, and the lovely sky in the west dissolves and
merges in color with the dark-blue vault above.
Shoio day is over ! Think of it !

THE ESCAPED GIANTS.
JONES: “Hallo! Are those two sprats all you've
got for your day?”
FISHERMAN : “ Yes, had beastly luck. Got two
real boomers nearly into the boat, and then
lost 'em.”
JONES: “If there's anything in the survival of
the fittest, there should be great fishing round
Sydney in about another century. It’s always
the giants that get away.”
The luck of farming. In Junee (N.S.W.)
district last season a violent hailstorm completely
destroyed the crops of half-a-dozen farmers. The
other day a similar storm, in almost the same
locality, repeated the performance, and this after
years of drought. But Australian farming is
mostly luck.

In a township down South the committee
of the children’s annual picnic expressed a desire
to have a Punch and Judy show to entertain the
youngsters, and advertised accordingly. One
reply was to this effect :
Sir, —Hearin from a trend that yer wanted a Punch and
Judy sho fer picknic in yer town on 17 i now hoffer my
sho to yer fer 2 pun ten fer day my show is ther best in
ther koloni. Punch Judy gost komick man beedle tinker
angman bobby dawg tobi hand 6 hothers hand 6 scenes
beer-shop watch-house and guvment house this is ther
day sho at night I kan turn ther theater hinto a galauly
sho with limelights heffects my hextras houtside the 2
pun ten his if talking eads bismark hally sloper jimmy
jones, hand sally brass me wife as got the Marvanetts who
perform hin ther tent she will do ther lot fer 10 bob. if
yer give me ther job will be there on 16 get me ther job
hand I learn yer sume tricks to do yerself how yer kan
swaller a nife without herting yerself and how ter chaw
up a pun hand bring hit hout of yer boots has good has
new hif yer want me rite to . He hes mi horchestra
konsists of ther big Drum, ther Reid wissel, Hufrican
Jews Arp han Skotch bag pipes.

According to N.S.W. Aborigines Board’s
recently-issued annual report, there were in the
State, in October, 1900, 3108 pure aborigines and
3793 half-castes ; the deaths amongst the former
having been 140, to 73 births ; as against 165 half-
caste births to 59 deaths. During the past eight
years the full-bloods have decreased by 3342,
while the half-castes have increased from 2379 to
3793.

was unshaven and un-
shorn,
A warning sight to see;
I never saw a wight forlorn
Look so forlorn as he.
His hair was of the hue of
ash,
His face the hue of coal;
Yet ’midst that squalor was
a flash
That presupposed a soul.
His collar was of color sad,
A mourning-color deep—
It seemed, indeed, as if he
had
Been orphaned while asleep.
His hat and hoots were of the kind
A dead-heat would despise—
But still there shone a haughty mind
From, his red-lidded eyes.
“Now, pray arise and say what be
Your projects here V' I said.
He slowly turned and looked at me
Beneath his eyelids red.
He did not curse ; he did not swear,
As vulgar people can ;
He did not say he was aware
I was no gentleman.
He held a paper in his hand,
And, in a tone polite,
He said—“ Just look at this thing, and
Please say if I’m not right."
I took the paper, and I knew
At once his proud conceit,
And admiration filled with dew
Mine eye for that dead-beat.
“ I may be, sir, a holy show—
A swine degenerate—
But yet," he said, “ thank God, I’m no
Selected Candidate! ”
CREEVE ROE.
Bishop Chalmers, who died t’other day
in a Sydney private hospital from the effects of a
fall on the chancel steps, and Bishop Thomas, his
predecessor in Goulburn, were alike in two
features. Both were outdoor evangelists, and
both bitter indoor controversialists. They
chummed with cabmen, hodmen and the like ; but
stripped teeth and bit when anyone opposed the
tenets of Anglicanism. Chalmers’ late encounter
in the press with Cardinal Moran was in the
Thomas vein. Goulburn has had but two C.E.
bishops. Both were English, both filled offices in
England before coming to Australia, and both
were very popular with the community as a whole.
Wood, “The Optician,” of 299 George-st.,
Sydney, and Equitable Buildings, Elizabeth-st.,
Melbourne, is sole agent for the Patent New Field
Glasses. The special feature of these glasses is
that, once you have them focussed to your sight,
they remain focussed for all time. Thus if you
focussed them to watch the Melbourne Derby,
and then closed them and put them away in their
case, you would find on Cup day that all you
needed to do was to take the glasses from their
case, and pull them open, in order to have them
focussed exactly as before. This perfect con-
trivance has made these glasses tremendously
popular with British officers in Africa.

Emily Hobhouse, whose exposure of the
horrors of the concentration-camps in S’Africa
enrols her among the humanitarians of the age, in
her last letter (Oct. 1) to Mr. Brodrick, English
Sec. of State for War, writes: —
Will no prompt measures be taken to deal with this
terrible evil? Three months ago I tried to place the
matter strongly before you, and begged permission to
organise immediate alleviatory measures based on the
experience I had acquired, in order thus to avert a mor-
tality I had plainly seen was increasing. My request was
refused, and thus experience, which I could not pass on
to others, rendered useless. The repulse of myself would
have mattered nothing had only a large band of kindly
workers been instantly despatched with full powers to
deal with each individual camp as its needs required.
The necessity was instant, if innocent human lives were
to be saved. Instead we had to wait a month while six
ladies were chosen. During that month 576 children
died. The preparation and journey of these ladies oc-
cupied yet another month, and in that interval 1124 more
children succumbed. In place of at once proceeding to
the great centres of high mortality, the bulk of yet a third
month seems to have been spent in their long journey to
Mafeking and in passing a few days at some of the
healthier camps. Meanwhile 1545 more children died.
This was not immediate action; it was very deliberate
inquiry, and that, too, at a time when death, which is
unanswerable, was at work—nay, when the demands of
death, instead of diminishing, were increasing. Will you
not now, with, the thought before you of those 3245
children who have closed their eyes for ever since last I
saw you on their behalf—will you not now take instant
action, endeavor thus to avert the evil results of facts
patent to all, and suspend inquiry into the truth of what
the whole world knows ? In the name of the little chil-
dren whom I have watched suffer and die, and whom I
cannot for a moment forget, I make bold to plead with
you once more.

A story of Bully Hayes, told to a Bul
letin contributor at Port River (S.A.) by a re
ceiltly-deceased “old identity Captain Jerry
Marlin: —
“ Yes ” said Jerry, “’e wus the finest an’ most and-
somest ’man I ever clapped eyes on. I never ,®® e p
equal. I bear in mind the times e come up the Port
R.ver, w’en I were a bit younger. I think it was in a
stolen brig named the Lenorey. ’Owsoever,e brought
’is vessel up as bold as brass, an’ stepped ashore sif e
owned the hull place. I ’appened to be present at the
time : well, 'e carried ’isself like as you might imagine e
wus the Prince o’ Wales, an’ ’e spent ’is money in eaps.
Most everybody kotowed to ’ini, an’ appeared to be never
so ’appv an’ comfor’able as w’en they was seen talkin to
’im. But, as a matter of fact, they wus keepin their
weather eye open, for noos of ’is doings in the Sou Seas
’ad reached ’em afore ’e come ’isself. ...
“ Well, things went on quiet-like fer a long time, until
one day comes up the river a Yankee timber barque.
Now, the crew of this craft was a mixed lot, an about as
good a cooleckshun o’ dabs as I ever seen. The bo sun,
moresumever, wus almost as big in the body as Ayes, an
’ad a swagger on ’im like a six-pound rooster. One day
’e an’ two or three o’ ’is messmates come across Bully m
the parlor o’ the pub., an’, being a bit full, ’e was disposed
to be cantankerous. ’Ayes, as it ’appened, wus relatin
some of his exper’ences in the copra trade. The bo sun -
1 forget ’s name—listened, quiet-like, fer a, few minits ,
then said ’e : ‘Go to’ell, ’Ayes! I don’t believe yer!
With that w’at did e’ do but throw is
an’ all, square in ’Ayes’s face. ’Ayes stood up an looked
’sif ’e wus goin’ to ’ave a fit, but ’e sot down agin and
calmly wiped ’is face an’ clothes with is ankerchief.
Everybody thought, ’ere’s a go, there’ll be the devil of a
row. But no, ’Ayes tossed orf a nobbier of brandy,
nodded to the comp’ny, an’ went orf to ’is ship. (
“Well, the bo’sun went round every w ere boastin as
’ow ’e ’ad tamed Bully ’Ayes, an’ sayin’ w at e would
’ave done ’ad ’e only ’ad the chance of ago at im. ±>y-
and-by this got to ’Ayes’s ears, an”e sent the bo sun a
perlite note desirin’ the pleasure of ’is comp ny to tea,
together with ’is mates. Well, they come in a body to
the pub w’ere ’Ayes ’ad invited ’em, an sot down to a
real good spread. W’en they ’ad all crammed theirselves
with poultry an’ cakes an’ cettar.y, ’Ayes invited em out
into the back yard an’ offered to show ’em the proper way
ow to ’old their ’ands in boxin’. So they raked up an old
pair o’ gloves, an’ ’Ayes said ’e preferred to teach em one
by one, an’ to take the bo’sun last, as ’e was afeared the
bo’sun might know too much for him, an’ ’e didn t want
to be made a show of too soon before all the people what
’ad gathered to see the fun. But w’en they stood up to
’Aves ’e knocked ’em flat every time until they ad
enough of it. Then e’ said ’ed tackle the bo’sun, who put
on the gloves with a sooperior air, and reckoned hed
knock ’Ayes into a cocked ’at. To the toon o the star-
spangled banner they footed the mark, an’ the bo sun
begun lashin’ out like an angry mule, w lie Bully just
dodged this way an’ that way,’ an’ only ’it the fellow a
feather tap ’ere an’ a feather tap there, until the bo sun
fairly lost ’is temper, an’ threw ’is gloves orf, an fought
with’is naked’ands. ...
“ xt wus just then I seen a kind of satisfied smile come
on 'Ayes’s face ; ’e whipped orf ’is gloves like lightnin ,
drew ’is right fur back, an’ larnched it forward like a

Warner’s Safe Cure.
FEDERAL TARIFF PRICES.
The proprietors of Warner’s Safe Cure beg to»
announce that, as they have a branch laboratory-
in Australia, Warner’s Safe Remedies will not be
subject to the duty of 25 per cent., which will-
have to be paid on all proprietary medicines
imported into Australia.
The wholesale prices of Warner’s Safe Remedies-
have been slightly advanced in New South Wales
so as to make prices uniform throughout Australia,
but this advance is so small that it will not be
necessary for the public to pay more than two-
pence per bottle over and above the prices paid
before the Federal tariff came into force.
Warner’s Safe Cure is the only lemely known,
to science which will unfailingly cure all diseases
of the kidneys and liver. The following diseases
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure, because they all
all drise from the retention in the system of
urinary and biliary poisons which it is the province
of the kidneys and liver to expel: Rheumatism,,
indigestion, gout, neuralgia, biliousness, lumbago,,
sick-headache, backache, sciatica, gravel, jaundice,
bladder troubles, blood disorders, anaemia, Bright’s
disease.*

The pore working man has certainly been
iniquitously treated by the tariff. His liqueurs,
his Habana cigars, his imported walnut furni-
ture, his silks and his plug hats will cost him a
heavy sum extra weekly.
“Australians have a right to look on Mr.
Chamberlain as their friend,” says Senator Best
(Vic.). Now, why? What has Judas Chamber-
lain done for Australia that future Australians
will not curse his memory for ? ,
King Seddon, it is cabled, goes out of his
way to assert that it is not Maoriland which is
offering more troops for S’Africa. There must be
something very serious the matter with King
Seddon. Perhaps a kick on the doormat.
The determined effort which is being made
by Syme’s Age to curtail Hansard, should be
resolutely fought. Newspapers might garble
Bpeeches even more unscrupulously than at present
if there were no reliable check-reports at hand.
N.S.W. Opposition leader Lee’s latest is
that he intends to keep the See Govt. “ up to the
mark.” When first appointed he said that he in-
tended to keep the Opposition “up to the collar.”
Parkes, Reid, Lee: Preetrade on the downgrade.
Vic. politicians consider it likely that there
will be an early coalition between Peacock,
Irvine, and Shiels against the Radical members,
who have been convinced by recent events that
they have nothing to expect from either Govt, or
Opposition.

acting generally as friend and mentor to the
Labor party ! The old order has, indeed, changed,
when Granny trims her sails to the Labor
breeze m the effort to save herself from the print-
ing-paper duty ?

Senate as an argument in favor of admitting the
Hindu and the Chinaman to Australia, “ that the
history of Asia ran back for thousands of years.”
“So does the history of lions and tigers,” inter-
jected Senator Higgs.

A Sydney newspaper correspondent quotes
a postcard he once got from Gladstone to argue in
avor of a repeal of the Australian sugar-duty and
the consequent absolute destruction of the sugar-
growing industry. One might as well quote a
Kanaka on the question of theology. No man
was more ignorant of practical economics than
Gladstone. Witness his famous recipe for the
cure of English depression : “ Why don’t the
farmers grow strawberries ? ”

The unco’ pious pro-Kanaka party in the
B «P S ; trowed out the scriptural quotation,
God hath made of one blood all nations that
dwell upon the face of the earth,” and, character-
istically, stopped there, as if that constituted the

An Austrian diplomatist once argued to
Leak, the Hungarian patriot, on the absurdity of
attempting to preserve Hungarian nationality in
preference to the grand Imperial idea of a United
Austrian Empire. “Your argument is ex-
cellent, replied Deak, “but then it would be
better still to be in Heaven ; yet no one wants to
die. I his excellent answer would serve for the
Jingo apologists for gory Imperialism, who insist
on Britain’s divine mission to thrust the blessings
of her institutions down the throats of little
peoples at the end of the bayonet.

The Bulletin, wearied by half-baked,
doughy Tories, with no crust on them, welcomes
with joy any utterance from the genuine article
like Henry Scotland, who spoke gently in M.L.
Upper House, the other day :
I am convinced that it would be % good thing for the
colony if the working men were to take up their traps
and leave Maoriland in a batch. Their places could
easily be filled by Chinese and Japanese, and the very
men we are trying to exclude from the colony by a £lOO
penalty we should then be only too glad to welcome to
our shores. The white settlers could carry on the agri-
cultural pursuits of the colony, and the 'working man
could fcaks his labor and skill to some other country.
The selfishness shown over the tariff by
the various sections of members bodes ill for a
concrete Australian national policy. The Farmers’
PULL DEVIL, PULL BAKER!
party wants Protection for wheat, etc., but free
implements and commodities. The Manufacturers’
party wants Protection for finished goods, but
free raw-material. The Labor party wants Pro-
tection for some labor, but certain duty-free com-
modities which will get other labor the sack. The
shoddy party wants Protective revenue raised
from things it doesn’t handle, and all its own
goods let in free. Everybody wants to get some-
thing and sacrifice nothing.
N.S. W. Labor-leader M’Gowen, instead of
talking nonsense about “the disruption of
the Commonwealth Constitution ” by reason of
lack of State Revenue, might devote himself
to saving money by means of State reform. By
the abolition of the Leg. Council; reduction
of Assembly seats; reduction of Governor’s
salary to £2500 a year nett; abolition of Agent-
General as a diplomatic officer, and the appoint-
ment of a commercial agent at one-third
the expense :—N.S. Wales could save upwards of
£lOO,OOO a year and be all the better for it. But
there’s no fear of the Macquarie-street crowd
tackling reforms like those—dominoes are much
easier.
The question whether a private member of
the Federal Parliament can move for an increase
of a duty in committee seems to have been settled
by the increase of the opiurn-duty from 20s. to
30s. per lb., on the motion of Hume Cook, with-
out any protest from the Speaker. Constitutional
usage was, on the whole, against the right of a
private member to propose an increase of taxa-
tion. The theory was that the Crown may ask
Parliament for money, and Parliament may grant
the amount asked for, or less, but no more, for
fear that the Crown should go out and make a
beast of itself on the extra amount. Now that
“the Crown” is merely a fiction, there is no
reason for preserving the restriction.
Captain Tickell, commanding Vie. Naval
Forces, amused Australians the other night, at
Mayor Gillott’s banquet, by coolly proposing that
the British navy should in future be recruited
largely from Australia. Tickell showed that,
while it could build the ships and guns, Great
Britain found much difficulty in getting the boys
of the bull-dog breed, who grew less and less eager
and less numerous every year. Consequently, it
was desirable to encourage the seafaring instinct
of the Australians. People of the Tickell type
can never get away from the idea that Australia
exists as a sort of kitchen-garden for England.
They view it in the light in which a glue-factory
is seen by the gorgeous person who makes his
profit out of it.
Most of these States have now established
a policy of resuming large estates for Closer
Settlement, and this is how the thing works :
(1) The Government sells the land, and uses the
proceeds aa revenue.
(2) It borrows money with which to buy back the land
(the revenue never, by any chance, disgorges the money
already received for that land).
(3) The land is either leased or sold in smaller lots. If
it is leased, any surplus it yields above the interest on the
money borrowed for its repurchase is not applied to pay
off the debt, but is used as revenue. And, if it is sold—
well, it is pretty safe to bet the proceeds will very soon
drift into revenue in the good old way, and this genera-
tion will have spent the land twice over, and left posterity
to pay the bill as usual.
The introduction of the Immigration
Restriction Bill into the Senate last week was the
subject of a great speech by R. E. O’Connor, but
the whole of its arguments were at once demolished
by Aunt Emma Pulsford’s production of hitherto-
unpublished letters from the Japanese consul
telling the Prime Minister that the Jap. Govt,
strongly objected, not only to the color, but to
the educational test as well. The educational
test was supported wholly by the allegation that it
was a means of pleasing the Japanese, and
Pulsford’s showing that the Japs, were so pleased
that they intended making the “strongest protest
in the proper quarter ” left the Bill naked and
unashamed. Anyhow, M‘Gregor’s amendment to
substitute the color-test is almost sure to be
carried in the Senate; and the Reps., which
knocked it out by a majority of only five, will
certainly accept the alteration. Then— then— will
come the row with the British Govt.; but if
Australia keeps a stiff backbone the row can only
end one way. Chamberlain and his Govt, are not
popular enough to risk a conflict with the Com-
monwealth.

lately laid before Maorx-
land Parliament, show
that after the cession of
Samoa to Germany the
Seddon Ministry was
specially informed,
whereat Seddon pre-

was transmitted without

lain. The document
complains :
Great Britain, thftx civilised
them (the islands); that
Christianised them ; that
first traded with them ; that
even has the most trade and
the most white population ;
and that has spent _ much
blood and treasure in the
islands, has abandoned them
to foreign powers.
But Britain gave up
Samoa as the price, of
German non-interference in S’ Africa,
and so the protest of ultra-loyal, ultra-
Jingo Maoriland went for nought.. “The
colonies ” are all right to flatter, but it is the
Powers which get the concessions.
The sweated clerks of Melbourne »vant to
be brought under the Factories Act, but are told
it does not apply to them. Vic. Clerks’ Union
is therefore advocating a Bill which has
been drafted for it, and to which several
State members have promised support. It
is proposed to classify clerical employees,
to determine a minimum rate of wage, and a
maximum number of hours per week, with regula-
tions as to overtime, Sunday work, etc. Such
legislation should meet with hearty support.
There is no more wretchedly sweated laborer than
the city clerk.
With tongue in cheek the Philpites - bring
forward their belated Electoral Reform Bill,
knowing full well that, despite protestations, there
is no “business” in it. This legislative botch has
so many puerile side-issues and cumbersome pre-
cautions that it will but further confuse the com-
plicated Acts that enable the right people to stay
on the rolls and wipe out the other kind every
time. Should it pass both Q. Houses, it has to be
submitted for the approval of Chamberlain (as
explained by Minister Foxton, with a derisive-
smirk) ; therefore there is no possible hope of it
being law before April next, and by then the Q.
State elections will have taken place, on the old,
disjointed, anti-democratic Electoral Act that?
gives Philp and co. a firm grip of the reins of mis-
government. No wonder the Q. Laborites surge !:
The Federal Labor party shows itself well
versed in “ practical politics.” The final refusal;
of the Senate to hear the petition of Mr. Saunders,
against the return of Senator Matheson is an,
example. Saunders petitioned against Matheson’a
return on the ground of undue influence, alleging
a gift to the secretary of the Labor Electoral
Leagueof W. A. of £5O. An Elections and Qualifica-
tions committee was appointed to investigate the
affair, and at once resolved itself into two partisan
camps. The members did nothing but try to work
points. The Freetraders moved heaven and earth
to secure the confirmation of Matheson’s election
and the Protectionists to upset it. The com-
mittee, having argued from the early part of June
until tlie beginning of Nov., came to the Senate
with the intimation that it “ could arrive at no-
agreement.” No evidence in the case whatever
had been taken during all this period. All that
had been done was to wrangle over technicalities.
The Senate decided to wipe out Saunders for
good ; this decision being arrived at by an alliance
of the Freetraders and the Labor members. The
strong inference is that the Freetraders want to»
deny justice to Saunders because he is a Protec-
tionist, and the Labor party want to do the same
because their election tactics may be exposed
whilst the Protectionists, on somewhat the same
lines, want to give him, if possible, a little mores
than justice.

Even Melb. Argus begins to squirm oyer
the war, and has a column-long leader raking
Ketchener for his inability to finish it. Following
day it publishes an explanation from a returned
(Q.) cohentingenter, who states that in the section
he was attached to there were 30 waggons, 23 of
which contained the eider-down quilts, har-
moniums, spring mattresses and spare eyeglasses
of the officers of the “mobile” column which
scud over the veldt after the elusive De Wet.
Curious allegations as to the Chinese
methods of business have been made in an assault
case now before the N.S.W. Court. A witness
deposed that at a public meeting one Celestial
said :
Before the duties had been put on, he had been in th e
habit of putting' on 10 per cent, to the value of his goods,
so that he could get bigger compensation from shippers
in the event of any damage to consignments. Now, in
view of the high duties, it would not be worth-while to
swell invoices. On the contrary, it would be advisable to
reduce the values stated. He suggested that, in future
invoices, taels should be reckoned as dollars. A tael was
reckoned as 2s. 9Jd., and a dollar equalled _2s., and the
suggestion therefore meant making a reduction of 28 per
cent, in invoice values.
This, on top of recent Bankruptcy Court evidence
that Chinese naturalisation papers are a subject
of regular traffic, seems to suggest the advisability
of adopting very specific precautions to deal with
Asiatic duplicity. Deportation of the Chinese
population to the Flowery Land, which must be a
little short of population just now, would, on the
whole, save a lot of trouble.

“ There’s a weapon better yet.
And stronger than the bayonet,
A weapon that comes down as still
As snowflakes fall upon the sod,
And executes the Freeman’s will,
As lightning does the will of God,
It suffers neither bars nor locks,
This weapon is the Ballot Box.’ — Amer.
One of the coming dangers is that of the
woman who lost a husband a long time ago, and
hasn’t got a very clear idea now of what he
looks like, so that no adult individual in
trousers is quite safe from her vehement sus-
picions. And the Law treats lovely woman as an
irresponsible allegator where her feelings are
thought to be concerned. There is one law for
the female and another for the male. If She
picks up Him in the street and gives Him in
charge for being her lawful escaped husband, the
world laughs when the charge falls to pieces. But
let Him claim Her one of these days, and what
then? Firstly, He couldn’t persuade a myrmidon
of the law to arrest Her for desertion, because
the law isn’t built that way. Secondly, the news-
papers would take up the case against claimant
Man, and when the wildness of his assertion had
been proved the woman would land him for heavy
damages, or send him to prison as a criminal
libeller. There are still many points in being a
woman, even without a vote.

& d « ey s ’, Jon ® s ’ and Barnes ’ wickets—s for 57.

rL M s deliveries. Victoria’s first innings
1 1 c o id and their second 89, thus leaving the visiting
® de ,^ lB , t 1 ° tbe good. Barnes (for the ’Ome team) shaped
excellentiy Monday, getting 7 for 38, and building up an
average of 17 for 166 for the first two matches. Vic.
fielding improved towards the end, but the opposing
team showed out capably right through, picking up and
returning in extremely smart fashion. Monday saw
Garnett effect two capitally judged catches “in the
country. The wicket was bad from the beginning,
causing the ball to get up awkwardly on occasions
* * * *
Judged by their work against a weakish Yic.
team at the Melbourne ground last Saturday, the
Maclarenites are a N.T.B. contingent, who may possibly
make the tests interesting enough. Barnes, the long
Lancashire man with no English average to show what
8 mad e of, looks a very serviceable bowler; young
Blythe, a slim and likely sort, got Laver c. and b. in
smart style, and Braund, the leg-breaker, strikes one as
being a clever tactician. He is older than the other two,
but Braund s age is only 25, and he has some character in
his nose. If the young members of Mac’s team are as
steady as they are said to be, they will improve their
form considerably. In fielding, the visitors made an
admirable all-round show on Saturday, and when they
had got rid of the Vic’s, for 133 on a wicket whioh ranged
from respectable to good, the price of their stock was
rising. Not Too Bad, with an accent on the Not.
Only three of them batted. M'Gahey and Hay-
ward should both have 'been caught before the score got
to 20, but they afterwards did their best to deserve the
luck of it. Hayward, who was never a very strong man,
seems to have lost a little vigor since he was out with
Stoddart’s XI; and, though he plays much about the same
sound game as before, his tiredness will discount his
ability. M‘Gahey, notwithstanding his 57 score and his
confidence when he got set, didn’t convince anybody that
he will often get set in Test-matches. He has a good
glance-stroke to leg and a spanking drive to the off (which
is only worth one run, as a rule), and several other strokes
of a rougher sort; but he’s very risky, is tall, muscular
M'Gahey. Braund, who came after him, and helped Hay-
ward to play till closing time, is a rather stylish batsman.
Jones, the captain of the Britishers in Maclaren’s
absence, managed to take a rise out of a “ barracker ” on
Friday. The ball was bumping a lot, and the straddle-
legged newchum didn’t look happy. “Show us why
Jones left ’Ome,” sang a voice from the shilling seats.
Jones stepped out to the next ball and banged it over the
fence for five. ' •*
*•* * *
Giffen’s bowling (for S.A.) against the English-
men (13 for 93), average 7.15, was an eye-opener; for most
followers of cricket rated George, at 43, very much of a
back-number. In his palmy days Giffen never did any-
thing as good. Maclaren looked at the wet pitch on
Tuesday and said his men would be favored, as they were
used to that sort of thing ; but he was a bit out, for the
Englishmen were just as much puzzled as the wheat State
players. The “ gate ”on the last day reached £69, and
receipts for the match totalled £7ll.
* * * *
That backdown on Monday night of N.S.W.
cricket association on the umpire question, was full and
complete and what reasonable people predicted from the
outset. How a presumably intelligent body could place
itself in the position of having to eat the leek so
jgnobly surpasses understanding. Its attitude was
indefensible and untenable, and so evidently the outcome
either of its notorious jealousy of the Melbourne club,
or of a desire to “ get even ” with the Marylebone 0.0.
(with which Maclaren’s lot have nothing whatever to do),
that the N.S.W.O.A. wiseacres have been heartily laughed
at everywhere. ' ,
Dear B. — Public opinion was too strong for
N.S.W. Cricket Association over the umpire business.
Simply put, the question came to this : Would the Jingo
Flat Immovables allow the Grassy Gully Irresistibles to
choose both umpires in their cup match ? According to
the man in the pavilion, the real truth about the trouble
never came to the surface, but bowler Marsh was the bone
of contention. Though honestly convinced themselves
that Marsh is absolutely fair, the Association committee
know that Crockett, Maclaren’s umpire (also honestly),
no-balled Marsh in the last match against Victoria : and
when the question of umpires came up. to make quite
sure, the committee decided to appoint their own men.
But, leaving the bowler concerned out of the question,
the Cricket Association had nothing to fight for.—Yours
etc., Paddington.
* • * *
London Daily Mail gossip about Maclaren’s
task. Lord Hawke would have managed a team. He
asked Maclaren, Fry, and Ranji, but they declined, and
he then threw the idea up. Maclaren’s great difficulty
in inducing players to come was the low remuneration
held out. In face of special terms offered by the York-
shire committee for the winter months, £3OO each wasn’t
good enough for Rhodes and Hurst. One “ star” would
have been satisfied with £BOO had it been forthcoming.
Inability to guarantee these sums forced Maclaren to
secure amateurs looking for a good holiday free of exes.,
and pros, prepared to accept the conditions. The Daily
Mail names the following as the 14 best players in Eng-
land :—Fry, Ranjitsinhji, Maclaren, Jessop, Mason, Tay-
lor (reserve wicket-keeper), Gunn (J.), Hayward, Tyldes-
ley, Quaife (W.G.), Rhodes, Hurst, Braund, and Lilley.
Eight of these are with the team in Australia now.
* * * •
Trial match, N.S.W. first 11 v. next 15, ended
Saturday in a draw. Previous Monday, the 15 had 10
down for 320. The last four only added 21. M. A. Noble
showed fine bowling form, and got 6 for 57; Billowell
took 4 for 60, Clarke 3 for 85, and Hopkins 1 for 93. The
11 had lost 6 for 107 when rain stopped further play. C.
Gregory got a nasty but not serious smash in the face
from the second ball aboriginal Marsh sent down, and had
to withdraw. On the whole, play was uninteresting.
Large crowds rolled up each day.
* » • *
A late ’Ome cricket item:—“Ex-Australian
Albatrott is on his way out to act as bowler and coach to
Hawkes Bay (M.L.) Cricket Association this season, and
will go back in time to play for Middlesex next year.”
A.T. is among the best all-rounders of his day. The M.L.
contract has probably been brought about by a desire to
escape the English winter, which does Albert no good.
* * * *
Recent M.L. Oup was a throw-in for Mo—a
complete skinner to most. Black daughter (Tortulla) of
Torpedo, who humped 8.1 and won, wasn’t given much of
a chance, though backed on the day to catch a few
hundreds. Quite half the starters—particularly Palaver,
Canteen, Ideal, Fulmen and Battleaxe—had solid support.
Melwood, Sirius and Kahuwal were also whispered fairly
good goods. The race was the most open ever run in
M.L. Touts, newspaper tipsters and alleged judges
generally failed badly in their predictions. Not one of
the hottest pots got a “ drum.” Tortulla was prepared
by Prosser, the trainer of Advance, and was ridden by
O. Jenkins, who has had to stand more abuse than any
dozen of his fellow jocks. The winner’s whack of the
stake totalled £l2OO. Subsequently Tortulla (with 9.3 up)
failed in The Jockey Club Handicap—l mile. Palaver
(8.2) scored cleverly from Oannie Chiel (7.9). Latter was
seriously interfered with by the winner, but owner (Sir
George Clifford) refused to protest, though it looked any
odds on him getting the money had he taken that action.

Gr. 0. Stead had a great time at Canterbury, M L ,
J.O. meeting, gathering in Riccarton Welter (£200) with

(£600) with Orloflf (Stepmak—Sortie) ; Steward’s Handi-

Harrow, Q. two-year-old, developed good form
at recent Q.T.C. meeting. After winning the Hopeful
Stakes on his own,” he ran in a handicap, carrying
1/lb. over w.f.a., and got within half a length of winner.
He is engaged in A.J.C. Champagne Stakes, and his
owner is sending him for it.

Profit on V.R.C. Spring gathering was just
over £lO,OOO. Last year ’twas £11,027. Stakes were
brought from £17,000 to £19,000 odd, hence the smiles
and choice grog in V.R.C. fold.

¥ any Moseses stood and backed Revenue for
Melbourne Cup. Said that “ Oxy ” won a good lump,
also that another of the tribe (a Sydney layer) gathered
in a thou, about the Hymettus-Re venue double. Tales
of the ring s heavy losses want heaps of seasoning to

Watch’em ” writes: “Heard of a punter who
picked the card at Caulfield on Saturday, 19th ult
Beginning with a wager of 10s. on the first race he played
his winnings up and left the course with £212 to the
good. Other side : A boy was left £BOO a year ago.
Taking a well-known tipster as his oade mecum, the
youthful plunger tilted at the ring until he lost every
cent., and is now driving a milk-cart. A former Melb.
Cup winner hawks vegetables, having lost to Ikey Mo.
the £12,000 he won over that race, besides £5OOO he had
saved.”
* * * *
Despite the moan of Ikey Mo and co. there
were various mokes in Melb. Cup that would have panned
out worse than McDonald’s good thing. As one of the
unchosen remarked to writer, “ If Australian Colors or
Hymettus gets home the Maoriland steamers will be
chock-a-block with hook noses and jewellery.”
A Maoriland paper bitterly deplores the break-
ing down of the steamer Monowai in mid-ocean—because
she carried a ton of Tattersall letters which thus missed
the Oup sweep.
* • * *
Sporting writers comment scornfully on the
mediocre crowd of nags for Melbourne Cup. Yet never a
protest when any horse up to w.f.a. form is bought for
other lands. Hautvillers is now off to S’America.
Unless the tide turns, the Melbourne Oup will, in a few
years, be about the calibre of a Moorefield Handicap (or a
backblocks hurry-scurry). Consider the expatriated of
the last ten years—Carbine, Abercorn, Trenton, Auruin,
Carnage, Australian Star, Clean Sweep, etc., etc., and the
tide still ebbs.
* * * •
Tucka Tucka (N.S.W.) stallion Gozo’s get did
most at V.R.C. Spring meet. Caledonia (two) and Ohio
(one) credited him with three races. Others to show out
well were Trenton (Revenue and Wakeful), Projector
(Ballyrogan and Point Piper), Wallace (Beanba and Inde-
pendence), Bill of Portland (Niphetos and Fishery) and
Malua (Glen Albyn and Kooma).

and Frank Kuhn (two each).
