He San Fran’s humping the crusher of 9.7 second
to Revenue. Carbine chased Bravo home in ’B9 with
lOst. in the saddle ; following year he won, carrying 51b.
more. Five years later Patron succeeded with 9st. 31b.
Next highest weight borne into a place was The Grafter’s
9.2 in ’9B. On the running it looks good odds that if San
Fran hadn’t been burdened with a penalty he would have
beaten Revenue.
♦ * * *
Revenue is the tenth first favorite to get home
in Melbourne Cup, and six of the longest-priced nags
have secured first money. Forty-one “ Cups ” have been
contested; 24 were kept in Victoria; N.S.W. got away
with 15, and S.A. two. Only one grey (Toryboy) has
passed the post first. Three-year-olds have succeeded
twice, four-year-olds eight, five-year-olds 13, six-year-olds
five, and aged nags four times.
• * « •
Sydney shop totes—with the robbery and job-
bery surrounding them given in—are always better invest-
ments for the small punter than taking Mo.’s cramped
odds. F’rinstans, Revenue paid 19s. for each ss. on him,
or nearly 3 to 1, while Ikey couldn’t be induced to dis-
gorge better than 2 to 1 or 5 to 2 at most on the day.
Besides, the tote investment would have provided a win
had Revenue run second, and had he finished third the
ss. might have been saved. In the Carnival Handicap,
Caledonia paid £33 odd for ss. Best odds Sydney books
offered were eights, and starting price was tens.
* * • *
Facts concerning “ Tatts.” Melb. Cup sweeps.
Owner of Kenley (Eden George) drew that nag in No. 3.
A Warrnambool man—dreaming he and two friends got
the right pea—straightway wrote the two, enclosing ss.
with the suggestion that they should subscribe a like sum
and get three tickets. Result Revenue. Twenty mem-
bers of Mount Lyell (Tas.), Stock Exchange drew Revenue
in the £1 sweep, and whacked £13,500 among them.
* * * *
Opponents of the totalisator ought to come to a
reasonable understanding among themselves as to where
the “ evil influence ”of the machine comes in. Hon.
J. H. Howe stated, a while ago, that he had “ visited every
race-meeting since the machine had been used,” and
found that its effect had been to double the number of
bookmakers. This is doubtless the reason why the num-
ber of registered books in Victoria and N.S.W. has
diminished since the tote was legalised in the other
States. The Ikeys in S.A., Q., and Tas. have grown so
rich through doubling their numbers and betting in
opposition to the tote that they don’t take the trouble to
get registered in N.S.W. Rats! Now, Roberts, M.L.A.,
tells a different tale to Hon. Howe’s. He says: “The
totalisator is the chief attraction at all race-meetings.
Its sensational dividends bring fresh supplies of people
into the gambling world.” But he hasn’t the gall to
assert that the sensational divs. of the tote have caused
double as many people as of yore to hanker after the
starvation odds of Ikey Mo. Every big div. paid by the
machine is a nail in the coffin of its bitter foe.
Roberts would probably admit this, whilst his anti-tote
brother Howe was still protesting that the tote created
new bookmakers by the simple process of calling scornful
public attention to the market prices of the old ones.
Roberts, M.L.A., confines himself to the assertion that
fresh supplies of gamblers are continually coming within
the radius of the tote. Therefore, its takings must have
increased enormously during the past 10 years, and the
easiest of things for Roberts,’ M.L.A., would be to
produce evidence in figures that the present population
of S.A. is alarmingly addicted to .the tote habit in com-
parison with the public of 1891. What figures can he put
forward ? Where does the evil influence of the machine
come in ?
• * * *
Yankee Jim Scanlan and N.S.W. slogger Billy
MeColl, both within an oz. of 11.10 at the ringside,
struggled through a painful exhibition that couldn’t, by
any stretch of imagination, be called a fight, at Sydney
Golden Gate A.O. Monday night last. More willing or
stronger pugs . have rarely been seen .; both were in
splendid condition, and both have a heap to learn before
they can be called clever. In the ring neither showed he
had a pair of feet, and both seemed to be under the im-
pression that the other fellow’s only vulnerable point was
his head. Scanlan hit clean and effectively when he did
hit—which was rarely ; and McColl’s punches beat
helplessly on the other’s bony elbows and fore-
arms. Scanlan’s defence took the bun for originality.
He smothered up and completely bamboozled Me., who
was fairly puzzled what to do. The way the Yankee
fended the Cornstalk’s swings and hooks was the one
redeeming feature in the whole 20 alleged rounds.- Betting
opened at evens, then 5t04 on Scanlan. In round two 2
to 1 was laid on MeColl, but later on Scanlan’s stock rose
considerably, and at the end he appeared to have won by
a fair margin. Referee Tom Nolan thought otherwise,
and gave the victory to MeColl. The big crowd evidently
went away disappointed, the fight falling far short o‘f
anticipation. MeColl, the best of his weight, shows how
Australian boxers have degenerated ; and the reputation
Scanlan brought from Amurka proves they are no better
over there.
* * * *
Melb. 11.4 pug., Jack Willis, since reaching
Sydney about a year back, has fought 12 battles, meeting
the same men twice on three occasions. Though Willis
isn t a champion, he takes on any contract with shekels
attached, and fights a game battle every time. Monday,
last week, he flattened out “ Kid ” Parker and McCarthy,
and prov ed conclusively that their forte was not bruising
The smashing of Teuton Gus Ruhlin by Jim
Jeffries in five rounds, at ’Frisco last week, was not un-
expected, though the fight must have been Homeric;
Four years ago, the same pair sledge-hammered each
other for 30min. (including special intervals) so equally
that it was declared a draw. Jeffries was only starting
then ; how he has improved the brevity of this last battle
tells, as Ruhlin has improved vastly, and is now only 27
years old. Maoriland freak, Bob Fitzsimmons, knocked
the mighty Ruhlin out in six rounds, 12 months ago, just
after Ruhlin had smashed sailor Sharkey, in 15 rounds
* » * *
N.S.W. swimming prospects are good. Satur-
day last nine clubs decided races, and 200 swimmers
lined, up—a common occurrence now. Despite Fred..
Lane s long spell up country, and fine sprinting form
already shown, he’s not likelv to shape in anything over
300 yards. Barring one or two meteor-like “unex-
pecteds,” it looks as if Read and Dick Oavill will stand out
in everything from 300 yards up. Last month, in Eng-
land, world’s champion Joey Nuttall failed to break Fred
. Lane’s 300yds. in 3.47 4-5 by l-ssec. When Lane did it,
many judges sniffed at it with the remark that the
distance of 300yds., being so rarely swum, rendered the
making of a record comparatively easy. Nuttall’s failure
proves Lane’s performance to have been pure merino
* * * *
All-comers’ first and second-class sculling events
at Balmain (N.S.W.) regatta, revealed some capable
coming men. Brother (O. Towns) of the world’s champ.,
heavily boated, supplied a tough struggle with C. Mes-
senger in the big event. Messenger shaped well, and
ultimately won. Hunter River man Chapman hadn’t
enough condition to live it out, and Burgess—a compara-
tive novice from the same quarter—rowed takingly, but
has something to learn about “ best ” boat work. When
he gets over that he’ll hum. Burgess looked to have the
second-class race “ on his own,” but young Reid came
with a well-sustained dash that just pipped the Northerner
by 3ft. Though there’s only a little of him, Reid is a
bull-dog. _ A bad course marred his prospects, and it was
only by dint of a brilliant sprint that he got there at the
right end.
* • • *
The Australian sculler abroad: After Towns
accounted for Jake Gaudaur, Harry Pearce easily
squelched Canuck John Hackett, who came to the front
during last four years. At Winnipeg regatta, Towns con-
ceded 3sec. in 1J mile, with a turn, to fellow-Australians
Tom Sullivan and Pearce, and the Canadian Durnan, Ned
Hanlan’s much-boomed pupil. Sullivan took the boodle,
with Pearce second, Towns third, and Durnan nowhere.
Pearce writes that Fox is the best sculler in England, and
that little Australian flier Daley could grill him brown if
they could only be got together.
* » • *
After all, it doesn’t look likely that world’s
premier sculler, Towns, will be out here for another year
at least, so Stanbury and co. may have to dwell on their
oars a bit. Towns is matched to meet “ Eddie ” Durnan
(Hanlan’s nephew) on the Thames next spring. This is
the outcome of the Canuck’s challenge issued months
back. Durnan hasn’t done anything beyond what an
ordinary good man might, but he has been boomed a
heap by the Yankee and Canadian press and his erstwhile
famous uncle. It looks a good thing for the Cornstalk.
* ♦ * *
Sims—winner of the recent bowling supremacy
of Australia promoted by theM.O.C.— is amongst the oldest
at the game in Victoria. _ Critics declared he had had his
day and was too much in the sere and yellow, but after
six hours’ solid graft on the closing day he looked as
fresh as the freshest. His drawing was accurate to a
degree, and his driving, when he did drive, invariably
evoked applause. Sims’ methods were a revelation to
some who believe in getting close to the “ jack ” every
time. He always placed his first two bowls behind, thus
depriving his opponent of the advantage of a “ rest,” and
being prepared for the shifting of the jack. Nothing but
superior knowledge and a cool, calculating style of
operating won the title for the veteran who has weathered
many engagements of a like nature. The championship
of Victoria fell to him on five different occasions.
* * * *
“Bias”: Rather remarkable that four prize-
takers, Sims (winner), Waxman (runner-up), Wartman
and Ellis, all act as rink skippers for their several clubs.
A big set-back for those who contend that leaders and
third players are as good as captains.
* * * *
At a recent Charters Towers cycle meeting
there was a race for goats attached to go-carts ; 12
starters. A leviathan opened-out at 4-to-l on the event,
and laid “ Stinker ” 11 times at 4,3, 2, and even-money
to crowns and half-crowns. Nothing else was backed,
and Stinker won easily.
* * * «
Victoria owns two youthful phenoms. in the
pigeon-murdering biz. “Amberite” is ]ust 13, and
“ Mafeking” will be 17 next birthday. The right names
of these shooting kids are Frazer and Fawkes respec-
tively. Fawkes was the biggest winner at Melbourne
Gun Club and North Brighton meets, winning £45 in the
two days. He lost his second nom. on the first day, but
carried the first to the end, shooting 19 straight, mostly
with the first barrel. Frazer, with a gun larger than
himself, brought down 22 out of 23 in the big thing of
the Melbourne Club gathering, and later, from 22yds.,
whacked a £25 sweep with Vic. champion A. E. Smith and
ex-champion Bobby Grant. He murdered 15 right off the
i reel, and at the close of the seventh round was the only
one to have his two noms. going.

Cyclists will be pleased to hear that the Dunlop
Tyre Co. intend issuing another of their artistic racing
souvenirs, 20,000 of which will be distributed at the
Austral Wheel Race meeting. The folder will this year
contain fine half-tone illustrations of J. Megson (N.S.W.);
E. Wilksch (S.A.); A. Ralston (N.Z.) ; D. Walker (Vic.) ;
and G. Sutherland (N.Z.).

• • * *
After many years. Famous Anglo-Australian
cricketer, S. M. J. Woods, will shortly visit Sydney for a
season and probably play for his birthplace, Manly. He
is a son of John Woods, of Manly, went home with two
brothers 17 years ago, and stayed there, being
captain of Somerset O.C. He skippered England in inter-
national Rugby matches, and played cricket for this s
against the Mortgagee Land.
* * *
Ancient, reliable jock, Tom Aspinall, after 26
years, has struck a seam of very bad luck. Months DacK
a heavy fall seriously affected one of his shoulders. After
being patched-up, he came another cropper, and frac-
tured a leg. He then requested the surgeons, while they
were at it, to remove his useless right arm at the socket.
He rode Tradition when that colt was second in Caulfield
and Melbourne Cups 13 years ago.
* * * *
The Assyrian is coming down upon another
part of the “fold.” At Coonamble (N.S.W.), the other
day, the £25 wheel-race was annexed by J. Ayoub, from
170yds. start, and the Patrons’ Gift fell to F. Simon, from
scratch ; both of these competitors being sons of Bel-
shazzar.
• * * »
N.S.W. country bike clubs have the Bilmartin
scare bad. Up Forbes way, t’other day, the great old
plugger wasn’t given a hope in the handicaps, and after
two essays, though he had other engagements in the
district, the ’Murkan rolled up his valise and went
Melbournewards. The winner of a mile race covered the
distance in 1.44. Martin previously had a cut at the
neighbouring Parkes meeting. The secretary encouraged
Bill’s visit with mailed information that though the starts
might look long, those who had them were “ pure
mulgas.” Seeing that the heats of the Half Mile were
won in 57, 58, 58 3-5 and 59sec., and one heat of the Mile
only occupied 1.46 j, the man from the land of the wooden
ham wants the Parkes people’s definition of “ mulga,” as
nothing he can get elsewhere fits his experiences.

At Poverty Point.
Last week’s paragraph in regard to Miss Irene Franklin
was incorrect in some details. Mrs. Franklin is in a
private hospital, not an asylum ; and the arrangements,
financial and otherwise, with regard to Miss Franklin’s
visit to London were made by herself, not by Mr. J. C.
Williamson, who assisted her only to the extent of giving
her letters of introduction to various managers there. No
other assistance was required. Mrs. Franklin, we are
deeply sorry to hear, is very seriously ill.
Printed by a London paper that “ an American syndi-
cate agent arrived in Australia lately to ascertain the
possibilities of running a ‘continuous’ (i.e., all day)
variety show in Australia on Yankee lines. He left quite
satisfied ; and Australia is now to have a continuous
variety entertainment.” From which infliction may the
Lord deliver us ! The continuous variety show in its
most awful form runs from II a.m. to 11 p.m., and never
gives its theatre a proper chance to get a good dose of
clean air. Its guiding principle is to give the public
quantity, not quality, for its money. You may go in at
11 a.m. and sit it out until the last awful joke at 11 p.m.
—that is to say, if you are stone deaf and hopelessly
idiotic. But, for the ordinary public, the management
provides a “clearing-out turn" every two hours, a per-
formance so utterly bad as to drive everyone out, and
thus make room for a fresh crowd. A comic man who is
so pre-eminently bad as to clear a continuous variety
hall with certainty and dispatch commands a very high
salary in the places where these horrors abound —another
illustration of the fact that in any Art to be supremely
bad is always better than to be merely good.
Lempriere Pringle, the operatic basso, had a big recep-
tion in his native Hobart the other day. He lived in
Hobart till 18 years old, and then went to Europe for his
musical education. Prior to leaving, young Pringle was a
clerk in the office of Lands and Works. He was received
at the Town Hall by a large gathering of citizens, and
welcomed by the Premier and the Mayor, on behalf
of his “ fellow-countrymen.” Pringle’s step-father is
C. J. Barclay, managing director of the Commercial Bank
of Tas. His father was, years ago, a clerk in the same
bank, but relinquished the ledger for the operatic stage.
Dear Bulletin. —Let me call your attention to the fact
that Adelaide is no longer a “holy city,” for, though
churches and parsons still abound, we now keep two
theatres going, and managers are no longer sure to lose
money here. We’d do even better if The Bulletin would
be more frequent and less satirical about us in “ Sundry
Shows.”—W.A.S.
Wilson Barrett talks about the “ millenary” of Alfred
the Great. Error in spelling. Millinery is the word,
when Wilson Barrett is concerned.

be wrong in saying that Mr. and Mrs. Robt. Heir played
at Bendigo in the early ’7o’s. Robert Heir’s widow was
married to George Darrell on Thames goldfield, M.L.,
before the dawn of the ’7o’s.” Which reminds The Bulle-
tin that George Darrell is getting to be considerably older
than he looked when he took a farewell benefit in Mel-
bourne some months ago.
The late revered “ Jimmy ” Alison was highly indignant
one morning because nearly the whole company was late
for rehearsal. “ Look here,” roared Jimmy, “if this
happens again I’ll have you all found I ”
Lily Titheradge was married, the other day, to a medi-
cal student in Scotland. The sprightly little woman is
understood to have given up the stage.
Cinquevalli has started again for Australia, but he
travels via America, and is booked for six months of en-
gagements in that country ere he comes to Rickards.
For once Charles Holloway intends to give blood-
curdling drama a spell, and will produce a costume play
of the Charles and Cromwell period, called “For the
King.”
Dear Bulletin.— Sentenced to sit out Wilson Barrett’s
“ Hamlet,” the other night. I made a discovery—the
first and second grave-diggers are the prototypes of
Dooley and Hennessy. The first grave-digger, keen old
philosopher, is Martin Dooley, and Hennessy (whose
creation, to my mind, is even a cleverer bit of character
work than Dooley himself) is manifestly identical with
the second grave-digger, trying hard, and in vain, to keep
pace with the intelligence of his companion.—D.W.
The visit of Mr. Henry Hallam, after an absence of
nearly 30 years, revives memories of his first wife, Hattie
Sheppard, a native of Tasmania, and an excellent actress.
Hattie Sheppard died at Melbourne in September, 1874,
aged 26 years, but she still lives in the praises of her old,
enthusiastic admirers.
The cast of Pinero’s latest “ serious” play (“ Iris”), at
the Garrick Theatre (London), includes three old Aus-
tralian friends—Dion Boucicault (who is also stage-
manager), Miss Beryl Faber, and Mrs. Maesmore Morris
in a minor part. Pinero is just now writing a light
comedy for another theatre, wherein Boucicault s wife
(Miss Irene Vanbrugh) will have the chief part. The
great little Dion probably wishes he had gone back to
London a few years earlier than he did.
The two first exponents of the dramatic art in Victoria
were Bichard Winter, who arrived in Sydney from
England in ’34, and made his appearance at the first
theatre in Melb. in ’4l; and Richard Capper, whose
appearance as an actor was a few months later, though he
officiated as machinist in the same initial performa-nce.
The “ last curtain ” fell on both 15 years ago, so that they
reached a good old age. Curious to read in an ancient
newspaper file that at the first theatrical performance m
Melb (“ The Widow’s Victim ” and “ The Lottery
Ticket ”), Arden, editor of the Gazette, was to haye
delivered the opening address, but was non est, being in
gaol under commitment by Judge Willis for contempt of
court • and that Kerr, of the Patriot, announced the
postponement of the recitation. Cheers were thereupon
given for the absentee, with hisses for the judge. Two
newspaper reporters—Davies and Boursiquot sustained
characters in “ The Widow’s Victim.” The first named
was father of the present proprietors of Hobart Mercury.
Re curious “shingles,” (B. 22/6/1). There used to be
exposed in a Vic. border town a sign-board with the
legend : “ F.X., Theatrical and General Bill-Poster, and
First Violin.”
“N.”: “The Sign of the Cross” is the most impudent
piece I ever saw. It might have been written by W. T.
Stead in collaboration with Marie Corelli and General
Booth.
A farcical comedy, written by the late Robert Buchanan,
and produced in Australia, but not mentioned in a recent
Bulletin paragraph, was “ The Strange Adventures of
Miss Brown.” It followed on the lines of ‘Charleys
Aunt.” Frank Thornton played Miss Brown, with Bessie
Rignold, Noney Seabrooke, Shepherd, young Terry, and
others in the co., and ran the comedy for seven weeks at
Melb. Bijou before taking it to Sydney. Another of
Buchanan’s forgotten comedies was “That Doctor
Cupid,” a fantastic novelty, which failed as a Xmas show
at Melb. Princess’s in ’B9-’9O. Elton, Janet Achurch,
Edward Sass, Herbert Flemming, and Harwood played
principal parts, but they couldn’t make the thing gee.
Those were the days of expensive co.’s and keen competi-
tion, when the old firm of Williamson, Garner, and Mus-
grove ran two Melb. houses —the Royal as well as the
Princess’s —and sometimes made £250 a week profit at
each.
George Lauri’s recent “ Florodora ” gibe at the troubles
of Tyrone Power seemed not in the best of taste. When
the orchestra played out of tune in thePrapses entertain-
ment, Lauri walked to the footlights and said, “ Don’t
worrv ; you’ll get paid all right.” Surely the theatrical
dog does not eat the theatrical dog 1 But p’r’aps this
questionable joke was intended for a sneer at the action
of T.P.’s unionist musicianers. Actors have no spirit of
union anyhow. There are always plenty of mummers
willing to work for “ glory ” in the place of one who may
strike for his arrears of salary.
R. 11. Nichols, actor, is now in durance at Brisbane for
disobeying an order of the court forbidding the produc- ■
tion of “ What Became of Parker” (practically identical
with “What Happened to Jones”). And, according to
the Chief Justice, in limbo Nichols has got to stay until
he comes down with the incidental costs ; which, as he is
dead-broke, he cannot possibly do while prevented from
earning the wherewithal. The senselessness of the law s
attitude in the matter is on a par with the imbecile rule
under which a man who has been ordered to pay main-
tenance to his wife may, for not complying, be, by
imprisonment, effectually prevented from complying.

Possessing exceptional discernment and
tact amounting to Sherlock Holmes’ genius, J. W.
Holdsworth (101 Swanston-street, Melbourne),
heads his profession as a Private Inquiry Agent.
For investigating private matters, intricate next-
of-kin claims, and for tracing lost persons he is
unrivalled.*
A certain well-known Sydney sportsman,
whose scalp for a period of nearly 15 years was en-
tirely free from any sign of hair, and who has had
produced a fairly good growth through using
Capilla Hair Tonic, cannot pass down any street
in Sydney without being held up by persons who
have heard of the cure effected, and made to show
his head with the hair thereon.*
The day of ugly wall-papers is surely passed. The
exclusive attention given to this class of business by
Messrs. Thos. Whitelaw and Co., the magnificent re-
sources of their Home house, its attendant factories, the
auxiliary of some of the best artists, the regular employ-
ment of a large number of the ablest colorists of the hour,
enables Whitelaw’s to maintain their pre-eminence as the
most successful wall-paper people in the world. Their
names, long associated at Home with those great English
interests that have risen from “ the ashes of a Conser-
vative apathy,” now may be ranked with the greatest of
giant energies in successful commercial enterprise. The
rapid extension of their business and its establishment
in the leading capital cities of Australia, marks another
note of progress. The branch house at 391 George-street,
Sydney, is without doubt the most complete in its
appointments, as alike in its marvellous assortment of
stock comprising goods which range from the cheapest to
the best the World can produce. Whitelaw’s are offering
their handsome papers at London marked prices, and, be
it said, the values of their cheaper Art products are a
matter of pleasure and surprise to the buyer of moderate
means. Their private series comprises the richest decora-
tions that have ever been sent to Australia. The whole
business indeed is a splendid example showing the possi-
bilities of Art applied to interior wall treatment.*
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. Limited report Wheat.— An extremely dull week
has been experienced. All interested are anxiously
awaiting the arrival in bulk of the new crop, which in
nearly every district promises well, and it is pleasing to
note the Railway Commissioners are. making special
efforts.to fully meet the requirements of the approaching
season. The value of prime grain is still nominally from
2s. 10id. to 2s. lid. Ohickwheat to 2s 9Jd. Sydney,

The waste of time in W.A. Assembly,
through the wrangles which finally led to the fall
of the Leake Ministry, shelved several important
measures—among them the Early Closing Act.
Eor two years an E.C. Act was enforced with
excellent results, but that Act expired by
effluxion of time on 31st Oct. Mr. Leake pro-
posed in its place a Shops and Factories Act
similar to that in force in Queensland, because the
Crown Law Dept, of W.A. had made almost use-
less the expired E.C. Act by declaring that no
person was a shop-assistant unless engaged in the
actual sale of goods. Milliners, dressmakers,
saddlers, packers were all excluded from its bene-
fits. The B. hopes that the new Premier will
follow out Mr, Leake’s intentions in this matter.
The Alien Restriction Bill debate in the Senate
was marked , by speeches that should have made
those old Vic. Leg. Council seats scream out in
protest. Introducing the Bill, Senator O’Connor
observed :
The tie which bound Australia to the mother country
was merely nominal. It rested upon affection and com-
mon.interesfc, and the community and sympathy of race ;
and if, at any moment, any of these factors were to disap-
pear, there could be no doubt that Great Britain herself
would be as glad as Australia would be to let us go our
own way.
Then Senator Harney (W.A.), made another
shocking assertion that he would sooner have
Australia discourteous and offensive to the Home
Office than enact a legislative lie. Higgs rubbed
it in by quoting the much-belauded Natal Act,
and showing that under it Hindoos were practi-
tically slaves, and had not even the right to walk
on the footpaths. He accused the British Govt,
of being “ got at ” by influential people ; pointed
out that, though the Q. Sugar Works Bill was
vetoed, other Bills with exactly the same provi-
sions, but not opposed by such naiee persons,
were allowed to pass; and declared that the
British Govt, did not represent the voice of the
British people, millions of whom were not en-
franchised. “Send a contingent,” said S.A.
M‘Gregor, and the sprinkling of old-time Tories
looked in vain for a bolt from Heaven to strike
him dead. John Bull, if he takes the pains to
think over the matter at all, must be troubled
when he marks the rising of the tide of Australian
Nationalism in our first National Parliament.
♦—
It is now generally believed that “Joe”
Barling will not take his seat again on N.S.W.
Public Service Board. Deputy member Bridges,
well-advertised by the public-school children’s
displays, was sure of the vacancy, but lately his
health has given cause for anxiety.
5 Old Dr. Agnew, who died, at Hobart,
t’other day, saw the arrival and departure of no
fewer than 20 Tasmanian Governors—from Sir
John Franklin to Chief Justice Dodds. Had he
lived a day longer, his record would have stood at
21; the new Governor, Havelock, arrived just in
time to attend the old man’s funeral. He was
born in 1815, somewhere in the North of Ireland ;
arrived in Sydney in ’39, and went to Tasmania
m ’4O, at the invitation of Gov. Franklin, to
§hysic the convicts at the Cascades Penitentiary.
ixty years ago he became a member of the Royal
Society of Tasmania; and, a few years later, im-
ported the first salmon-ova to the island, at a per-
sonal cost of £9OO. Hobart Museum and Art Gal-
lery found him a liberal donor, and the kindly,
unassuming old Doctor will long be remem-
bered in the Isle of Dreams as an unsuccessful
politician and a successful philanthropist.


A QUICKSAND BATH.
Serious Consequences.
A GENERAL RESTORATION.
The Case of
Mrs. F. MARTIN.
(by a special reporter.)
If the inhabitants of Sydney were all
wealthy people, with nothing but the pro-
motion of their own happiness to think
about, would they be content to while away
their time in the many picturesque spots
surrounding the city, or would they go
“farther and fare worse?” People upon
whom fortune does not smile too lavishly
are philosophical in patronising the many
beautiful picnic resorts within easy reach of
their homes, and the experiences sometimes
resulting are worthy of a more romantic
period. A pleasure excursion to Botany
some time back had a sensational termina-
tion, for the details of which we are in-
debted to Mrs. Flora Martin, of No. 11
Suttor-street, Alexandria. “It was a small
family party,” explained Mrs. Martin to the
writer, “ composed of my husband, a married
couple, some children, and myself. We
were fond of taking a trip to Botany, where
we used to sit admiring the noble expanse of
water whilst the young ones played on the
sandy beach. On one particular day my
sister-in-law and I were strolling leisurely
along with the youngsters, where the tide
had a few minutes previously run out, when
I stepped on to a treacherous bed of quick-
sand and suddenly began to sink. Before
my husband could reach me I had
almost disappeared from view, my head
being the only part visible, It was a lucky
thing that my screams were heard, otherwise
I would have been smothered. My husband
ran to my assistance, and with considerable
difficulty extricated me in a fainting con-
dition from the jaws of death. My baby
was then only three months old, and I was
not ve’-y strong. The shock my system
sustained had a disastrous effect upon my
enfeeoled condition for months afterwards.
“ For nearly three weeks I did not stir
out of my bed. My doctor ordered me to
refrain from any exertion, which was
scarcely necessary, seeing that I was in such
a condition of helplessness. I was so weak
and nervous that the slightest start brought
on a shivering fit, besides making me give
spasmodic jumps. The first two weeks in
bed were quite sleepless. I could not drive
from my brain the thoughts of the narrow
escape I had. Lying awake night after
night my mind wandered back to that day
of horror, and the most exaggerated
imaginations followed each other in quick
succession until they made me tremble con-
vulsively. I could take no solid food of any
description, and the milk diet upon which I
relied to keep me alive failed to agree with
me. After the light things which I slowly
took in small quantities I was immediately
seized with pain in the stomach, and in the
left side. There was a choking feeling in
the throat, and the stifling sensation re-
sulting filled me with dismay. I was afraid
my breathing was going to cease altogether.
I believe this distressing symptom arose
from the leaden feeling of oppression on my
chest. At any rate, the sufferings entailed
by forcing down any nourishment were so
unbearable that I did not eat a quarter as
much as my body, required. Consequently
I lost all my flesh. There was not a streak
of colour left in my cheeks, and I was so
irritable and nervous that I did not care to
see anyone, and sometimes could not bear
the sound of my child’s voice. Being so
restless in bed, and not getting any better, I
decided to try and get about a little.”
| « Did the change do any good ? ”
«* No. I got worse and worse as I greM
older. I suffered from bad headaches, ana
I was very often sick. Boring pains over
the eyes were very troublesome, and fits 01
dizziness made me reel and almost fall to
the ground. Every morning, on rising
from bed, a dreadful fit of nausea, accom-
panied by violent vomiting, attacked me
with unerring regularity. A bitter fluid
arose from my stomach, and my throat felt
parched. Liberal draughts of water during
the day did not ease my feverish thirst,
which, I think, was induced through a con-
tinual feeling of faintness. My weakness
prevented me looking after the house woik,
and the inability to exercise my limbs pro-
duced great weariness. Constipation was
another ailment, my tongue being unclean
and unwholesome. Just below my ribs the
flesh became very tender, and behind my
left shoulder I felt sharp, darting pains. A
sensation of chilliness in my feet and limbs
was often followed by flushings of my face,
and then a dry, hacking cough came on.”
“ You had a perfect complication of ail-
ments.”
“ I think I had all the ilk imaginable
rolled into one severe attack, and it was
only natural that I became low-spirited and
despondent, besides being discontented
with everything and everybody. I was
always in dread of trouble arising, and had
a melancholy desire to be alone. Even
talking fatigued me ; reading was out of the
question, as my eyes were too dull and
heavy. From downright exhaustion I
occasionally fell into a short doze, and then I
had dreams of a frightful character, which
made me dread falling asleep.”
“ What did your doctor say ? ”
“He said I was suffering from excessive
nervousness, and that my digestive organs
were greatly impaired. He gave me a lot of
physic, which did no good. My nerves
were still shattered, and my frame was
reeking with pains. My husband again came
to my rescue. I was more dead than alive
when he brought home a bottle of Clements
Tonic. Until then I had not found a remedy
to relieve my troubles. Clements Tonic
gave relief immediately. It gave tone to
my nerves, enabled me to eat and sleep,
and destroyed all my aches and pains. My
health was soon perfected by Clements
Tonic, and that is why I desire my cure to
be made public, which you can publish in
any way you like.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I Flora Martin, of No. 11 Suttor-street, Alex-
andria, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have oare-
fu ly lead the annexed d cument, consisting of three
folios and consecutively numbered from one to three,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cuie by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full peimission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
ing any payment; and I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true and by
virtue of .he provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majerty,
intituled “ An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South
Males, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof,
and for the suppression of voluntary and Extra-
judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”
Declared at Alexandria this 28th day of September,

The Ways of Many Waters
By E. J. BEADY.


Tlie cruel system which compels married
and unmarried females to attend at police-stations
to draw their maintenance-money ought to be
altered Frequently a young girl who has had an
accidenta! baby by a mean father has to ask for
the allowance, fixed by the Court, in front of a
dozen or so of policemen, as well as civilians
having buuness at the station. More privacy
would .be secured if the C.P.S. had the handling
ot the m mey. The average woman—wife or
mistress-who has had to apply in court for the
maintenance of her child is deserving of all the
jiitiful consideration the community can give to
Prisoners sentenced at Broken Hill and
ordered to be confined in some Sydney-side gaol
are still taken across country—3oo miles or so
mostly desert—by coach. Nobody else ever goes
that way ; it is much cheaper, and more comfort-
able and safer and speedier, to go by train through
o.A. and \ letoria. A N.S. W. policeman cannot,
however, take a N.S. W. prisoner through other
States ; and there is, as yet, no Federal police-
man with authority to hang on to his man any-
where in Australia.
To sentence a man to 12 months* gaol in
N.S.W. means to fine the State, roughly speak-
ing, £5O ; and that is exclusive of cost of police,
courts, and erection and repair of gaols. At Dar-
linghurst, still the most populous prison, the cost
of keeping a prisoner is a trifle less—just under
£45 last year; and at Biloela, where Port Jackson
sharks (unpaid) largely take the place of warders
it is done for £27 lfis. But in the country it gets
to as much as £l7l, the Deniliquin figure ; and
at even such a large and accessible gaol as Trial
Bay the cost averages about £7O, of which £4O
goes in salaries and wages.

The Wooing of XJongasa.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
He Was a very great chief of the Royal Line;
therefore, it behoved him to keep up his dignity.
It took him all his time ; he usually kept it up
on a mat, seated cross-legged, with the national
garment twisted round his limbs and a white mess
jacket on his body. On state occasions the jacket
had appropriate continuations —military and gold-
braided, for the wearer knew what was due to
civilisation and to his dignity. His other occu-
pations were smoking strong native suluis, pre-
tending to plait sennit, uttering sententious
maxims of wisdom at some fono, and accepting
the ministrations of a bevy of plump and semi-
nude hebes.
Over these presided Taema, the Chieftainess,
hustling, bustling, with a two-edged tongue, but a
kindly soul withal. Her scoldings finished, she
Would squat down merrily to a mistress-and-maid
meal, on the share-and-share-alike principle—with
the biggest share for the hand-maidens. Having
married into the Royal Line, she also was a great
personage, in proof whereof she never appeared in
full undress, but habitually flaunted a European
chemise over her lava-lava. There was nothing
small about Taema ; her figure and her house-
keeping were on a generous scale. She recalled
an active French mdnagere, done very brown and
remarkably sloppy about the waist.
The principal house—there were several—was
on a scale commensurate with its master’s dignity,
the roomiest and most circular bread-fruit house for
miles round. But what set it apart as a house on
the Royal Line was its air of foreign luxury, the
number of its camphor chests, the framed photo-
graphic portraits hung up inside the dome, the
large bronze bust on tne floor against the forked
central bread-fruit pillar, the toilet-table with a
couple of looking-glasses elaborately draped in
antimacassars. The crowning effect of civilisa-
tion was seen at night, when, in the seclusion of
a mosquito-curtained sanctum adorned with
turkey-red and scarlet-braid streamers, Uongasa
rested his august bald head, not on a common or
farden bamboo bolster, like Taema snoring beside
im, but on genuine papalangi pillows, beauteous
with starched frills.
Taema was fat, frolicsome and forty. Now,
forty is a great age for a partner of a beau of
sixty. So on weekdays Uongasa sat on his mat,
smoking suluis, watching Taema, and pondering
many things. On Sunday, adorned with the
braided continuations and a button-hole, he went
to church with Taema, when he heard about
David and Solomon and other much-married
worthies, and pondered still more. One day it
dawned on him that the village required more
fine mats.
The village was quite of his opinion; it held
fonos —otherwise committee meetings limited to
one sex—and plaited 3ennit, and discussed the
question. For new fine mats meant a wedding
and a Taupo with a big dowry. One day Uongasa
spoke to Taema with tears in his leering old eyes.
“ My beloved,” he said, “ it grieves me to the
heart this that I am bound to say, but you know
what the village is—how greedy, how selfish.”
Yes, Taema knew.
“ And how a chief, a royal-line chief, has to
sacrifice himself and be sacrificed.”
Taema knew.
“They clamor for new mats, my noble one.”
Taema knew. Apparently, what she didn’t
know wasn’t worth knowing. She came to the
point. “They have selected the Taupo?”
“Ah, well—-yes—l believe —that is to say, with
your wish and approval, they talk of the Taupo of
Laloiloilevaoloi. Her mats, alas ! are many. ”
“And she is young and fresh, and a Catholic as
I was,’’quoth Taema, with a cheerful nod and a
side-glance which puzzled her lord. “ Well, when
do you wish the courting to begin ? ”
“My very noble chieftainess ! It is but a form.
The girl will come for a time, to secure the mats
to the village ; then she will return to her home
and thou to my heart, as before. ”
Again that satisfied nod and quizzical look.
“I shall go to my people directly the church
feast is over,” said the discarded one, placidly ;
“ meanwhile I will make a few more pillow-slips.
Young Taupos don’t care to be sewing and starch-
ing and ironing.”
Uongasa heard her with a pang. He had his
own misgivings as to that Taupo’s sentiment
towards pillow-frills, and other comforting
matters. Moreover, Taema was so very heartless,
took it all as a matter of course, without a touch
of sentiment. It was, on the whole, rather trying.
Perhaps Taema had her own bad quarter-of-an-
hour as she squatted before her sewing-machine,
her ironing, or her siapo board ; dark lines came
under her eyes, and the lids drooped heavily, but
her mouth had a proud curve, and she made no
sign. The church feast passed off, she gave a
grand dinner, and beamed benevolently as she
flew round waiting upon her guests, and made
her little jokes, and held her head bravely. On
the morrow, when she left, it really looked as if
she were discarding Uongasa. That was exactly
how he felt : an ill-used, cast-off spouse, as he sat
in his grand house on the two following days,
with the rain falling. The semi-nudes who re-
mained forgot all about the morning tea, to which
Taema had accustomed him, and couldn’t find the
materials when remonstrated with. They forgot to
pull down the mat-venetians so as to keep the
draughts off his chest. They left the mosquito-
curtains down all day. And they stuffed him
with the remains of the feast—cold, fat pork—of
which he ate a great deal too much, and dreamt
that six fat Taupos dragged his pillow from under
him and then sat upon his chest.
* * • *
Bun, on the third day, the Royal Line reasserted
itself. The villagers stored the malanga boat
with roast pigs innumerable, and Uongasa started
off, flags flying, awnings tricked out in blue and
red, thirty rowers, all garlanded with green,
bending to their oars as they chanted a sea-song,
and a fine young man on the prow, brandishing a
club and cutting capers for the benefit of be-
holders.
The pigs were excellent etrting, the Taupo was
pleasant to the eye, yet something seemed lack-
ing. The village virgin made light of the num-
ber and quality of the porkers; she eyed her
royal suitor coldly ; she danced carelessly, and
she bestowed kindly glances on a handsome young
chief who scowled at the Royal Line. Evidently
there was going to be trouble. But a Samoan
wooing, when a state affair, is slow and patient,
involving many feasts and offerings. Uongasa
came home with less flourish of trumpets, but
with confidence unabated. Many times that
heavily-laden malanga-boat might be seen going
down the coast, returning each time with a slower
swish of the oars and a quieter chant. At
last the village chiefs remonstrated: they
had contributed more pigs than in rea-
son; it was quite time to see the color
of those mats. Then the truth came out. The
virgin’s people were very sorry to lay any obstacles
on the Royal Line, but theirs was a quite ex-
emplary and high-toned virgin, belonging, more-
over, to the Catholic faith ; it was out of the
question to allow her to be married without a
proper legal ceremony. And Uongasa had no
divorce paper.
Was that all ? Uongasa would at once see Ins
noble-minded chieftainess and get her consent to
the divorce ; it was but a form. And with much
show of confidence the malanga boat proceeded to
the adjoining island, where Taenia had retired
into family life. But in his corner of the boat the
married wooer drew a scarlet turban down over
frowning brows, for he had misgivings about the
noble-hearted one.
Taema’s views Were distinct : she had been
brought up a good Catholic. Though she did
alter her creed to marry Uongasa, she had now
returned to the true faith, and had no notion of
risking her soul. He might go to heaven as soon
as he pleased ; indeed, it was time for him to be
thinking of the journey, but so long as he re-
mained on earth he must abide by the law of the
church.
“ But the Taupo’s honor,” remonstrated
Uongasa.
“The Taupo is young,” answered Taema, as if
youth were a crime to be atoned for; “she has
time for repentance, you have not. But if I
agreed it would be useless ; my brothers refuse
their consent, and without it the divorce cannot
be obtained.”
This proved true. What was to be done ?
Uongasa had an inspiration. Samoan pigs were
Elayed out, but papalangi delicacies remained.
loaded with 500 loaves of bread, three casks of
salt beef, two sacks of rice, that boat sped
along once more, and the drum played, and the
trumpet brayed, and pistol shots were let off as
the rowers leapt to shore. Alas, for the wooer !
No strapping Taupo was there to dance the siva
for him ; she was off to the hills with her father
and mother . . . and a handsome young chief
as protector.
Uongasa had no hair to tear, but he wrung his
hands and trampled on his dignity. “Her
honor ! ” he cried ; “ the honor of my future chief-
tainess ! ”
“Be still,” answered the other chiefs, “ and
show us your divorce-paper. Do you suppose her
honor is not as dear to us as to herself ? ”
It was probably dearer. A Taupo with damaged
reputation falls, not in caste, but in market
value ; her people are mulcted in so many extra-
fine mats.
When the chiefs saw that there was no divorce-
paper they shook their heads; but when they
noted the white bread, the red beef, and the rice,
they nodded approvingly. “We will re-consider
that question of marriage,” they said ; “ we have
eaten of your food, you deserve our mats. We
shall not mind what the missionaries say. As our
forefathers married —faa Samoa, so shall you. ”
And that day, as the malanga boat returned
towards the great house, its rowers sang the song
of the accepted bridegroom.
• * » *
But the bride tarried and made no sign. And
when Uongasa, weary of waiting, went down for
the last time with full wedding paraphernalia, he
found the central figure of the wedding again
absent. She had crossed him, going by land as
he came by water, and she was now ensconced with
some relatives of her mother’s a few miles from
his own home. It was rumoured that she uttered
slighting remarks concerning the Royal Line,
declaring that nothing should induce her to start
a matrimonial journey on it with a bald-headed,
wheezing companion.
And Uongasa, urged on by his wrathful village
chiefs, was equally determined. He is sitting on
his dignity at Laloiloilevaoloi, asserting that he
will not arise till he possesses a suitable number of
fine bridal mats. The bride may be lacking, but
her mats, in payment of all those pigs and
papalangi delicacies, he must have. And Taema,
in her own old home, sits and smiles proudly.
She put very inferior material into those last frills,
and can calculate to a nicety the time when she
will be recalled to resume her old sway with in-
creased vigor.
And the handsome young chief is calculating
the cost of stealing a Taupo by force. It is
an expensive indulgence, but he thinks he sees his
way to it.
So does the Taupo.
SAVAII, NAVIGATOR ISLES.
ROSE DE BOHEME.
At Day-Dawn (Murchison, W.A.), there is
a black alderman (American negro).
Why do not the legal-code consolidators of
other States conform with Victorian custom and
annotate Acts with the gist of important cases
decided in various sections? Such notes would
shed a brilliant, unofficial light on the bewildering
object-lessons of word-jugglery affected by the
average Australian draughtsman.
Judge G. B. Simpson is quite right in say-
ing that police magistrates, in cases of assault with
violence, should, instead of fining the accused,
send them up for trial. As things stand, a mem-
ber, or members, of a push having a grudge against
somebody, just pay the reckoning well and fully;
if caught, they are taken before the P.M., and (in
most cases), fined from 10s. to £2, which, to a push
of 30 or 40 or thereabouts, means from 3d. to Is.
apiece. Their troubles !
At Geelong (Vic.) police-court, the other
week, a middle-aged man named Phillips was
sentenced to a month for indecent exposure. The
man admitted to having got 10 years and a flog-
ging for a similar act of sex-madness, so his next
conviction may be expected to come along shortly.
As a treatment for Phillips’s insanity the lash
must be considered a failure.
“ W.S.T.” : To the question—“ What
makes an officer popular with his men ? ” a
returned Australian swaddy answered—“ Sticking
to them in the firing-line, and seeing that they
get their grub. One of our lieutenants, who is
now a major, was the idol of his company because
he never wasted a man, was always up amongst
them, and, if the dog-biscuit and coffee weren’t on
hand when business was over, somebody had to
lookout. Now,” went on the warrior, “another
Australian officer, who went over with much
trumpet, faded into insignificance after his first
engagement. On the way over he was very fond
of lecturing his men, and on one occasion plain-
tively remarked that, if he were wounded in
battle, he must be carried forward that his
example should encourage the rank - and - file.
Well, the first time the Mauser bullets began to
whistle about his ears, it apparently occurred to
him that he could direct the force with more
advantage from a considerable distance in the
rear. And he never ventured to ‘ flute ’ to the men
any more.”

PURVEYORS BY SPECIAL APPOINTMENT TO HIS MAJESTY THE KING.
The Wanton*
[For The Bulletin.]
Thou pallid, cold and glistening moon j
The seas are proof against thy charms no mtire:
Earth cannot hold them ; unseen arms
Thou stretchest forth, and shepherdest thy loveS:
The rough Atlantic whelms the Western world,
The calm Pacific rages thro’ the East,
Earth’s ancient might and learning swoon and die.
The waters gather, heaped up, turbulent,
The breast of Earth is mud, and slime, and death,
While from the peak of Himalaya leap
All her sweet waters.
One huge heaving globe
Sails flashing thro’ the air, intense with life
To thy cold bosom—thee it meets, and fills
Thy hollows and thy valleys and thy deeps,
Thou sparkling, brightest moon ;
Yet most cold moon.
And what of this ?—what tho’ the earth is left
To dry, and rot, and crumble into dust ?——
My heart's vitality has quivered out,
To grace an irresponsive woman's soul.
The toil of years, the sweat of years of toil,
The joys of life, its honor, and its pride,
All in one mad desire have merged,
Have left me barren to the laugh of Time,
While she has all, and gives me not again.
Ah, God ! the passion of a life, the hope,
The peace and all have fused,
Gone out to her in one confession ; swathed
Her cold life in a beauty never dreamed —
And left me naked, shivering and ghast,
Single in desolation.
M.L.
AHAU.
A Bush Naturalist.
[For The Bulletin.]
“ I travelled a bit one time with one o’ them
bush natur’lists,” said Tarkalson, “ an’ he beat all
the rummy coves I ever shouldered swags with.
Overhauled him one mornin’ near Condobolin.
He’d left his swag an’ billy on the road, an’ was
away off proddin’ up a frog with a stick to make
it jump. When it ’ud do a pretty good leap, he’d
out with a two-foot rule an’ measure it.
“ ‘ Discovered a frog on Cockle Creek,’ ” he said,
‘ that jumped 14 foot. B’lieve it’s a record, but
I want the frogs out-back t’ave a go at it ’fore I
give Cockle Creek the belt.’
“Wal, I thinks to meself, he’s an int’restin’
sort of a joker, anyway, an’ I pal’d in with him.
I got pretty tired of him, though, after a week.
He was nat’ral hist’ry mad, an’ always wantin’ me
to help him get specimens.
“ When yer dead tired, an’ yer ploddin’ along
ter get to camp, yer don’t like chuckin’ yer swag
off all of a sudden to run down a frill-neck ; an’
yer feel like swearin’, too, when yer stopped
ev’ry ten minutes t’ admire grass’oppers an’ such
like. At that rate o’ goin’ w’d ’ave got out-back
sometime in our old age.
“At first, when I barked me shins, or come
near knockin’ me eye out, I looked on it like he
did, as little things ter laugh at. Sort o’ give
the game more int’rest yer know. But when I
ripped the leg o’ me Sunday pants, our views
wasn’t exactly sim’lar. I swore I wouldn’t be
worried an’ blown an’ busted in the int’rests o’
Science any more. I didn’t reckon ter get much
credit if I helped it along ever so much. Some
big bug, who never tore his pants at the game,
would get whatever credit was goin’, an’ old Bill
Tarkalson wouldn’t be mentioned.
“Still I stuck to Mr. Richard De Quinlan, the
natur’list, though from that out he ’ad ter
natur’lise on his own. He asked me ter call
him Professor De Quinlan before strangers.
’Twould make us look more important.
“Anyhow, we were makin’ for the Barwon, an’
there was any gors-quantity o’ grubs an’ things to
keep him int’rested. We wasn’t together much
except in camp. He was mos’ly chasin’ something
or squintin’ at a new inseck through a telescope.
Lend’s yer knife a minute.
“ I useter tap the stations for rations while he
was huntin’ for ant-lions, or tryin’ which grubs
kicked the most on a ant-bed. Scoopin’ in butter-
flies with his hat on a stick, an’ lassooin’ spiders
with thread, were things he gloried in. If he only
sat down for a bit of a smoke he’d ’av a couple o’
caterpillars fightin’ in front of him, or he’d be
deliverin’ lectures on a blowfly. Yer must’ve ’ad
this knife a long time. It’s purty blunt.
“ I gen’elly’ad the fire lit, an’ a heap o’ wood
ready for night, before De Quinlan showed up.
Sometimes his billy ’d be swarmin’ with bore-
sinkers an’ wood-riddlers. He’d ’ave ter crucify
’em all with puns ’fore he could make his tea, so r
useter offer him some o’ mine. De Quinlan said
he never liked t’ impose on anybody’s good nature ;
but he always drank my tea, an’ wired into my
damper, for all that.
“Now an’ ag’in he’d hoist a dead snake or two
out o’ that billy, an’put’em in bottles. Other
times it’d be two or three kinds o’ lizards. He’d
tether them be the legs, an’ watch ’em half the
night to learn their ’abits.
“When he’d be lecturin’ on triantiwontigons
an’ them crimby-jimby-something-gigantus (I was
never much of a French scholar) I’d feel’s if l
wanted to put me jaw in a sling. An’ he’d want
me to ’andle his snakes to prove they wur cold-
blooded, or to sit at the fire an’ count the legs of a
santipee.
“ His swag was a reg’lar trav’lin’ menagerie.
When he unrolled it yer’cl think it was a stink
fact’ry. Beetles in all conditions yer could
mention ; an’ heaps o’ skins, half cured, or not
cured at all. Useter strike me that De Quinlan
was rather too thorough, ’sif he wanted ter see
how many v’rieties o’ smell a skin could shed ’fore
it rotted away. How his int’rest in nat’ral hist’ry
kep’ up in spite o’ the stink o’ them specimens
useter knock me bandy.
“He kep’ a note-book, too, an’ yer’d see him
ev’ry spare minute jottin’ down’ things ’bout
bandicoots an’ other insecks. Couldn’t make ’ead
or tail of his scrawl meself, an’ if he didn’t say ’em
over purty often ter keep famelyer with the
writin’, I be hanged if he could make it out
either.
“Wal, it was rainin’ when we got ter th’
Barwon, an’ two days later on we wur flood-bound
on a bit of an island. De Quinlan didn’t mind if
we wur stuck there f’r ever, providin’ plenty 0’
live things come ashore. In less’n a Week lie ’ad
the grass wore off all round that there island doin’
sent’nel dooty.
“One o’ the first things he pounced on was a
turtle. An’ wasn’t he proud of it ! ’Struth,
yer’d’ve thought he’d got a son. He tethered it
bn a patch o’ sand, an’ called oil it ’bout 40 times
a day. Called it Friday. Said it was a fine
female specimen, an’, it bein’ the layin’ Season, he
wanted ter see how many eggs th’ adult turtle
laid per diem. He always said per diem.
“ Anyhow, a blackfeller swum over to us one
day, an’ he looked at that turtle. De Quinlan
explained why he ’ad him tied up. The black-
feller looks at the turtle ag’in—an’ then he
laughed. ‘Baal dat one lay um eger,’ he said—
‘ dat feller ole man ! ’
“ The weather kep’ on show’ry most o’ the time,
an’ as we’d only one tent —which b’longed ter
Quinlan—l ’ad ter camp ’longside of him. In the
daytime I useter try to get int’rested in his ex-
periments. Sort o’ put the time in. He wanted
ter breed a cross ’tween a worm an’ a caterpillar.
He tried with sev’ral sorts —-till the bloomin’ tent
was crawlin’ alive with ’em. They’d fight an’
buck an’ wriggle like billy-o, but wouldn’t ’filiate
worth a cuss. So I tried t’ explain as how grubs
didn’t breed that way ; but he got narked.
ll Then he ’ad two spiders hung at the tent door
—wich made it awkward ter gerrin in an’ out
—an’ he’d fish for ’em with a fly on a bit o’ cotton.
If they didn’t bite well, he’d swing ’em together
an’ lerrem knock slops out o’ one another. Reach
us that fire-stick over.
“ The mos’ shuddersome exhibition he give me
was a fight ’tween a t’ran’la an’ a santipee. De
Quinlan thought it was great sport, an’ after that
he was always huntin’ for t’rantlas an’ santipees.
I took ter huntin’ too—to kill all I could find. I
tried hard ter get at a brute of a scorpion he ’ad.
Lived in dread o’ the thing gettin’ away.
“ He ’ad his side o’ the tent dotted with speci-
mens. He’d run out o’ card-board, an’ took to
pinnin”em outer th’ tent.
“There was rows and rows of ’em: Tiger-
beetles, Pie-dishes, Nutcrackers, Dung-beetles,
Whirligigs, Cow-bu’sters—in fact, I dunno what
he didn’t ’ave in that line.
“I didn’t mind ’em so much in the daytime,
but at night it was awful. De Quinlan ’ad all me
match-boxes emptied ter put beetles in, an’ the
seratchin’ o’ them blessed things at night was
enough ter shatter the nerves of a shingle-back.
Then there was the buzzin’ an’ whirr-r-in’ of
pinned things that hadn’t died yet; the stink o’
them as had ; an’ —yer know them 'orny-legged
mantis things '! —wal, they’d be kickin’ an’ jumpin’
in jam-tins fit ter drive yer mad. The caterpillars
that’d broke loose would come crawlin’ over yer—
I tell yer it useter give me about 390 nightmares a
week.
" There was one little black beetle that was
always gettin’ on its back, an’ then it’d crack its
head like blazes on the bottom o’ the box till it
righted itself. Then it wouldn’t be satisfied. It
’ud jerk it’s neck about till it got topsy-turvy
again. Then more cracks ! I’ve cursed that there
thing, an* Professor Quinlan and his menagerie
half the night. His troubles ! He was in his
glory. The flood was fetchin’ whips o’ live stock
to his island, an’ he hoped the flood ’ud keep up
for a month. Never see such a pipe’s this for
gettin stuffed up. Phe-e-ew !
“ Wal, anyhow, it was the stingin’ grubs that
broke up our ’appy home. He’d two sorts of ’em,
One’s a black-an’-yaller horror found in clusters.
When you go near ’em they chuck up their tails,
an’ the look o’ th? upended cluster would make a
cove shudder his back-teeth loose.
“ T’other sort’s a hatter—mos’ly seen on wattle
trees. It’s four times bigger, an’ not bad lookin’;
but the least touch of it’ll rise a white blister big’s
a sixpence. ’Twas this thing that gorra stray one
night, an’ by the holy smoke, he gives me pertic’ler
fits.
“ I got up an’ put me boots on, an’ I jumped on
that crawlin’ iuseck till there wasn’t no inseck ter
see. Then I flung th ■ lamp at De Quinlan’s pet
lizard, an’ kicked his best bottle o’ frogs into the
river.
“ Poor De Quinlan nearly took a fit. The loss
o’ them frogs was a nash’nel calamity ; an’ he
feared the lizard would lose the sight o’ one eye.
I felt sorry for the lizard till De Quinlan started
ter tongue-wang me. Then I copped him by the
neck an’ hurled him through the beetle depart-
ment.
“I rolled up there an’ then, an’ swum that
billabong at midnight ; an’ the larst I saw of
Professor De Quinlan was him in his shirt an’ clod-
hoppers, chasin’ an escaped beetle with a slush-
lamp.
N.s.w.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
A Kiss.
[For The Bulletin.]
They were both young. She was 24, he 26. They
had met and admired each other in a strictly
Platonic fashion for some considerable time, and
hail now arrived at that state of evolution which is
not Platonism. But neither would admit it. He
had asked for a kiss in a voice which trembled
with —Platon ism.
“ But, you know, the Platonics do not allow of
that. You are departing from your creed.”
■‘ Purely a—er —passionless, brother one,” he
urged.
“But what an absurd request! You are neither
in love, nor are you sentimental—and —and—l am
not. And really it is very silly. Cannot we do
as you. suggested—admire each other in a coldly
critical fashion ? Was it not you who said, ‘ Fall
in love and all your admiration for a woman
vanishes after a few weeks of vapid, love-sick in-
tercourse and inane sentimentality ? ”
He was silent. “You know,” he argued, “it
is only an experiment—just to see, in fact,” he
added, daringly, “ whether it is Platonism.”
“ I’m sure it is on my part, at least. Ido not
wish to verify it in that way. You have heard,
perhaps, that I have a heart of similar durability
and hardness to these diamonds,” which were
glinting on a palpitating breast.
“Suppose,” said he, “ suppose they were pa do.”
“Supposing they were?” she replied, com-
posedly.
“They would be” —he hesitated—“very
soft .”
“ Perhaps, when one pierced the artificial crust,”
she finished, demurely.
“ But, dear, what is this ‘ artificial crust ’ ? ”
It is . . . shall we call it Platonism ? ”
He gazed at the exasperatingly averted head.
“ May I ? ” he asked, tremulously.
She blushed—“and pi . . . Oh, d !”
“Thanks, dear,” he ended.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
gratefui patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of. scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.

of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and unparalleled
